SECTION 4 Valid Trend Line Use

¢ A widely held belief has it that the longer and stronger a trend, the more
likely it is to continue. Cyclic concepts indicate this is not entirely true.
Strong trends tend to continue long, but the longer a trend has been in
existence the more likely it is to be reversed.

* In conventional charting techniques, a trend line break by prices sig-
nifies additional price movement in the direction of the break. This is
quite often true but can be disastrously wrong. Many different trends
may be in effect at the same time, and the one trend reversal indicated
by a trend line break can be easily overpowered by other trends.

A valid trend line break by prices is used to indicate a trough or a crest
of the next longer wave than the wave on which the valid trend line is
based.

* Refer back to figure 2-3. The composite wave has only been formed
in this figure over about one-half of the wave components shown. The
valid downtrend line of this figure has not been broken by the
composite wave.

* Now turn to figure 2-9. Here, the same component waves and compos-
ite wave are shown, but with the summation performed over both the
solid and dashed portions of the wave components.

* The same valid downtrend line is also shown, and it is seen that it was
subsequently broken by the composite wave going from below the valid
trend line to above it.

‘* The meaning of this break is inferred as follows:

1. The wave on which the valid trend line is based is wave B.

2. The wave next longer than wave B is wave A.

3. The upward break of the valid downtrend line based on wave B
implies a strong probability that the next longer wave A has
formed a trough in the recent past.

* Reference to waves A and B will confirm that this conclusion is
indeed correct.

Page 18


Page 19

CycliTee Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 4 Valid Trend Line Use (Cont'd)

© Notice too the danger of the more usual inference from such a break.
In this case this inference would have been that additional price move-
ment would take place in the direction of the break, or upward. A small
amount of additional upward movement did follow the break, but not
much,

© In dealing with the complex patterns of actual price motion, it is neces-
sary to clarify what is meant by a trend line break. Any price penetra-
tion of the trend line, no matter how brief, is considered to be a trend
line break.

Thus, if the price bar for a given day dips down through an uptrend
line by only a small fraction of a dollar and the price bars for subse-
quent days pull back above the trend line and do not penetrate it
again, the trend line is nevertheless said to have been broken.

When using a valid trend line break to identify a wave trough or crest,
proceed in the following way.

For a downtrend line break:
a. Look back from the break into past time by an amount equal to
the period of the wave next longer than the wave on which the
trend line is based.

b. Find the lowest price low within this span of time.

c. Consider that in all probability a trough of this next longer wave
is associated with this price low.

For an uptrend line break:
a. Look back from the break into past time by an amount equal to
the period of the wave next longer than the wave on which the

trend line is based.

b. Find the highest price high in this span of time.

c. Consider that in all probability a crest of this next longer wave
is associated with this price high.

SECTION 5 FLD’s — The Price Move Predictors
© FLD is short for Euture Line of Demarcation.
© There is an FLD associated with each wave of a cyclic model.

Construction of an FLD consists essentially of shifting past prices into
the future by a calculated time span called the FLD number.

The first step in the construction of an FLD is to compute this FLD
number.

The FLD number is derived as follows:
1. Divide the period of the wave on which the FLD is based by 2.0
2. Add 1.0 to this
3. Round the result to the nearest whole number.

© Example determination of an FLD number for a wave with a period of
9.7 days:

1. Divide wave period by 2.0: 9.7 + 2.0 = 4.85
2. Add 1.0: 4.85 + 1.0 = 5.85

3. Round to the nearest whole number: 5.85 becomes 6.0

4. Therefore, the FLD number corresponding to a wave with a
9.7-day period is 6.0 days.

Example determinaton of an FLD number for a wave with a period of
16.2 weeks:

1. Divide wave period by 2.0: 16.2 + 2.0 = 8.1
2, Add 1.0: 8.1 + 1.0 = 91

3. Round to the nearest whole number: 9.1 becomes 9.0

4. Therefore, the FLD number corresponding to a wave with a 16.2-
week period is 9.0 weeks.


Page 26

CyellTee Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 5 FLD’s — The Price Move Predictors (Cont'd)

Once the FLD number has been derived, the corresponding FLD is
drawn on the price-bar chart as follows (please refer to figure 2-12 as
you proceed):

1. Note the most recent price-bar on the chart (normally the last
price datum available and plotted at the right of the chart).

2. Counting this last price-bar as one, continue to count price
bars to the left, one time unit at a time, until the FLD number
(which is 6 for the example of figure 2-12) is reached.

3. Note the average price for the price-bar reached at the end of the
count. (Note: average price can be obtained graphically as the
point half way between the high price and the low price on a price
bar, or it can be computed as the sum of the high and low prices
of the bar divided by two).

4, Plot the value of this average price in the first future time position
on the chart (one time unit to the right of the most recent price
bar plotted).

5. Now note the average price of the price bar immediately to the
right of the one found by counting back the FLD number. Plot
this average price in the second future time position (two time
units to the right of the most recent price-bar plotted).

6. Continue in this manner until you run out of price bars or
run out of space on the chart.

7. Connect the plotted future-time average price values by short
straight lines. The result is the future line of demarcation for the
wave from whose period the FLD number was derived

Note: If the FLD-number count should end on a time space when the
markets were not open (daily charts only), connect the average prices
of the two price bars on either side of the time gap by a
straight line. Use the price value of this straight line where it inter-
sects the day of the needed average price.

« Figure 2-12 and the above explanatory material should be studied until

model and check your results against figure 2-14.

© The FLD is an extremely powerful tool of cyclic analysis, and it can be
used in a wide variety of ways. The most important of these uses is the
estimation, or prediction, of the objective for a potential change in
price — before a price reversal has even started! That is why this first
introduction to the FLD concept has been entitled FLD’s — The Price
Move Predictors.

SECTION 6 How FLD’s Are Related to Waves

 FLD’s work because of the actuality of the basic cyclic concepts and
principles in equity price movement.

 

 

* Applying the FLD process to a single, ideal wave results in a second
wave that is identical to the first except that it is phase-shifted forward
in time by essentially one-half of the wave period.

© As indicated in figure 2-15, two such waves, identical except for a phase
difference of one-half of the wave period, exhibit three useful character-
istics:

ling Without Permission Str

1. When the FLD of the wave is forming a trough in the future,
that is where the price wave will form a crest when that future

 

é time has arrived.
Similarly, when the FLD of the wave is forming a crest in the fu-
ture, that is where the price wave will form a trough when that
@ future time has arrived.

2. When the FLD of the wave is moving down in the future, that is
where the price wave will be moving up when that future time
has arrived.

Similarly, when the FLD of the wave is moving up in the future,
that is where the price wave will be moving down when that future
time has arrived.

°

When the price wave moves up through its FLD, the price wave
has formed a trough one-fourth of the wave period in the past, and
the price wave is one-half as far up as it is going to go for that
cycle.

Similarly, when the price wave moves down through its FLD, the
price wave has formed a crest one-fourth of the wave period in the
past, and the price wave is one-half as far down as it is going to
go for that cycle.

‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

« It is this last characteristic that provides FLO’s with the potential for
price move prediction at two times during each cycle of each price
wave in a current cyclic model

« Please turn now to figure 2-16. On the lower half of this figure, a wave
with a period of 16 time units is shown up to time A.

Page 31


Page 32

CyeliTec Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 6 How FLD’s Are Related to Waves (Cont'd)

© In order to better illustrate the basic ideas of FLD interactions with
price movements, an FLD number for this wave has been derived
omitting the addition of the number one. Thus, the FLD number used is
just period divided by two, which is: 16 + 2 equals 8.

Note: The reason for the addition of the number one in normal
FLD operations will be made clear in a future lesson.

This (slightly modified) FLD is shown in the lower half of the figure as
a dashed line out to time D.

« In the upper portion of this figure, time has elapsed (from time A to

time D) so that the original price wave is now shown complete to time
D. The portion of the FLD shown below is repeated here and extend-
ed as far as it can be drawn.

Comparing the two displays of this figure, the elemental relation-

ships between a wave and the associated FLD are clearly visible.

1. In the lower display the FLD is showing a trough in the future
(at time B). This should predict the occurrence of a price wave
crest at the same time in the future. Comparison of the two dis-
plays shows that this came about.

2. In the lower display the FLD was moving up from time B to time
D in the future. This should mean that during the same time in
the future the associated price wave should be moving down.
Comparison with the upper display shows that this also came
about.

3. At time C the price wave moved from above its FLD to below.
This should mean that the price wave formed a crest one-
fourth of its period earlier (16+ 4, or 4 time units before time C)
and that the price wave is just one-half as far down from this
crest as it will go on this cycle. Once again it is seen that these
are correct conclusions.

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 7 How FLD’s Relate to Price Movement

° FLD

° FLD
vide

interactions with single ideal waves provide exact conclusions.

interactions with composite waves or with price movement pro-
a great deal of useful information, but do not provide exact

conclusions.

° The

interpretative statements regarding FLD interactions with single

ideal waves can be restated in a more useful form for use with price
movement as follows:

1.

When an FLD forms a crest in future time, it is implied that
price action caused by the associated price wave will probably
form a trough in the vicinity of the same time in the future.

Similarly, when an FLD forms a trough in future time, it is implied
that price action caused by the associated price wave will prob-
ably form a crest in the vicinity of the same time in the future.

When an FLD is falling in future time, it is implied that price
action caused by the associated price wave will probably be driven
upward during that same period of time in the future.

When an FLD is rising in future time, it is implied that price
action caused by the associated price wave will probably be driven
downward during that same period of time in the future.

When prices have been above an FLD and then break below it, at
the time of the break one should measure into the past one-half
of the period of the wave on which the FLD is based. Within
this span of time, the highest price high is probably associated
with a crest of the wave on which the FLD is based.

The amount that prices have fallen from this highest price to the
price at the time of the FLD break has predictive implications.
Prices can be expected to move further down by this same amount
before reaching the price low associated with the trough of this
particular wave.

When prices have been below an FLD and then break above it,

Page 35


Page 36

 

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 7 How FLD’s Relate to Price Movement (Cont'd)

at the time of the break one should measure into the past one-
half of the period of the wave on which the FLD is based. Within
this span of time, the lowest price low is probably associated with
a trough of the wave on which the FLD is based.

The amount that prices have risen from this lowest price to the
price at the time of the FLD break has predictive implications.
Prices can be expected to move further up by this same amount
before reaching the price high associated with the crest of this
particular wave.

It should be noted that in the ideal case, crests and troughs of a price

wave are found one-fourth of a wave period prior to the FLD break. On
the other hand, in 5 and 6 above you are directed to look within the last
one-half of a wave period for such troughs and crests. This deviation
from the ideal is required in the case of price movement because of
variance from ideal conditions, and because the sum of a number of
waves is involved.

* The break of an FLD is also complicated by the non-ideal waves and

complex composite patterns of actual price motion. At times, price
waves shorter than the wave on which the FLD is based will cause prices
to move back and forth across the FLD several times before they finally
remain on the side indicating a break. Such occasions will produce a
range of break points. The inferences drawn should also be expressed
in terms of a range, such as a range of values for the predicted price
move to the next crest (or trough) of the wave on which the FLD is
based.

© Prices are said to break an FLD when the average price falls on the op-

posite side of the FLD from the side it has been on in the recent past.

The actual point of the break will usually lie in between the time lines
(representing days or weeks) on the chart. However, the point can be
easily located by drawing a straight line between the centers (average
prices) of the two price bars that straddle the break. The point where
this line crosses the FLD line is the desired break point.

As previously mentioned, an estimate can be made at the time of an
FLD break as to how much further in the direction of the break prices

SECTION 7 How FLD’s Relate to Price Movement (Cont'd)

can be expected’ to go before the occurrence of the next price reversal
associated with the wave on which the FLD is based.

For an FLD break in the upward direction, this estimate is made in
the following way:

1. Locate the price bar associated with the most recent trough of
the wave on which the FLD is based.

2. Determine the price difference between the lowest point on this
trough-associated price bar and the actual point of the FLD break
(established in the manner described in the preceding paragraph).

3. Using this price difference, measure upward from the point of the
FLD break to a higher price wnich now becomes the estimated price
objective sought

Similarly, for an FLD break in the downward direction, an estimate of
the extent of further price movement in the direction of the break is
made as follows:

1. Locate the price bar associated with the most recent crest of the
wave on which the FLD is based.

2. Determine the price difference between the highest point on this
crest-associated price bar and the actual point of the FLD break.

3. Using this price difference, measure downward from the point of
the FLD break to a lower price which now becomes the estimated
price objective sought.

SECTION 8 More Information from Multiple FLD’s

Single FLD’s are useful for many purposes, but the real power of the
FLD concept lies in the interpretation of the patterns formed by all of
the FLD's associated with a given cyclic model. Please refer to figure
2-21 during the following discussion.

© FLD Location vs Price

When prices are going up, FLD’s tend to be below prices. The more
FLD’s that are below prices, the more vulnerable prices are to a down-
ward reversal. When prices are going down, FLD's tend to be above
prices. The more FLD's that are above prices, the more vuln ‘rable
prices are to an upward reversal.

This can be thought of as the magnet effect, with FLD's tending to draw
prices toward themselves.

© FLD Distribution

When a number of FLD’s are all moving steeply in the same direction (up
or down), and they are evenly spaced and parallel with respect to each
other — a price reversal, when it comes, is likely to be large and swift.

Irregular spacing, with wide gaps between some FLD’s and narrow gaps
between others, implies a spotty or weak potential price reversal.

When FLD's are flat (horizontal), they are unlikely to be broken by a
potential price reversal

¢ The FLD Pause Zone

One or more FLD's may form an oscillatory, horizontal pattern in future
time. Such a pattern implies a price-move pause or stop in the area of
the zone indicated in this way. Such a region is called a pause zone, or
PZ, for short.

© The FLD Cascade Effect

FLD's may arrange themselves so that a break of one FLD projects a
price move that implies a break of the next, projecting a larger price

Page 44


Page 45

CycliTee Services

 

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

SECTION 8 More Information from Multiple FLD'’s (Cont'd)

move, etc. Such a potential for multiple FLD breaks is called the
cascade effect. A cascade stops when prices project into a gap between
FLD's, when prices project into a reversal pause zone, or when there are
no more FLD’s to be broken in the direction that prices are moving.

* You should study the examples of figure 2-21 to gain an initial
feeling for these distinctive FLD patterns. When you have finished
study of this figure,

SECTION 9 Action Signals, Stop-Loss Levels, and Potential-Risk Ratios

© Cyclic analysis procedures applied to actual price movements provide
conclusions as to likelihoods rather than certainties. This results in the
need for action signals to tell us when the probabilities are high that
anticipated wave action in prices has actually started.

¢ The most common action signals are price breaks of valid trend lines
or FLD's.

* To be useful, action signals must be set up before the price reversal
they are to test occurs.

¢ Before a decision is made to enter into a transaction, four informa-
tion items are needed:

1. Assurance that the price reversal anticipated is underway.

2. An estimate of how far prices are likely to move in the desired
direction.

3. The price action that will signal that the transaction has gone
wrong.

4. The transaction entry price that will satisfactorily limit risk, and
produce an acceptable potential profit percentage.

* Action signals satisty the first need.
* FLD price projection methods satisfy the second need.

© The third need is satisfied by a special kind of action signal called
the stop-loss /evel. It comes into being after the price break of an FLD
or VTL has identified the occurrence in the recent past of a critical wave
trough (or crest). The stop-loss level for a long transaction is the price
‘one trading unit below the price low associated with such a trough. The
stop-loss level for a short transaction is the price one trading unit above
the price high associated with such a crest. If prices break a stop-loss
level, that break is the signal that the transaction has gone wrong and
should be terminated

* FLO price projections define sell or cover-short price levels. The stop-
loss level defines the price at which the transaction will be terminated if


Page 48

CycliTec Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2

(Cont'd)

the expected price move does not develop satisfactorily. Thus, once
these two transaction termination price levels have been established, a
transaction entry price can be determined such that the ratio of profit
potential to loss risk is any desired value. Setting the transaction entry
price in this way then fixes the potential profit percentage gain
expectation.

SUMMARY

Page 54


Page 55

 

CycliTec Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

Summary

¢ The Cyclic Concepts of Lesson number one were converted in this les-
son to methods and procedures by which transaction decision-making
information can be derived from a chart of past equity prices.

Both downtrends and uptrends can be in existence at the same time.
The concept of trend does not have value unless it is associated with a
time period. Cyclic Concepts provide a convenient way of doing this.

¢ A meaningful definition of trend is:

The trend underlying a given price wave is the sum of all wave
components longer than that wave.

* Trend is always spoken of in association with a wave of a given period.

The cyclic concept of trend permits estimation of trend direction and
strength in future time.

© A valid trend line is a trend line drawn according to specific rules so as
to have meaning related to cyclic concepts. These rules are:

1. A valid trend line is drawn connecting two consecutive price lows
(or highs) associated with identified wave troughs (or crests).

2. A valid trend line is drawn so as not to cut through, or intercept,
price bars on a chart.

3. A valid downtrend line must not be drawn from price highs be-

tween which there is a trough of a wave longer than the wave on
which the valid downtrend line is based.

Rule number 3 applies only in the case of a valid downtrend line; it
does not apply for a valid uptrend line.

A valid trend line break by price movement does not necessarily imply
additional price movement in the direction of the break.

Valid trend line breaks can be used to identify wave troughs and

paasasay SU31y ITV
“uy ‘ss24g SA9PELL CL61 @ 1SUAdO)

CycliTec Services Training Course Page 56

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

Summary (Cont'd)
crests in complex charts of composite price movement.

When using a valid trend line break to identify a wave trough or crest,
proceed in the following way.

For a downtrend line break:
a. Look back from the break into past time by an amount equal to
the period of the wave next longer than the wave on which the
trend line is based.

b. Find the lowest price low within this span of time.

c. Consider that in all probability a trough of this next longer wave is
associated with this price low.

For an uptrend line break:
a. Look back from the break into past time by an amount equal to
the period of the wave next longer than the wave on which the
trend line is based.

b. Find the highest price high in this span of time.

c. Consider that in all probability a crest of this next longer wave is
associated with this price high.

Valid trend lines are referred to as VTL’s.
© FLD is the abbreviation for Future Line of Demarcation.
* There is an FLD associated with each wave of a cyclic model

* An FLO is a line or curve drawn on a price chart. It is constructed as
follows

1. Determine the FLD number. This number is found by dividing the
associated wave period by two, then adding one.


Page 57

 

cycliTec Services Trai

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

Summary (Cont'd)

2. Counting the last price bar to the right on a price chart as one,
count time units into the past until the FLD number is reached.

3. For the price-bar where the count ended, find the average price
and replot it in the first time position to the right of the last price
bar on the chart.

4. Then find the average price for the price bar just to the right of
the one on which the count ended. Re-plot this value in the sec-
ond time position to the right of the last price bar on the chart.

5. Continue transferring consecutive average prices to future time
positions until you run out of data, or run out of space on the
chart.

Note: The average value of the high-low range for a price bar can be
found graphically as the center of the price bar, or it can be found by
adding the highest price of the bar to the lowest price and then dividing
by two.

Note: If the FLD-number count should end on a time space when the
markets were not open (daily charts only), connect the average prices
of the two price bars on either side of the time gap by a straight line.
Use the price value of this straight line where it intersects the day of the
needed average price.

When an FLD based on a given wave is broken by prices, a high
probability exists that within a time span into the past equal to one-
half of that wave period, a price low (or high) corresponding to a trough
(or crest) of that wave has occurred.

When an FLD based on a given wave is broken by prices, prices have
moved approximately one-half as far as that wave is likely to carry them
for that cycle. Twice in each wave cycle such an estimation of future
price movement becomes possible, once when prices are moving down
and once when they are moving up.

When prices are moving down, some FLD's will be above prices. Such
FLD's may be thought of as acting like magnets to eventually pull prices

up.

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
‘All Rights Reserved

CycliTec Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

Summary (Cont'd)

When prices are moving up, some FLD’s will be below prices. Such
FLD’s may be thought of as acting like magnets to eventually pull prices
down

When an FLD is moving down in the future, that is where prices will be
pushed up by the wave on which that FLD is based, provided the trend
underlying that wave does not override such a move.

When an FLD is moving up in the future, that is where prices will be
pushed down by the wave on which that FLD is based, provided the
trend underlying that wave does not override such a move.

When an FLD forms a trough in the future, that is the approximate time
when the wave on which that FLD is based is likely to cause a price high
associated with the wave crest.

When an FLD forms a crest in the future, that is the approximate time
when the wave on which that FLD is based is likely to cause a price
low associated with the wave trough.

FLD interactions with a single ideal price wave produce exact con-
clusions; while FLD interactions with the composite waves of actual
price movement produce probabilistic conclusions.

When the FLD's associated with all of the waves of a cyclic model are
plotted, they tend to form distinctive patterns which provide additional
useful information as to the most likely course of future price action

An FLD cascade is likely to occur when a price break by a single FLD
implies that another FLD will be broken, thus further implying still larger
price moves, etc.

When several FLD's are evenly spaced above prices and moving down,
the next price reversal in the upward direction is likely to break all of
these FLD's in sequence providing a cascade of estimates for the
ensuing price rise.

When several FLD's are evenly spaced below prices and moving up, the
next price reversal in the downward direction is likely to break all of

Page 58


Page 59

CycliTec Services Training Course

LESSON NO. 2
FROM CYCLIC CONCEPTS TO TRADING DECISIONS

Summary (Cont'd)

these FLD's in sequence providing a cascade of estimates for the
ensuing price drop.

Uneven spacing of FLD's implies the likelihood that a cascade of price
move estimates will not develop, or will stop in a wide gap between
FLD's.

‘A congestion of one or more FLD’s in future time in a horizontal region
is called a pause zone, or PZ for short. Such pause zones imply a high
likelihood of a price-move pause or reversal, or of a price region where
any existing cascade may terminate.

An action signal is a price break of a valid trend line or an FLD that
implies that an anticipated price move is underway and that action
should therefore be taken according to plan.

FLD projections provide transaction-termination price objectives.

A stop-loss level is a special kind of action signal. It is set up after
the price break of a VTL or FLD has identified a price high (or low) as-
sociated with a critical wave crest (or trough). The stop-loss level is the
price just one trading unit above such a price high (for sell-short trans-
actions) or one trading unit below such a price low (for long or buy
transactions). If prices break a stop-loss level, that break is the signal
that the transaction has gone wrong and should be terminated

For a long transaction, the difference between the buy price and the sell
price is called the potential of the trade.

For a short transaction, the difference between the sell-short price and
the cover-short price is called the potential of the trade.

For a long transaction, the difference between the buy price and the
stop-loss level is called the risk of the trade.

For a short transaction, the difference between the stop-loss level
and the sell-short price is called the risk of the trade.

The ratio of potential to risk (which is potential divided by risk) is called
the potential-risk ratio, or PR for short.

Summary (Cont'd)

* The potential-risk ratio tells you how many times larger the potential
profit of a transaction is likely to be than the loss that is risked.

« The potential percentage is the percent profit (relative to invested funds)
that is expected from a successful transaction. For long transactions,
this is 100 times the potential, divided by the buy price. For short trans-
actions, it is 100 times the potential, divided by the sell-short price.

« When a transaction termination price has been established (a sell or
cover-short price) and a stop-loss level has been set, it is often desir-
able to determine the transaction entry price (the buy or sell-short
price) corresponding to several different potential-risk ratios. The fol-
lowing relationships are used for this purpose.

For a Buy Situation:
The buy price, B, that we wish to find is given by

p - PRX SU +S
PR +7

where
PR_ is the potential-risk ratio of interest
SL_ is the stop-loss level
Sis the sell price.

For a Sell-Short Situation:

The sell-short price, SS, that we wish to find is given by

=(PRx SL) +C
ss PR +7

where

C is the cover-short price.

A price break of a VTL or FLD indicating a
strong likelihood that an anticipated price
move is under way.

The size or amount of the price movement involved
as a price wave goes from trough to crest.

The wave period estimate formed by averag-
ing together two or more trough-to-trough
measurements made using probable loca-
tions of the wave troughs in the composite
price movement.

A common way of plotting or graphing equity
prices. The high and low prices for a day are joined
by a vertical straight line to show the range over
which prices traded for that day. The consecutive
set of such lines is called a daily bar-chart, or bar-
Graph of that equity. Similarly, the high and low
prices for a week can be compiled to form a weekly
bar-chart for the equity.

Prices break a VTL or an FLD when they pass from
‘one side of the VTL or FLD to the other (from above
to below, or vice versa).

A rippling, or one-thing-followed-by-another effect.
In cyclic theory, the tendency under certain circum-
stances for an FLD break to predict prices which
will break another FLD, thus predicting still greater
price moves, etc.

A term to indicate that price movements of nego-
tiable equities have similar qualities, in spite of the
diversity of visual appearances of price charts.

A single wave in price movement, or in the com-
posite wave formed from several single waves. One
component of price action would be, for example,
the 18-month wave, or the wave whose period is
nominally 18 months.

(or SELL SHORT) To sell borrowed stock in the
expectation that prices will subsequently go down.
If they do, a profit is made by replacing the
borrowed stock by purchase on the open market at
the reduced (later) price.

A special kind of action signal. A price level at
which a transaction is terminated because the
probabilities no longer favor a profitable outcome.

against the answer key, circling any that are wrong. Then go back through
the examination and refer to the text material to refresh your memory on

Cassette Script

Review and Introduction

This Is the start of the recording
that goos with lesson number two
fof the Cyclitec Services Training
Course. The lesson is entitled
From Gyelle Concepts to Trading
Decisions.

In lesson number one you learned
‘of & surprising number of charac-
teristics shared by price move-
ments of all negotiable equities.
You were told that all price mover
mente consist of the sum of price
waves. Further, that these waves
have harmonicatly-related periods,
‘and show @ strong tendency to
have troughs that coincide. Such
waves have amplitudes that in-
‘crease as periods get longer—and
it Is one particular set of waves
that tends to persist throughout
time. You learned that these waves
slowly and smoothly change sm-
plitude as time goes by, and that
wave period varies slightly with
such amplitude change. These
facts were stated as principles.and
wore given names so that they can
be readily referred to.

In adgition, you wore told of the
kinds of decision-making informa-
tion that can be derived from a
‘chart of past equity prices by the
Use of these principles, but you
have not as yet been told how such
Information 1s obtained,

 

 

You became aware In lesson num
ber one of a nominal cyclic model,
oF a list of the periods of waves
you may generally expect to be
Present In. price movement. You
‘also learned of variation, one
result of which is the fact that the
Current eyclle model active at a
‘ivan time ina given equity is
likely to be somewhat different
from the nominal model, and from
the current cyclic model for other
‘equities at that same time.

‘The techniques needed to extract
pre-transaction information on
‘what prices are most likely to do In
the future depend strongly on our
ability to determine the cyclic

eyelle model

Hine. ‘Similarly, a downtrend line

owntrend line, ete

5.SECOND PAUSE
BEFORE CONTINUATION

Valid Trend Line Use

(One of the many adages that
abound on Wall Street says, in

The longer and stronger @ trend,
the more likely itis to continue.

 

Inthe tight of eyctic concepts, we
would be inclined to believe the
stronger part, but to disagree with
the longer connotation.

‘The Principle of Proportionality
tells us that the greater the ampli
tude of a price wave, the longer the
period. Since a trend that is strong
Includes a price wave of large
‘amplitude and since such a wave
algo has 2 long period, the result-
Ing trend is likely to continue for
some time.

(On the other hand, the Principle of
Gyclicality assures us that price
movement consists of waves.

Thus, the longer a trend has been
inexistence, the mote likely itis to

reverse direction soon, as the res-
onsible wave progresses toward
completion of a full cycle,

In conventional charting tech
niques, a trend line Is used to
signal such possible reversals of
direction of trend. Used thus,
when prices break from above
own through an uptrend line, the
interpretation is that the uptrend
indicated by the line has been
fevorsed and that lower prices are
likely to follow. Similarly it prices
break from below up through a
downtrend line, the implication is
that the downtvend indicates by
the line has been reversed and that
higher prices are likely to follow.

Many times these expectations are
realized, but it is somewhat dan-
gerous fo depend on this. We now
know that many different trends
exist at the game time. AS a resul
whether prices will continue to
move in the direction of the break
fof one of the associated trond
lines Is dependent on the strength
land. direction of the remaining
trends,

We will use valid trend lines for a
slightly different purpose. To un-
derstand what this purpose is and
‘why it is important, consider the
following

When price waves combine by
summation, very complex com-
posite waves can be formed In
‘which visual identification of the
component waves. Is difficult.
We need methods which can
help us locate the time positions
‘of individual wave troughs in
these complex composite price
movements. One such method
makes use of valid trend tines.

Without turning off your tape unit,
feler back to figure 22 at the
fend of section 2. In this figure,
notice how the trends underlying
shorter and shorter period waves
reverse direction more and more
often. In fact, you will note that
trend underlying a_given wave
tends to reverse itself as the next
longer wave reverses. From this
‘observation we can draw a generat

‘Avalid trend line based on a giv
fen wave strongly tends to be
broken by prices after the for
mation of a trough or a crest of
the next longer wave.

We can re:phrase this statement in
a more positive and useful way.

A break by prices through a
valid trend line strongly sug-
{ests that the wave just longer
than the wave on which the valid
trend line is based has formed a
trough or a crest in the recent
past.

‘Thus, we see that a valid trend line
‘can be used as one way of con-
firming that a given component
wave has formed troughs or crests
at various times In the past. From
the locations of these troughs or
crests we can gain valuable meas-
Uurements of component wave peri-
ods and phases.

It should be emphasized that any
cone technique, such a8 this one,
will give information at one time
but perhaps not at another. This is
why several methods are needed in
‘order to gain sufficient information
fon which to base trading
decisions

How FLD's are Related 10 Waves

FLO's work only because of the
validity of Gyclic Concepts. These
Concepts assure that FLO'S will
have certain characteristics that
will be found to be most useful
Let us now investigate several of
these characteristics.

‘Assume that a glvan price move-
ment consists of a single, idea!
wave. To construct an FLO based
fon this one wave, we would first
derive the associated FLO number
by dividing the wave period by two
{and adding one.

We note that, except for very small
wave periods, the number one that
has been added is small by com-
parison with half of the wave
Period. Thus, for waves of reason
able length, the FLD number is
very close to being just one-half of
the wave period,

In forming the desired FLO, we are
therefore transferring wave values
‘hat are very close to one-half of a
wave period in past time,into new
future time positions. The result-
ing FLD is a duplicate of the
friginal wave, except that it is
time-shifted forward by one-half of
the wave period. We recognize this
a being a case of two otherwise
identical waves with a phase ci
erence between them of one-half
of the wave perio,

 

Now, waves of the kind experl=
fenced in price movement show
three very interesting characteris-
ties when phase-shifted by one-
half perioa

First: One wave is just forming a
crest as the other is forming a
Hough, and vice versa,

Second: One wave is always moy-
Ing up while the other” moves
down, and vice versa

Third: When the two waves inter-
sect each other, one has formed a
‘test just one-fourth of a wave per
od earlier. its, at the time of the
Intersection, one-half as far down
as it will go, The other wave has
formed 8 trough onesfourth of a
wave period earlier and is one-half
as far up as it will go.

Al of these characteristice ave
Useful, Out it ie the last one that
provides FLD's with the potential
for prediction ot possibie price

How FLD's Relate to

You nave observed the manner in
which a single, ideal price wave
Interacts with its own FLO. in such
ideal cases, the conclusions 10 be
derived are exact. Such exact con-
clusions are not possible when
FLO's are used with actual price
movements because price moves
ments are composed of the sum of
2 number of waves and because
the Principle of Variation teaches
us that component price waves are
‘ot ideal. In spite of these diff.
culties, FLO's provide an immense
amount of useful information when
used with price action, a8 we shall

Our purpose now Is to restate the
rules for interpretation of the inter=
action between prices and FLO’s in
4 form more useful in connection
with composite waves and price
action in general

When we see an FLO dropping in
value in future time, we can ase
sume that the price wave associa-
{ed with this FLD will be causing
prices to rise at that time in the
future, provided that the trend
Underlying this wave is not over-
riding the wave movement at that
time. An analogous statement can
be made if an FLD is rising In
future tine

When we see an FLO forming a
trough in future time, a price high
‘associated with the crest of the
wave on which the FLO is based Is.
uite likely to be formed at or near
this same future time. Again, an
analogous statement can be made
Concerning futurestime FLO crests

When prices have been above an
FLD, then drop down through it
Ime probabilities favor a price high
‘associated with the crest of the
wave on which the FLD Is based

5-SECOND PAUSE
BEFORE CONTINUATION

More Information trom
Mutipie FLO's

You have now studies the con-
struction and use of single FLD's,
land have gained some feeling for
hhow FLD's can be used. AS effec
tive as they are, single FLD's tell
fonly part of a complex story, and
‘the real power of the FLD concept
becomes apparent only when
FLO's are interpreted as a group.

Remember, an FLD can be con
structed corresponding to each
component wave in any cyclic
model. Each such FLO Is unique
land itfers trom all the other

feven though they all are construc
ted from past price action. When
all of the FLD's associated with a
yell model are plotted, patterns

 

tare formed. From these patterns,
‘reat deal can be learned regarding
the future movements of prices.
The more important of these pat-
terne will be studied in this lesson
fang refinements to the pattern
interpretation process willbe intro
duced in following lessons.

When prices are going steeply
down many FLO's will be found
above prices, and sloping down
ward also. Such a pattern behaves
much like a magnet as far as
Drices are concered, tending to
Cause prices to stop going down
and start going up.

 

‘The reason for this tendency is as
follows: Whenever prices break
FLD's, additional price movement
Jn the direction of the break is
implied. If FLD's ate all above
prices, then no downward FLO
break ‘can occur to Imply further
downward price movement. But
consider the potential for upward
price movement. We know that
price waves will sooner or later
cause a price reversal to take
place; and when this happens,
many FLO's are present above
price to be broken, thereby imply-
Ing additional movement in the up-
ward direction

A similar situation exists when
prices are going steeply up. In this
ase, many FLO's will be found
below prices—and this pattern can
also be interpreted as. indicating
that prices are vulnerable to a turn-
around.

AAs a corollary, it can be sald that
the more FLD's there are above
prices, the more likely it 1s that
prices’ will soon stop going down
and start going up. Similarly, the
‘more FLD's that are found below
prices, the more likely it is that
prices will soon stop going up and
start going down.

‘There are other important kinds of
FLD patterns, also. A number of
FLO's may be above prices and
moving down (for example), and
may be either evenly spaced or
very irregularly spaced with re
spect to each other. It evenly
spaced, the expected price tuin-
around, when it comes, is likely to

result in an extensive new price
‘move in the opposite direction. If
the FLD spacing is iregular, the
price tumaround is likely to be
truncated,or broken into a series of
cyclic thrusts and pullbacks,

 

‘The reason for this pattern operat-
Ing as it does is the very Import-
‘and FLO cascade effect. Imagine
fan FLD based on a short-petiod
wave being broken. When It is, a
price prediction in the direction of
the break is implied. Then further
‘suppose that the FLD based on the
ext longer period wave is so
located that it is. likely to be
broken ifthe fist FLO price predic-
tion comes true. Then a second
price move prediction is implied
‘even before the second FLD break
thas occurred. Clearly, the more
regularly spaced and parallel a
series of FLD's are with respect to
each other, the more likely are
‘these sequential breaks to happen
‘Thus, regular and parallel spacing
‘of FLO's implies large potential
price change—once the expec-
ted price reversal takes. place—
because of the resulting tendency
for FLD price projections to
cascade,

Finally, suppose a group of FLO's
are spaced close together and are
more of less horizontal with wide
‘spacings on either side before
cother FLO's are encountered. This
would imply that prices would
tend to oscillate in ‘small moves
within the region of such FLO
‘groupings. Thus, such groupings
signal where, in the future, prices
are likely to pause in a given move
(These pauses are, of course,
‘caused by the wave action that we
‘are by now familiar with.) Such
fegions of FLD congestion are
appropriately called pause zones.

‘The interpretation of these various
istributions of multiple FLD's will
play an important role in the cyclic
analyses of trading and invest-
‘ment situations as taught in this

and refer to section 8 entitled More
Information from Multiple FLD's of
the accompanying text.

Action Signals, Stop-Loss Levels,

So far, you nave learned that cyclic

inition of trend tines that makes

0 far, and have been given

Ideas and methods are requires
bbotore all of these concepts can be
related 10 one another effectively
You will nave ample opportunity In

techniques to work, and to observe
the interrelationships involved

You should now become acquain-
ted with the idea of action signals.

When a cyclic analysis is perform:
‘ed on an equity, certain probabil
Istic conclusions will result. By
this it is meant that the analysis,
will lead you to stiongly suspect
that certain future price move-
ments are likely to come about
and that such movements are lke
Wy to occur in the vicinity of a
particular future time,

Because such conclusions regard
ing future occurrences represent
likelinoods rather than certainties,
It is wise to set up teste for the
anticipated occurrences. These
tests require tuture prices to be-
hhave in a particular manner betore
WW can be sald the probabilities
favor that the predicted, or expec
ted, price movements are actually
underway,

Such tests are called action sig-
nals

‘The most common action signals
are price breaks of valid trend lines

and price breaks of FLD's, both of
which you have already’ learned
something about when they are
used in a somewhat different
sense, Another type of action
signal isthe stop-loss level, which
will be described in a moment.

But first we must introduce anoth-
fer concept that you shouls become
acquainted with: the thorough
assessment of the possible finan
cial outcome of a transaction,

REVIEW

© Lesson number two presented a set of definitions, rules, and methods based
on the cyclic concepts of lesson number one. These are so important to all
future work with cyclic ideas that a complete summary is presented here
for review.

@ A current cyclic model is a table of the periods and phases of the waves
found to be responsible for the price movement of a given equity at a given
time.

@ The srend underlying a given price wave is the sum of all waves longer than
the given wave.

© Valid trend line, or VTL's, are trend lines drawn according to the
following rules:

1. A valid trend line connects two consecutive price lows (or highs)
associated with identified wave troughs (or crests

  

2. A valid trend line is drawn so as not to cut through, or intercept, price
bars lying between the price lows (or highs) which define the valid
trend line.

 

3. A valid downtrend line must not be drawn from price highs between
which there exists a trough of a wave longer than the wave on which
the valid downtrend line is based.

@ When a valid uptrend line is broken by prices in the downward direction,
information is implied with regard to the wave just longer than the wave
on which the valid uptrend line is based. In the recent past, within a time
span equal to the period of this next longer wave, the highest price high is
very likely to be associated with a crest of this next longer wave.

© When a valid downtrend line is broken by prices in the upward direction,
information is implied with regard to the wave just longer than the wave on
which the valid downtrend line is based. In the recent past, within a time
span equal to the period of this next longer wave, the lowest price low is very
ely to be associated with a trough of this next longer wave.

 

© A future line of demarcation, or FLD, is formed by shifting consecutive
price averages forward an amount of time equal to one time unit more than
one-half of the period of the wave on which the FLD is based.

 

Page 1.

 

 


 

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 
 
  

—-

tet

REVIEW continued

@ When an FLD forms a crest in future time, the probabilities favor that
in the vicinity of that same time a price low will form that is associated
with a trough of the wave on which the FLD is based.

   

 

© When an FLD forms a trough in future time, the probabilities favor that
the vicinity of that same time a price high will form that is associated
with a crest of the wave on which the FLD is based.

 

@ When an FLD is falling in future time, the implication is that price action
caused by the associated price wave will be driven upward during that same

period of future time.

@ When an FLD is rising in future time, the implication is that price action
used by the associated price wave will be driven downward during that

same period of future time.

 

@ When prices have been above an FLD and then break below it, at the time
of the break one should measure into the past one-half of the period of the
wave on which the FLD is based. Within this span of time, the highest price
high is very likely associated with a crest of the wave on which the FLD
is based.

‘At such times one should measure how far prices have fallen from this
highest price to the price at the time of the FLD break. This measurement
is an indication of how much farther down prices are likely to go before
this particular wave forms a trough.

@ When prices have been below an FLD and then break above it, at the time
of the break one should measure into the past one-half of the period of the
wave on which the FLD is based. Within this span of time, the lowest price
low is very likely associated with a trough of the wave on which the FLD is
based.

At such times one should measure how far prices have risen from this lowest
price to the price at the time of the FLD break. This measurement is an
indication of how much farther up prices are likely to go before this partic-
ular wave forms a crest.

© When prices are going down, FLD’s tend to be above prices. The more
FLD's there are above prices, the more vulnerable prices are to an upward
reversal.

| LESSON

      

© When prices are going up, FLD’s tend to be below prices. The more FLD's
there are below prices, the more vulnerable prices are to a downward
reversal.

© When a number of FLD’s are moving steeply in the same direction and are
evenly spaced and parallel with respect to each other, a price reversal (when
it comes) is likely to be large and swift.

© When a number of FLD's are irregularly spaced, with relatively large gaps
between some FLD's, a price reversal (when it comes) is likely to be weak
or spotty.

@ When FLD’s are horizontal they are unlikely to be broken by a price
reversal.

@ When FLD’s lie in the direction of a potential price reversal, but are moving
in the same direction that prices would be moving after such a reversal,
such FLD’s are unlikely to be broken by prices when the reversal does
occur.

© FLD's may arrange themselves so that a break of one FLD projects a price
move that implies a break of another, thus projecting a larger price move,
ete. Such a potential for multiple FLD breaks is called a cascade formation.

© Cascades tend to stop when prices project into a large gap between FLD’s,
when prices project into a reversal pause zone, or when there are no more
FLD’s to be broken in the direction that prices are moving.

© One or more FLD’s may form an oscillatory, horizontal pattern in future
time. Such a pattern implies a price-move pause or stop in the price region
occupied by the pattern. Such a region is called a pause zone, or PZ. FLD.
cascades often tend to terminate in such a pause zone.

AVTL or FLD break can signify recent-past occurrence of a critical wave
trough or crest. If such a break has been chosen as an action signal for a
contemplated transaction, then the stop-loss level for the transaction is just
below the price low associated with such a trough or just above the price
high associated with such a crest.

© Potential profit, or potential, is the difference between the sell objective

and the sell-short price for a short transaction.

INTRODUCTION

© The concepts of trading and investing taught in this course are dependent
on the ability to separate price movement into individual price waves and
to determine the current status of these waves.

@ A tabulated summary of the information so derived is called a current
eyelic model.

© The process of construction of a current cyclic model involves three steps:
1. Construction of an initial cyclic model
2. Completion of a phasing analysis

2. Extraction of information from the results of a phasing analysis to
permit assembly of a current cyclic model.

© The accomplishment of these three steps requires knowledge and appli-
cation of the basic cyclic concepts and the coordinated use of several
techniques and methods based on these concepts.

© Most of the ideas, methods, and techniques needed have been treated in
prior lessons,

© In this lesson several more of the ideas and methods needed will be pre-
sented. Then the process of setting up an initial cyclic model will be des-
cribed. Next, phasing analysis will be dealt with. And finally the methods
of extracting information from the phasing analysis for use in constructing
a current cyclic model will be discussed,

© The process of phasing analysis requires more knowledgeability and
expertise than any other aspect of cyclic analysis. Although it is readily
learned, it is not a mechanical process. Instead, basic concepts and the
initial cyclic mode! serve as a starting point. Then the analyst uses a chain
of cyclic logic, augumented by a number of graphical methods, to build
evidence that leads to a final conclusion.

© Because of the central importance of the phasing analysis process, this
lesson includes extensive exercises designed to give you a thorough under-
standing of the methods involved. Then in subsequent lessons, ample op-
portunity will be provided for you to develop the necessary expertise.

THE CYCLIC MODEL CONCEPT REVISITED
© A cyclic model is a tabulation of wave variables.

The nominal cyclic model, or nominal model, is a tabulation of the average
expected wave periods for all negotiable equities.

A current cyclic model is a similar tabulation, but it contains the best esti-
mates of the values for the characteristics of the waves active in a given
equity at a given time.

 

© A current cyclic model contains not only wave period information but best
estimates of wave phases as well,

© Wave phase is most usefully tabulated in terms of the number of time units
that have elapsed since the last wave trough occurred. This measurement
is spoken of as “days along”, “weeks along”, etc.

   

© A current cyclic model also displays estimated current wave direction. Four
basic forms of arrows are used for this purpose as follows:

1, Down 1
2. Bottoming \_4
3. Up '
4. Topping
5. Flat —

(The flat arrow is usually only used in reference to ZL, which will be dis-
cussed in a later section of this lesson.)

© Additional information can be gained from these arrows by usi
ations of the basic forms such as:

1. Down, but a bottom is expected soon \_y
2. Up, but a top is expected soon (\ .

The information imparted by the arrows is directly derived from the phase
tabulation coupled with knowledge of ideal wave shape.

 

ig combin-

©@ It is convenient to include in the current cyclic model the FLD number
for each wave based on the best estimate of the actual wave period.

THE CYCLIC MODEL CONCEPT REVISITED continued

© Please turn to figure 3-1. Here a single wave with a 22-day period is shown
up toa current, or “now”, time. (This time would correspond on a price
chart to the last price-bar on the right.) The time positions of the troughs of
this wave are shown by the diamond markings below the wave.

 

 

Note that the phase of this wave at now time would be indicated as being 16
days along, meaning that 16 days have elapsed from the last wave trough

to present time. Given just this information, and without observing the
picture of the wave at all, we would know that the direction of this wave is
down at present time. (Be sure you understand how this conclusion would
be reached. If necessary, review this section in the tape cassette.)

poasasy siya IV
“Duy ‘ssa4g S49pE4] €L6] @ BEAD

© Ifa given price movement consisted of nothing more than this wave, the
current cyclic model would be shown like this:

©@ In this simple example the relationships needed are of course easy to see,
and a tabulation of the current cyclic model is not really needed. However,
in actual price movements, which consist of many non-ideal waves, the cyclic
model tabulation is essential.

© Recall figure 1-2 for the copper example of lesson one. This was a plot
of just the price-bars for copper as of 24 November 1972. By inspection
of this chart no conclusions were possible as to what prices were likely
todo next. If anything, the “longer-stronger trend” adage would lead
to the expectation of lower prices.

© But the current cyclic model, a copy of which is shown on the following
page, tells us something very different. It shows that all wave components
from 20 weeks to 18 months are past due for a trough. It also shows that
the 80-, 40-, 20-, and S-day waves are up. Only the 10-day wave is down,

THE PERIODOGRAM CONCEPT

© In order to set up a current cyclic model, itis necessary to identify the time
positions of the price lows corresponding to all the troughs of all of the
waves operating in price motion. This process is called a phasing analysis,
and the price lows identified are usually marked by phasing diamonds.

 

 

© The best estimate of wave period for each wave of the current cyclic model
is then obtained by averaging the lengths of a number of period samples
as measured from trough to trough.

@ Wave phase for each wave is automatically found from the position in time
of the last trough of the wave.

©@ Itis very difficult to do effective phasing analysis without some computa-
tional assistance. One form of computerized assistance designed for this
purpose is called a Periodogram.

© The Periodogram is a method of presenting information on any number of
waves simultaneously.

@ It is a plot of wave amplitudes versus wave periods. Amplitude is plotted
on the vertical axis, while period is plotted on the horizontal axis.

@ Each wave is represented on a Periodogram by a single vertical line. Am-
plitude is represented by the vertical height of the line, and period is
represented by the position of the line on the horizontal axis.

  

© In figure 3-2 a single wave is shown along with the corresponding Periodo-
gram,

Study this figure carefully so that you understand how the Periodogram
display depicts the wave shown below it.

© In figure 3-3 a series of waves is shown. Plot the corresponding Periodo-
gram at the top of the page, and check your results against figure 3-4,

© In figure 3-5 a tabulation of wave amplitudes and periods is provided.
Complete the corresponding Periodogram at the top of the page, and check
your results against figure 3-6.

@ Figure 3-7 shows how you can lay out an ideal price wave with reasonable
accuracy if you know amplitude, period, and phase. On a separate piece of

 

Page 10——


A
Hf HET f courses

THE PERIODOGRAM CONCEPT continued

paper, choosing your own period and amplitude, follow the instructions
given in the figure. Draw one or two waves (taking short cuts and omitting
the labelling as you become familiar with the process) and then proceed
to the next exercise.

 

© Figures 3-8 and 3-9 constitute an exercise in reconstructing waves from the
information given in their Periodogram. The Periodogram in figure 3-8
shows the periods and amplitudes of three waves. Their phase is established
in figure 3-9 by the vertical dashed line, which marks the time at which
troughs of the three waves are to concide. Without looking at figure 3-10,
use the method learned from figure 3-7 to draw the three waves on the
center lines provided in the lower portion of figure 3-9.

Next, sum the three waves to form the composite wave on the center line
provided in the upper portion of figure 3-9. After you have drawn the com-
posite wave, draw in phasing diamonds in the space just below it. Place a
phasing diamond to mark each trough of all three component waves,
using a separate row of diamonds for each component. Be sure you have
drawn the waves so that a three-diamond nest-of-lows falls on the dashed
line. When you have finished, compare your results with those shown in
figure 3-10.

FIGURE 3-7 HOW TO GRAPH A PRICE WAVE

10.
1.

14.
15,

 
  

1973 Traders Press, Ine.

epee

. Draw a horizontal center line about which the wave is to oscillate.
. Mark off on this line, using small tick marks, a distance equal to the wave period.

 
  

ivide this distance in half by placing a tick mark in the center.

. Divide each of the halves in half; then divide each of the quarters in half again so that the period

distance is divided into eight equal parts separated by tick marks.

. Starting at the left, label the nine tick marks a through i.
. Directly on the center line, at tick marks a, e, and i, place heavy dots, or points. Label these points

1, 5, and 9, respectively.

. Measure vertically up from tick mark c a distance equal to half the wave amplitude. At this spot,

place a point and label it 3.

Similarly, place point 7 one-half amplitude directly below tick mark g.

Directly above tick mark b, place another tick mark at the same height as point 3 (one-half ampli-
tude). Label this tick mark j.

Half way between tick marks b and j, place another tick mark labeled k.

Half way between tick marks j and k, place a point and label it 2.

Directly above tick mark d, place point 4 at the same height as point 2.

In the manner of steps 9 through 12, and using tick marks m and n, place points 6 and & at three-
quarters of the half amplitude directly below tick marks fand h.

Erase tick marks j through n and the labels for tick marks a through i.

Now join points / through 9 sequentially with a smooth curve, and you have a reasonable approxi-
mation of a price wave.

THE PRICE MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM AND INITIAL

CYCLIC MODEL

@ A Periodogram for a set of ideal waves is exact and definitive. A precise
Periodogram can be constructed from the set of waves, and the set of waves
can be precisely reconstructed from the Periodogram.

© A Periodogram for a particular price movement cannot be so precisely inter-
preted. Nevertheless, it is one of the most powerful aids available in setting
up a current cyclic model.

 

 

© A Periodogram for price movement consists of vertical lines equally spaced
on the period (horizontal) axis of the Periodogram. This spacing is arbi-
trarily chosen when the Periodogram is constructed and has no special
wave meaning.

© As one proceeds along the horizontal axis, these lines tend to increase in
height (representing wave amplitude) until a peak is reached, after which
the line height dim.nishes. Then line height again increases until another
peak is reached and then again diminishes. Such a peak is interpreted as
an indication that the associated price movement contains a wave with a
period about equal to the period of the line at the peak.

 

© To interpret a price-movement Periodogram:
1. Start at the long-period end of the Periodogram.
2. Tabulate the period of each indication of a hump, or peak, that is
present.

3. Designate by appropriate comments those peaks that are most
obvious and unambiguous.

4, Eliminate the non-meaningful periods by comparing with the nominal
model and by using the Principle of Harmonicity.

© Refer now to figure 3-11. At the top of this figure is a Periodogram depic-
ting a set of pure, idealized waves. You will immediately recognize that
Periodogram lines are present corresponding to the 10-, 20-, 40-, and 80-
day wave components of the nominal model.

@ Now direct your attention to the lower part of figure 3-11. This is how
the same segment of a computer-generated price-movement Periodogram

Page 21am

 


ro WW I ] Ty } IIT]| SERUIZES

would look if the nominal model were active at the time and the Principle

of Proportionality were strictly observed.

© This is an idealization that is seldom approached in actual Periodograms,
but it is useful as an introduction to Periodogram interpretation.

© Note that in both cases the vertical (or amplitude) scale is not included. For
price-movement Periodograms it is the periods at which the peaks are
formed by the vertical lines that are important, hence the amplitude scale
is implied but not included in the plot.

@ The Price-movement Periodogram of figure 3-11 would be interpreted as
shown in the following tabulation:

and each peak centers exactly on a period of the nominal model. Thus there
is no need in this example to call upon other interpretive aids. The mean-
ing of the Periodogram is completely clear.

@ Now turn to figure 3-12 where increasingly complex vaiations from an
ideal price-movement Periodogram are shown.

tabulate all the indications of amplitude peaks, starting with the one of
longest period.

© Here it is seen that the conclusion from the Periodogram is that at this time
for this equity all wave periods are running somewhat shorter than those of
the nominal model. This may or may not be borne out in fact, but it is one
more information element to be taken into account when forming the best,
estimates of wave periods for the current cyclic model.

 

© Notice also that neither proportionality nor harmonicity is precisely indi-
cated, although the discrepancies are not great in this case.

© Another price-movement Periodogram is provided in figure 3-13. Complete
the Periodogram analysis tabulation below the plot on this figure as an
exercise.

© You should have little difficulty in arriving at the conclusion that the Period-
ogram shown is giving evidence of waves of periods 66, 32, and 14 days
as part of a current cyclic model. These correspond to the 80-, 40-, and 20-
day components of the nominal model and would be labeled “strong”,
“strong”, and “‘very strong”. In this case, this is an actual Periodogram
produced by computer for the stock Research-Cottrell and based on
daily data as of 9 March 1973.

© Not all price-movement Periodograms will be as simple to interpret as this
one, and not all will give as much useful information at any one time. How-
ever, the purpose at the moment is just to introduce you to the concept of
the Periodogram. Unusual cases and the ways in which even more infor-
mation can be extracted will be covered in later lessons.

© 4 tabulation relating nominal-model periods with Periodogram indica-
tions and containing comments on relative strength is called an initial
eyelic model. The initial cyclic model is the means by which basic cyclic
concepts and the useful characteristics of a Periodogram are brought to
bear on the task of phasing analysis.

SOME FEATURES OF CYCLITEC STOCK AND
COMMODITY CHARTS
© The bottom portion of each Cyclitee chart contains a Periodogram indi-

cating the wave components that are probably present in the price-bar

plot. (Please refer to the typical Cyclitee Services chart shown in figure

3-14 as you read the following discussion.)

   

@ The Periodogram and the price-bar plot are related in that they both
derive from the same raw data. But they are completely unrelated from
a graphic standpoint, being plotted on entirely different sets of axes
having completely different meanings.

© The horizontal axis of the Periodogram represents wave period; the
horizontal axis of the price-bar plot represents calendar time. The vertical
axis of the Periodogram represents wave amplitude; the vertical axis of the
price-bar plot represents stock or commodity price.

@ The fact that a certain price bar may fall on the same vertical grid line
as one of the Periodogram lines below it does not in any way imply a
relationship between them.

 

@ Just above the Periodogram there is a row of numbers across the chart.
These numbers, marking every tenth grid line, serve as a time scale for
the chart. This time scale is convenient for measuring wave period sam-
ples marked on strips of scratch paper. It will also be used throughout
the training course when it is desirable to refer to specific time locations.

 

© A volatility index appears in the upper right corner of each Cyclitee
chart, This index is a measure of the relative rapidity with which prices

for the equity being plotted tend to change.
© On daily Cyclitec charts the sequence of price bars has gaps, ov missing
price bars, for all days on which the auction market for that equity was

not open for trading. These gaps are left because cyclic principles operate
alendar time, without regard for whether the market is open or closed.

Failure to provide a time space for the days on which a market is closed
distortion of the wave information, particularly for the

 

 

would resul
shorter-period waves.

© All Cyclitec charts contain a Dominancy Envelope, which is a pait of
smooth dotted curves on the upper and lower sides of the price movement.

Page 29-———

 


 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 
 
 
 

SOME FEATURES OF CYCLITEC STOCK AND
COMMODITY CHARTS continued
This envelope encloses most of the price bars within a constant-height
channel. It contains within it the wave movements in the price action
that are most visually evident, that dominate the visual appearance of the

price plot.

  

© The Dominancy Envelope has a centerline that is plotted as a solid line.
‘This centerline tends to represent the trend underlying the dominant
price wave for each chart.

@ The Dominancy Envelope serves as a bridge between the initial cyclic
model and actual price movement. Its function in this capacity will be
discussed in a following section.

© Please examine carefully the typical Cyclitee Services daily price chart
shown in figure 3-14 in order to completely familiarize yourself with the
items just discussed:

a. The Periodogram

b, The Dominancy Envelope

c. The centerline of the Dominancy Envelope

d. The missing price bars on weekends and holidays
e. The volatility index.

@ When you have finished your study of figure 3-14,

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD’s IN PAST TIME

© FLD’s may be used in past time to aid in the location of price lows or
highs associated with suspected wave troughs or crests.

 

Ml LESSON

© For this FLD application, a highly accurate estimate of wave period is
not necessary. Instead, whatever is the best available estimate of wave
period is used. This may be a Periodogram indication, the nominal model
period, or even a combination of these (formed by averaging the two).

@ Because the wave period estimate is known to be inaccurate, the FLD
number used does not need to be extremely accurate but is simply one-
half of the estimated wave period. There is no need to add one or to
round this number to a whole number for this usage.

   

@ The FLD number is laid out on a strip of scratch paper using the time
scale on the Cyclitec chart as a measure.

© The left-hand mark on the strip is passed roughly through price aver-
ages with the strip kept essentially horizontal. Minor fluctuations of the
price averages are not important in this application. The eye tracks the
movement of the right-hand mark until it is clear that price intersection
would have occurred if the FLD had been drawn.

@ A light mark is then placed on the chart approximately at the time lo-
cation of the indicated break

© The time span on the paper strip is then used to measure into the past the
time interval during which a price low or high corresponding to the sus-
pected wave trough or crest is likely to have occurred.

@ Please turn to figure 3-15 for an example of this process. Here it is noticed
that the Periodogram indication related to the nominal 80-day wave is
76 days. It is a reasonable assumption that the average of the nominal-
model and Periodogram estimates of period is a better criteria than either
alone because no other evidence is available tending to favor one over
the other. (Note: Such an assumption is only valid until further evidence
is at hand, as from a phasing analysis.)

© The average of 76 and 80 days is'78 days (obtained by adding 76 and
80 and then dividing by 2),

ge 52


 

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD’‘s IN PAST TIME continued

@ The tentative FLD number derived from this period estimate for the
purpose of trough or crest location is just one-half of 78 days, or 39 days.

 

© In figure 3-15, four consecutive positions of a paper strip are shown,
with the time between arrows on the strip being 39 days. The left-hand
dashed line shows the path that the left-hand arrow on the strip took as,
the strip was moved along the general path of the price averages. As the
paper strip was moved from position (1) to position (2) to position (3) to
position (4), the right-hand dashed line shows the path that the right-
hand arrow traversed.

© This right-hand dashed line would not ordinarily be drawn. (It was shown
in this figure only for purposes of illustration.) Instead, it would simply
have been noted by eye that a downward price break took place between
strip positions (3) and (4). Then backtracking with the strip would have
permitted a mark at the approximate price-break time location (some-
where between grid lines 105 and 110).

 

@ Because this was a downward break, the highest price high in the (ap-
proximately) 40 weeks preceding the region of the break would have a
high likelihood of being associated with a crest of the (nominal) 80-day
wave.

 

@ Exercise: Use the same 78-day initial period estimate to provide evidence
that the price lows at grid lines 42 and 123 are very likely associated with
two consecutive troughs of the 80-day wave.

© Exercise: Repeat the above exercise using the Periodogram estimate of
76 days as the basis for the same two trough locations.

© Exercise: repeat the above exercise using the nominal-model estimate of
80 days as the basis for the same two trough locations.

@ What conclusion do you draw from these exercises? Why can you con-
clude that the two trough locations found are consecutive troughs of the
80-day wave?

© Complete the exercises, working on the chart of figure 3-15, and an-
swer the above two questions to the best of your ability before reading
the following discussion,

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD’s IN PAST TIME continued

@ Because all three period estimates confirm the same time locations for
the wave troughs, it can be concluded that this FLD application is reas-
onably tolerant of error in the period estimate. This is the basis for the
ability of FLD’s to start from a rough (and probably incorrect) period
estimate and provide useful evidence of wave troughs. These wave troughs
then provide period samples which can be averaged to obtain a refined
(more accurate) estimate of period.

 

‘The two troughs found are considered to be consecutive troughs on the
basis of the following points of evidence:

1. Only one FLD-confirmed crest is located between the two troughs.

2. The two troughs are located the expected time distance apart (in
this case 81 days).

3. In relation to the price action the FLD action across the time span
between the two troughs behaves in the one-half-period-phase-
difference manner to be expected if one and only one cycle of the
wave in question exists between the troughs. (Refer to figure 2-15
in lesson two.)

@ This FLD application is very important. If you experienced any difficulty
in locating these two troughs on your own, you should reset your tape
unit to the start of the section-seven discussion and study both recording
and text until the method presented is clear to you,

INTRODUCTION TO PHASING ANALYSIS

@ An accurate current cyclic model is the basis for all trading and invest-
ment decisions.

© The current cyclic model is derived in several steps. First, an initial cyclic
model is formed using basic cyclic concepts and the Periodogram.

The initial cyclic model is then used as a guide in the execution of a
phasing analysis, which identifies the time locations of wave troughs.

Finally, information is extracted from the phasing analysis to form the
current cyclic model, which is a tabulation of the best possible estimate
of the current period and phase of each wave in the initial cyclic model.

 

© Phasing analysis is of key importance in setting up the current cyclic
model. Phasing analysis is not a mechanical process, although useful
procedural guidelines are available. The technique of phasing analysis
consists of using basic cyclic concepts, computer-generated aids, and
graphical methods rooted in cyclic concepts to compile evidence that
leads to conclusions regarding the time location of wave troughs. The
art of phasing analysis lies in the individual's ability to apply cyclic con-
cepts and methods in such a way as to extract the most meaningful wave
evidence from price data.

  

© Phasing analysis is always started with the longest chart on hand for the
equity of interest. In the case of Cyclitee charts, this would be the weekly
chart for stocks and commodities, and the monthly chart for the Dow
Jones Industrial Average.

   

© Similarly, for a given chart, phasing analysis
the longer period waves.

always started with one of

@ Phasing analysis consists of several types of activities:

1, Phasing analysis entry, in which trough positions are located for
one of the longer waves

2. Identification of harmonious, synchronous, and non-ambiguous
troughs for all shorter waves

3. Resolution of ambiguities.
