INTRODUCTION TO PHASING ANALYSIS continued
© The results of a phasing analysis are the time locations of all troughs
of all waves of the cyclic model.

© Phasing analysis results are recorded directly on the chart being phased
in the form of several horizontal rows of diamond-shaped marks.

© Each diamond has its vertical points located on the time position esti-
mated for a wave trough.

@ Each row corresponds to one wave in the cyclic model, with the topmost
row representing the longest wave under consideration.

© Successively lower rows represent successively shorter waves.

@ A phasing analysis entry can be made in several ways. The purpose is to
establish a time position for one or more troughs of one of the longest
waves identifiable on a given chart (the longer the better).

© Once an entry is made by identifying one or more troughs of a long wave,
then phasing diamonds for the next shorter wave are placed at the same
time positions. This can be done because the Principles of Harmonicity
and Synchronicity tell us that if the trough positions of a given wave are
known then it follows that all shorter waves have troughs at the same

¢ locations, It is important to note that the opposite is not true. That

is, if the trough locations of a given wave are known, it does nor follow

that any /onger wave necessarily has a trough position at any one of these

same times.

 

© ‘The most important principle of phasing analysis is this: Always phase
the most well-defined situations first — and pass by those situations that
are not cleat. In this way evidence continues to build based on the strong
indications. And the more such evidence builds, the more easily the basic
cyclic principles can be used to resolve the ambiguous situations initially
passed by.

ENTERING A PHASING ANALYSIS

©@ To enter a phasing analysis means to find evidence in price movement
of one or more troughs of a wave close to the longest wave the chart is
capable of displaying.

© A number of methods for phasing analysis entry are possible. In some
situations, several entry techniques may be needed in order to supply sat-
isfactory evidence of the desired wave troughs.

© The most generally useful phasing analysis entry employs the character-
istics of the Dominancy Envelope and its centerline. These function as
wave-period dividers.

@ The Dominancy Envelope contains a longest wave, called the dominant
component or dominant wave, summed together with all shorter waves in
price movement.

© The Dominancy Envelope centerline consists of the next longer wave
than the dominant wave, called the pre-dominant wave, summed together
with all longer waves in price movement.

© The two parts into which price movement is so divided are each true
composite waves, but each is greatly simplified by comparison with the
total composite wave that is price movement. This effect makes visual
wave identification in each part simpler than in total price movement
itself.

© The composite wave contained within the Dominancy Envelope is useful

for separating out the shorter waves.

 

© The Dominancy Envelope centerline is useful for separating out the long-
er waves.

© The oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline are smooth, easy
to follow with the eye, and strongly tend to fluctuate in sympathy with
the pre-dominant wave component.

© These oscillations can be observed and rough measurements taken from
low to low and high to high. If these period measurements compare reas-
onably well with expectations from the initial cyclic model, then the most
significant price lows near the centerline lows are very likely to be

Page 38

 


    
  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 

ENTERING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

associated with troughs of the pre-dominant wave. These characteristics
make the Dominaney Envelope centerline ideally suited as a means of
entering a phasing analysis.

© Other phasing analysis entry methods include use of FLD’s in past time
to give evidence of long-wave troughs; direct comparison of initial cyclic
model period estimates with price movement; and use of knowledge of
the longest wave for which a full cycle can be displayed on a given chart.
In this latter method, the probabilities favor that the lowest price low on
the chart is associated with a trough of the longest wave that the chart
can display.

© In this lesson, emphasis will be placed on the use of the Dominancy Envel-
ope centerline as the means of entry into a phasing analysis.

@ Please refer back to figure 3-14 in section six. This is a Cyclitec daily
chart for Loews Corporation (ticker symbol LTR) as of Friday, the 25th
of August, 1972.

© Note the time scale printed on the chart. This is the row of numbers
from 10 to 200 just above the Periodogram. We will be referring to vari-
ous price bars on the chart by their grid line number taken from this
time scale. For example, the price bar for the date of the chart is the
last price bar to the right, located at grid line number 179.

 

© To demonstrate the Dominancy Envelope centerline method of entry to
phasing analysis, an initial cyclic model is needed. Construct this model
for the chart of figure 3-14 as an exercise using the transaction analysis,
form of figure 3-16.

© Now note the Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuations of figure 3-14.
Starting at the left, a first indication of a centerline low is seen at about
grid line 20. The next one to the right is seen at grid line 71 or 72. A third
low shows at grid line 121. There may be another low forming about
grid line 165, but since this is not a certainty as yet, this indication should
not be counted on, (Actually, the evidence is present on this chart that this
latter low has indeed occurred. The methods for resolving such situations
will be covered in subsequent lessons.)

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS

@ In entering a phasing analysis, attention is concentrated on the fluctu-
ations of the composite wave represented by the Dominancy Envelope
centerline.

@ In extending a phasing analysis, attention is concentrated on the price
movement within the Dominancy Envelope and fluctuating about the
Dominancy Envelope centerline.

@ The function of the Dominancy Envelope and its centerline as a wave-
period divider should be thoroughly understood and kept in mind
throughout the phasing process. Reference to a price movement Period-
‘ogram can help in this understanding.

©@ Figure 3-21 is such a Periodogram. The vertical dashed line represents a
design characteristic of the computer program which produces the Dom-
inancy Envelope. For the design indicated in this figure, waves repre-
sented by Periodogram peaks A and B, plus all shorter price waves not
shown by the Periodogram, constitute the part of price movement con-
tained in the Dominancy Envelope.

 

 

 

Similarly, waves indicated by Periodogram peaks C and D, plus all long-
er price waves not shown by the Periodogram, constitute the part of
price movement represented by the Dominancy Envelope centerline.

© From this figure it is readily seen that the Dominancy Envelope center-
line is the trend underlying the longest wave contained by the Domin-
ancy Envelope (in this case, wave B).

  

© After entering a phasing analysis by identifying a suitably long wave,
the phasing analysis extension takes place in four steps:

1. Next-shorter wave diamonds are automatically placed at the same
time locations as the diamonds for the wave just previously phased.
‘The justification for this step is the combined effect of the Principles
of Synchronicity and Harmonicity.

2. The price movement between each set of consecutive troughs of
the next longer wave is searched for visual evidence of a trough ap-
proximately midway between. This process can be assisted by direct
comparison with initial cyclic model estimates. Phasing diamonds
are placed to mark any troughs identified.

$$ Prige 50

  


CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  

  

ee trie

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

As in the preceding step, the justification is the combined effect of
the Principles of Synchronicity and Harmonicity.

3. Price movement is explored to the left and to the right of identified
next-longer wave troughs for which no consecutive diamond is avail-
able in the direction explored. Phasing diamonds are placed where
there is visual evidence of a trough at approximately the expected
period. The period estimates of the initial cyclic model augmented
by the period samples gained in step 2 are used as a guide.

4. When no further troughs of the next longer wave are available to
work from, direct comparison of the expected period is made against
raw price data. All period samples obtained up to this point are
used in the comparison.

© Whenever direct comparison is called for, it is made using a measuring
strip constructed from scratch paper as shown below.

—— Prtied Sampres

 

  

Reference. Nominal Model Periodogram
Mark Estimate Estimate

© In all steps, the ambiguous trough locations are left for resolution until
more evidence is added by phasing successively shorter waves.

© The procedure just described uses ordered steps so that those troughs for
which the most decisive evidence is available are identified early in the
procedure. The troughs located in this way then provide period samples
which increase the validity of subsequent selections.

© The application of cyclic concepts and methods to phasing analysis is best
learned by doing. In this lesson you will be guided through a phasing
analysis for the copper example of lesson number one. Following lessons
will provide multiple opportunities for the finer points of phasing anal-
ysis to be learned.

igure 3-23 is a weekly chart for copper as of 10 November 1972. As an
exercise, generate the weekly initial cyclic model in the manner you have

Now enter the phasing analysis by phasing the pre-dominant component.

Complete this exercise before referring to the following discussion or
to figure 3-24 where the correct results are shown,

Setting Up The Initial Cyclic Model Weekly]:

© Only one decision is required in setting up this initial cyclic model, and
that is whether to accept the 44-week or the 58-week Periodogram indica-
tion as a representation of the 9-month wave. The cyclic arguments, pro
and con, would go as follows:

Favoring the 58-week peak
1. A tendency is shown in the Periodogram for all waves to be consid-
erably longer than nominal expectations (12 weeks versus 10 weeks;
28 weeks versus 20 weeks).
2. The 12-week and 28-week peaks establish a ratio near 2 to 1, leading
to an expectation for the next longer wave to be 56 weeks (twice 28).
This is closer to 58 weeks than to 44 weeks.

 

3. The Periodogram shows normal strength for the 58-week peak and
weak strength for the 44-week peak (as noted in the comments
column).

Favoring the 44-week peak

1. The Periodogram indication for the 18-month wave is 79 weeks.
This would lead to an expectation of one-half of this, or 39.5 weeks,
for the next shorter wave. This is closer to 44 weeks than to 58
weeks.

Page 52,

 

 


  

  

Page 53,

ee tiem

COURSE©

  
   

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued
2. The 44-week Periodogram peak is closer to nominal (39 weeks) than
is 58 weeks.
‘The evidence would slightly favor retention of the 58-week Periodogram
estimate. However, a wide tolerance for error is acceptable in the initial
cyclic model. You would find that if you retained the 44-week estimate
instead, the final phasing analysis results would be the same.

 

@ In fact, there wotild be nothing wrong with accepting the entire range
from 39 weeks (nominal) to 58 weeks as the initial estimate of the (nom-
inal) 9-month wave period.

© This situation is discussed at some length here, not because of its impor-
tance in this particular case, but because it is a good example of how
cyclic-concept arguments can be built up for or against a given hypoth-
esis. This procedure is one that you should become accustomed to. It
amounts to building a case by compiling bits of evidence from several
sources in order to reach a conclusion or to resolve an ambiguity.

‘cussion that follows pertains to entering the phasing analysis by

 

@ The

© Observation of the fluctuations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline
identifies two clear-cut lows at grid lines 82 and 128. There is also evi-

32.

@ Highs of the Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuations can be seen at
grid lines 40, 100, and 147 (approximately).

© Measurements from low to low and high to high result in a set of period
estimates ranging from 46 to 60 weeks. Comparison of this range with
the initial cyclic model results in the conclusion that the Dominancy
Envelope centerline is oscillating in sympathy with the (nominal) 9-month
wave.

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

© The measuring strip at A of figure 3-24 is an indication of how well the
initial cyclic model served as a guide in phasing the pre-dominant wave

Extending the Phasing Analysis (Phasing the 20-28 week wave):

@ The pre-dominant wave was phased during the entry to phasing analysis
and was found to be the current representation of the nominal 9-month
wave. Now you should attempt to extend the analysis by phasing the next
shorter wave of the initial cyclic model, the 20-28 week wave. Do this
using the four steps previously described before referring to the follow-
ing discussion or to the correct results shown in figure 3-25.

© The first step in phasing the 20-28 week wave is to locate phasing dia
monds for this wave directly under the diamonds placed for the next
longer wave (in this case the pre-dominant component). This places
diamonds, as shown hollow in figure 3-25, at grid lines 84 and 129.

© In the second step, evidence of a 20-28 week wave trough is sought half-
way between each set of consecutive troughs of the next longer wave. In
this case only one such set exists; namely, the pre-dominant wave dia-
monds at 84 and 129. At A in figure 3-25 a measuring st is shown in
the proper exploratory position and with the period estimates of the
initial cyclic model marked on it.

 

© The most significant price movement between grid lines 84 and 129 does
indeed fall in the anticipated time region. However, either of two price
lows, located at grid lines 104 and 108, could be selected as the proper
one. If these had been widely separated, the situation would have been
left for later resolution. But because they are quite close together, they
can be marked with X's (as possible) in order to establish rough period
samples early in the analysis.

@ Since this exhausts the available consecutive trough sets, step 3 is under-

@ Search to the right of the diamond at 129 reveals two widely separated

then adds reassurance that reasonable trough locations have been found
for this wave.
@ Note: It could be argued that the placement of a diamond for this wave
at grid line number 3 is risky because of the proximity of the
left edge of the chart. In such cases it is always possible that a
more promising potential trough location could be noted to the
left of 3 if several more weeks of data were displayed.

The placement of a diamond at 3 could be delayed for this reason,
although the placement at 33 would still be valid. If the placement
at 3 were to be delayed, phasing of the shorter waves would eventu-

COMPLETING A PHASING ANALYSIS

@ The skill in phasing analysis lies mostly in the entry and extension por-
tions of the process. Subjective judgements are required here which are
based on familiarity with the basic cyclic concepts and aided by com-
puter-generated graphics.

 

The process of completion is, by contrast, nearly mechanical. It involves

direct application of the Principle of Harmonicity and is spoken of as
filling the harmonic gaps.

 

© Before this procedure can be started, the shortest wave phased in the

extension process must have the locations of all its troughs indicated by
diamonds.

© Usually there will only be a few phasing gaps left for this shortest wave,
and these will be due to the rule that only the more positively evidenced
trough locations be established during the analysis extension. Such gaps
can now be filled, arbitrarily if necessary.

© The justification for this action is the fact that by the time this point in
the analysis is reached, there is a large body of evidence that this wave
is present and active in price movement. Further, it is a known charac-

teristic of complex composite price waves that occasional trough loca-
tions will be obscured.

© However, if numerous gaps exist for this shortest wave, the analysis should
be extended to a still shorter wave in order to amass further evidence.
Phasing analysis is usually extended only to the 10-week wave for weekly
charts or the 10-day wave for daily charts. But there are times when ex-
tension must include the 5-week or 5-day wave in order to satisfy the
information needs of the longer waves.

© On those infrequent occasions when even the S-week or S-day wave can-
not be completely phased, the chart analysis need not necessarily be
abandoned. But such situations will be covered in a later lesson.

© The process of filling the harmonic gaps is begun under conditions such
that there is one shortest wave that is fully phased and a series of longer
waves that are partially phased.

© Working with successively longer waves, we use the Principles of Har-
monicity and Synchronicity to fill phasing gaps on the basis that every

 

 

Page 64

  


  

  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 

COMPLETING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued
other phasing diamond of a given wave must coincide with a phasing
diamond of the next longer wave.

 

LESSON i

© This basis is the general rule. And as with all rules, there are exceptions.
However, these exceptions will be dealt with in lessons to follow.

© Please return to figure 3-23 and complete the phasing analysis of this,
example by applying the method just described. After you have finished,
consult the following discussion and figure 3-27 for comparison with your
own results.

© The first step in the completion process is to look for gaps in the phas-
ing of the 10-12 week wave. It is seen that four of the spacings between
diamonds appear long by comparison with the majority of period samples
available, These apparent gaps are:

 

1. Grid line 3to 19
2. Grid line 33to 51
3. Grid line 84 to 108
4. Grid line 108 to 122

Let us discuss them in order.

© The first question is: Should another phasing diamond be located be-
tween grid lines 3 and 19? To answer this question it should first be
noted that the quality of the 20-28 week period sample between grid lines
3 and 33 is high. Further, as things now stand there are two samples of
the 10-12 week wave period contained within this single cycle of the 20-28
week wave. This satisfies the requirements of harmonicity; and placement
of a new diamond would force complete reappraisal of the indicated 20-28
week phasing, which appears to be normal.

On this evidence, the decision should be made that the gap between grid
lines 3 and 19 should not be altered.

© Next, consider the gap between grid lines 33 and S1. Four 10-12 week
period samples are now shown between the two 20-28 week diamonds at
33 and 84, leading to the strong suspicion that a 20-28 week diamond
is missing at 62. If a new 10-12 week diamond were placed between 33

and 51, there would be no way that harmonicity could be satisfied with
respect to the 20-28 week wave.

Once again it is concluded that this gap is natural and should not be
filled.

@ Now we come to the gap between grid lines 84 and 108. The 10-12 week

diamonds at 108, 122, and 129 indicate two cycles of this wave, leading
to the strong suspicion that the X mark at 108 should be associated with
a 20-28 week diamond. Since the next diamond to the left for this 20-28
week wave is at 84, harmonicity would require a trough of the 10-12 week
wave between 84 and 108.

‘Thus, the conclusion would be reached that this is a phasing gap that
must be filled.

To do so, select any price dip as close to the center of the space between
84 and 108 as possible and place a 10-12 week diamond at that time
position. In the figure, the diamond is placed at grid line 97.

@ The long space between 108 and 122 is considered next.

‘The reasoning of the last paragraph indicates that only two cycles of the
10-12 week wave should occur between 108 and 129. Further. the cycle of
this wave between 122 and 129 appears to be unusually short, which
would obviously leave the cycle between 108 and 122 unusually long.

The conclusion is that no additional 10-12 week diamond can be added
without disrupting the entire phasing analysis. One other conclusion that
might be drawn is that the actual wave trough probably took place at
about grid line 119 instead of at 122 at best indicated by price movement.

@ These reasoning processes leave the impression that, to the best of our

ability, a fully phased “shortest” wave has been attained and that phasing
of a still shorter wave would not provide any useful additional informa-
tion. Hence the criterion is met for starting the process of filling the har-
monic gaps.

@ This process is begun with the wave next longer than the fully phased one,

or the 20-28 week wave in this example. Working from the left, a dia-
mond for this wave is placed at 62. This placement provides for two cycles

are shown in figure 3-27 for reference and study. The hollow diamonds
are the ones that have been added in the overall process of gap-filling.

@ Observe that on this figure diamonds have also been placed for the (nom-
inal) 18 month wave. A discussion of how this was accomplished follows:

© Recall that a weekly chart shows 180 weeks of data and therefore will
always contain at least one (and perhaps two) nests-of-lows which include
a trough of the 18-month wave. A good general rule of thumb is that the
lowest price low on such a chart is probably associated with an 18-month
trough (although this does not have to be the case).

© In this example, this rule of thumb would lead to the initial suspicion
that the price low at 84 is associated with an 18-month trough.

© This suspicion is confirmed by use of an FLD in past time. The FLD
number could be based on the nominal wave period, the Periodogram
estimate from the initial cyclic model, or an average of these estimates.
However, a more accurate estimate can be formed at this stage of the
phasing analysis by averaging the samples now available for the (nominal)
9-month wave and multiplying by two. This would result in an FLD num-
ber of 47.3 (You should go through this process and arrive at this FLD
number for yourself as an exercise.)
‘The results of this test are shown in figure 3-27. A price high associated

with the 18-month wave is located at 41, and the following 18-month
trough is indicated to be at 84 — as previously suspected.

On this first attempt at the complete phasing analysis of a chart, you
have probably experienced considerable difficulty and been left with a
number of questions in your mind.

 

This is to be expected. The purpose of this lesson is to expose you to the
overall concepts needed in phasing analysis and to provide a first degree

of involvement only.

Successive lessons will provide additional experience in phasing analysis
and additional examples of cyclic logic which result in extraction of new
kinds of information. As you progress through these lessons, the process
of phasing analysis will become first comfortable, then instinctive with
you

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS

© The process of phasing analysis consists of an entry, several steps of
extension, and completion.

© Entry can be effected in several ways
1, Through the oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline
2. By use of FLD’s in past time

3. By direct comparison of initial cyclic model expectations to price
movement.

@ These entry methods can be combined, thus serving as checks on each
other.

© Extension takes place in four steps:

 

1. Direct application of the Principle of Synchronicity

2. Application of the Principle of Harmonicity and of initial cyclic
model estimates to all sets of consecutive troughs of the next longer
wave

3. Direct comparison of initial cyclic model estimates, augmented by
period samples, to price data on either side of trough diamonds of
the next longer wave

4, Direct comparison of initial cyclic model estimates, augmented by
period samples, to the most visually obvious price oscillations.

© These steps are accomplished in the order stated for each extension of
the phasing analysis to a next shorter wave.

© Completion is started by completely phasing a “shortest” wave. The
phasing gaps for this wave are filled by cyclic logic or by an additional
step of extension (to a still shorter wave). The second step in completion
is called filling the harmonic gaps and is a direct application of the Prin-
ciple of Harmonicity. The filling of harmonic gaps proceeds from shorter
to successively longer waves.

   

@ The process of phasing analysis is an inexact art. This is so because it is

physically impossible to have perfect price movement information and
wave phase information simultaneously.

ge 70.

 


—— Page 71.

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS continued
Part of the art of phasing analysis lies in arriving at best estimates in a
workmanlike manner without excessive concern about the absolute cor-
rectness of the results.

 

@ In a phasing analysis it is vitally important to account for each consecu-
tive wave in the nominal model (making due allowance for the Prin-
ciple of Variation), even if a given wave appears to be weak or inactive
at the time.

@ The time locations of wave troughs and associated price lows do not nec-
essarily coincide. The same is true of the time locations of wave crests
and associated price highs.

 

© This time difference phenomenon is called time translation.

© Time translation, when applied to wave troughs versus price lows, is
called low translation.

© Time translation, when applied to wave crests versus price highs, is called
high translation.

 

@ Knowledge of the effects of time translation can be very useful in the
processes of phasing analysis.

@ The time-translation effect is best understood in relation to ideal price
waves, Please turn back to figure 3-10 in section 4 where three ideal

waves are summed to a composite wave.

©@ In this exercise you were asked to place phasing diamonds at the time
locations of the price wave troughs. In this instance, it was possible for
you to do this exactly, because you knew in advance precisely what waves
formed the composite wave.

@ Ina phasing analysis the situation is reversed. Here you have before you
the composite wave (price) and are required to deduce the trough locations
of constituent waves.

© Ifyou were doing such a phasing analysis on the composite wave of
figure 3-10, you would incorrectly locate a phasing diamond at grid line
17 instead of 16. Similarly, you would incorrectly place a diamond at grid
line 31 instead of 32. That is, you would incorrectly place these diamonds
if you were unaware of the effects of time translation.

“sup ss0ug s19pHa EL6I @ WBKIOD

‘All Rights Reserved

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 

 

     

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS continued

© The cause of time translation is underlying trend. When the trend under-
lying a wave is down, low translation is to the right and high translation
is to the left. When the trend underlying a wave is up, low translation is
to the left and high translation is to the right.

 

| LESSON

 

@ Examples of both low and high translation are present in the composite
wave of figure 3-10. You should study this figure until you have identified
all the occurrences of time translation and feel that you have a firm grasp
of the concept.

 

igure 3-28 is an excellent example of high translation in actual price
data. At the base of the figure the phasing diamonds for a price wave

are shown, and the wave component so isolated is sketched in. It is clear
in this instance how this wave moved prices both up and down. But notice
how this movement vas combined with trend underlying the wave to dis-
tort the time position of the price highs associated with this wave.

  

xercise: Return to the phasing processes shown in figures 3-24 through
3-26 and identify the cases where you placed phasing diamonds in one lo-
cation and they were shown in another in the discussion and comparison
figures. Resolve in your own mind how knowledge of the time-translation
effect was applied to these situations.

@ With these new ideas and concepts freshly in mind, you should now
experience another example of phasing analysis. Figure 3-29 is a Cyclitee
daily chart of copper as of 24 November 1972, Using the transaction
analysis form of figure 3-22, construct an initial cyclic model for this
chart. Then perform a phasing analysis entry by phasing the pre-dom-
inant wave. Do this as best you can before referring to the following dis-
cussion or to the correct results shown in figure 3-30.

 

© The initial cyclic model presents no ambiguities in this case, but it does
serve notice that current wave periods are running considerably longer
than nominal, It will be remembered that this was also the case in the
weekly chart of copper. That this sheuld be true for both the daily and
the weekly analysis is an expectation based on the Principle of Harmon-
icity and lends credence to the conclusion.

  

 

© In entry to the phasing analysis, attention is first devoted to that part

of the price action represented by the Dominancy Envelope centerline.
Oscillation lows are noted at grid lines 28, 63, and 129. Oscillation highs
are seen at grid lines 43, 93, and 144, (See arrows at centerline on figure
3:30.)

© Strip measurements from low to low and high to high (shown at A of
figure 3-30) compared with Periodogram shape (as shown by arrows
leading from measuring strip to Periodogram scale) tell us that the pre-
dominant wave producing these centerline oscillations is the 40-50 day
wave of the initial eyclic model.

 

 

@ Of the three Dominancy Envelope centerline lows identified, only the
one at grid line 28 has unambiguous price lows in the vicinity. Accord-
ingly, one diamond placement for the pre-dominant wave is made at grid
line 28. The next centerline low to the right (at 63) could be associ-
ated with one of the price lows at $3, 67, or 80. This is too wide a spread
to permit marking with X's as possibles, so resolution is left for later
in the analysis. A similar situation occurs at the centerline low at 129.
Possibly associated price lows are at 112, 119, 126, and 136. Again the
spread is too wide to be meaningful, and a diamond cannot be placed
with confidence.

© The phasing analysis entry could be terminated at this point with only
a single diamond placed. However, in order to introduce a useful new
idea, a slightly difterent tack will be taken. Following the oscillations of
the Dominancy Envelope to the far right (and confirming impressions
gained by use of the measuring strip), it is seen that at current time the
pre-dominant wave is expected to be in the vicinity of a trough. This
means that the price high at 142 is very probably associated with a wave
crest of the pre-dominant wave. And the price high at 99 is clearly assoe-
iated with the centerline high at 93 and therefore with a pre-dominant
wave crest

‘These price highs then, with a high degree of confidence, form the basis
for a valid downtrend line based on the pre-dominant wave.

Figure 3-30 shows this critical VTL, and we see that it was broken in the
upward direction by prices on the date of the chart. The meaning of
such a break is that the price low at 172 is very likely associated with

a trough of the wave next longer than the pre-dominant wave. The com-

assure a trough of the pre-dominant wave at this time also.

@ By this line of cyclic logic, two diamonds are placeable at 172, one for
the pre-dominant wave and one for the next longer wave (the 80-day
wave of the initial cyclic model).

© Thus it is seen that the processes of phasing analysis can be augmented
by cyclic theory and methods in a variety of ways.

© Now, extend the phasing analysis to include the 20-28 day wave of the
initial cyclic model. Do this as best you can before checking your results
with the discussion that follows and with figure 3-31.

© The first step of the extension is to apply the Principle of Synchronicity
directly, resulting in 20-28 day diamonds at 29 and at 172.

© No second step is possible in this extension because of the lack of any
sets of consecutive troughs of the 40-50 day wave.

© A third-step measuring strip search to the left and to the right of the
40-50 day diamond at 29 results in 20-28 day diamond placements at 7
and 53. A search to the right of the diamond just placed at 53 places
a new 20-28 day diamond at 80. (This last diamond is actually a step
four placement and could be delayed if desired until step four is formally
undertaken.) This in turn, by the Principle of Harmonicity, resolves the
ambiguity surrounding the 40-50 day trough associated with the Domin-
ancy Envelope centerline low at 63 so that a 40-50 day diamond also may
be placed at 80. (This placement of a 40-50 day diamond falls in the cate-
gory of filling the harmonic gaps and could, if desired, be left until the
completion stage of the analysis.)

© A search to the left of the 40-50 day diamond at 172 shows widely spaced
possibles at 136 and 154. There is too much doubt here for diamond
placement.

© ‘Step four of the extension (direct comparison of strip measurements to
price data) does not find any additional unambiguous placements, so the
20-28 day extension effort is considered complete.

© It will be noted from this discussion that several elements of the various
steps of extension and completion were combined out of sequence. This
was done deliberately as an example of how practice in the formal pro-
cedure of phasing analysis builds up skill so that procedural efficiency can
be improved. As your experience grows, you will find yourself looking
ahead to extract information related to phasing more quickly and effec-
tively. However, when doubt exists, you should always fall back to the
formal rules of phasing analysis to establish a solution.

@ Now extend the phasing analysis to include the 10-16 day wave. Do this
as best you can before referring to the following discussion or to figure
3-32.

© First step extension produces diamond placements at grid lines 7, 29,
53, 80, and 172.

© Second step extension places diamonds at 43 and 67. The possible second
step placement between the consecutive 20-28 day diamonds at 7 and 29
is considered too ambiguous at this point in the analysis.

© Third step extension places a diamond at 93 and another at 154. The
placement at 154 enables a step-four search to the left resulting in an-
other placement at 136.

© Additional third step, and then fourth step, extension attempts run into
ambiguity at grid lines 106, 112, and 119. Resolution of the correct dia-
mond placement in this area is best delayed.

© Note that placement of the 10-16 day diamond at 136 also defines another
20-28 day diamond at the same time (by Harmonicity),

© The ambiguities in the gap between grid lines 93 and 136 are severe
enough to warrant another extension before attempting completion. So
extend the analysis to the 5-8 day wave and complete the analysis by
filling the gaps before consulting the following discussion and figure
3-33. (The 8-day estimate for the 5-8 day wave comes in this case from
one-half of the 16-day period rather than directly from a Periodogram
peak.)

© The extension to the 5-8 day wave is straightforward except for one
point that warrants discussion.

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS continued
On a daily chart, a wave trough (and the associated price low) can occur
during a time when the markets are not open (over a weekend, for ex-
ample). This is particularly troublesome when dealing with a wave as
short as S days. When it appears that a wave trough logically falls on a
weekend or holiday, it is quite acceptable to place a diamond for the
associated imaginary price low on the grid line for that weekend day or
holiday. In such cases, diamond placement is made so as to minimize
the variation of the resulting period samples.

   

© Filling of the remaining harmonic gaps proceeds in a straightforward
manner as indicated in figure 3-33.

@ This exercise completes your exposure in this lesson to the process of
phasing analysis. Each step of the process has been explained in elab-
orate detail and you have spent considerable time in the phasing of a
single equity. As your experience grows and your skill increases with
practice, you will find a drastic diminishing of the effort and time in-
volved. Eventually you will find that a complete phasing analysis is
possible in a remarkably short time.

EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING

ANALYSIS

© During construction of a current cyclic model, work proceeds in several
steps:

Hl rr

   

1, Interpretation of the Periodogram
2. Construction of the initial eyclie model
3. Entry, extension, and completion of a phasing analysis

4. Extraction from the phasing analysis of information on which to
base the current cyclic model.

© The phasing analysis also yields estimates of the probable future times
at which various wave troughs are likely to occur.

© Extraction of information from the phasing analysis also takes place in
several steps:

1, Determination of the average of the period samples for each
wave

2. Determination of the best estimate of wave trough locations in
future time

3. Determination of wave phases (the elapsed time since the last trough
of each wave)

4. Determination of wave directions (the current tendency of each
wave to cause prices to rise or fall),

 

© When extracting information from a phasing analysis, we must treat
commodity analyses somewhat differently from those of stocks. This is
due to the fact that commodity futures contracts for a given contract
month do not exist long enough to generate sufficient price data for
weekly analysis purposes.

© Since it is highly desirable to have a means of estimating long wave
periods, commodity weekly charts are generated from price information
that is considered to be suitably similar to that which might be expected
for the contract being charted. This subject will be discussed at length
in subsequent lessons. It is brought up here only in order to warn that

ge 2


  

 

  

LESSON I

—— Page 84

 

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 

 

 

  

 
 

EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING

ANALYSIS continued
phase information extracted from a commodity weekly chart is always to
be considered suspect. This, of course, is not true in the case of weekly
stock charts.

© Now let us see how to go about extracting current-cyclic-model infor-
mation from the phasing analyses of the preceding sections.

 

 

© ‘The first step is to determine the average of the period samples for the
waves phased on both the weekly and the daily charts.

© The easiest way to do this is as follows: Subtract the grid fine number of
the leftmost diamond from the grid line number of the rightmost dia-
mond, and divide the result by the number of period samples lying be-
tween. (It is important to realize that prior to this operation, the wave
must have been completely phased. That is, all phasing diamonds must
have been placed. This will result in the correct sample count for the
averaging process just described.)

@ As an example of this procedure, refer back to figure 3-27 at the end of
section eleven. For the bottom row of diamonds:

The rightmost diamond is at grid line 172
The leftmost diamond is at grid line _3_

The difference is: 169 weeks

‘The number of period samples is: 14

‘Therefore, the average period is: 169-+14=12.0714 weeks.
This result rounds to 12.1 weeks.

 

@ As an exercise in applying this method, please make a tabulation of the
average wave periods from both the weekly and the daily phasing analyses
of copper (figures 3-27 and 3-33). Note that it is often necessary to go
through this procedure for a single wave only, since in most cases phasing
analysis results in precisely harmonic wave- period estimates. As an
example, the next longer wave of the preceding paragraph is found to be
2 times 12.0714 or 24.1428 (which rounds to 24.1 weeks). The resul
period estimate usually results in an accuracy improvement for the longer
waves for which period samples are limited in number. Make your tabu-
lation from figures 3-29 and 3-33 before referring to what follows.

@ Where weekly and daily analyses overlap (as in this case at the nominal
80-day wave), a choice must be made as to which estimate to use. In the

case of commodity analyses, the daily analysis estimate is preferred be-
cause of the nature of the weekly data. In the case of stock analyses, the
choice depends on several factors and will be discussed in a later lesson.

@ Estimates of the time of occurrence of future wave troughs are obtained
as follows:

1. Lay out the average period of the wave on a measuring strip.

2. Lay out the periods of each sample of the wave on the same strip,
measuring from diamond to diamond on the phasing analysis and
laying out each sample from the common reference mark on the
strip.

3. Use the completed measuring strip to measure into future time from
the last phasing diamond on the right.

4, Mark the time position of the period average with a circle, and
extend a horizontal line on either side of the circle to the extreme
period-sample positions on the measuring strip.

© At this point you may have realized that you normally construct such
measuring strips during the phasing process. In practice, it is good pro-
cedure to label the measuring strips as they are created during phas-
ing and then use them again later to lay out the expected trough Io-
cations in future time.

© This process of laying out expected trough locations in future time is
illustrated for the 5.9-day wave at A in figure 3-34. The desirable prac-
tice of labeling the rows of phasing diamonds with the average period is
also illustrated.

@ From figure 3-34 it is seen that the elapsed time since the latest phasing
diamond for all waves from 5.9 to 95.3 days is estimated to be 7 days.
This is the phasing information needed for these waves and is entered in
the current cyclic model.

@ The phase of the still longer waves must be derived from the weekly
analysis. Since this is a commodity chart and such phase information is
therefore suspect, it is best to measure phase from the last well established

© Returning to figure 3-27, we see that the last firm diamond of the 24.1-
week wave is at grid line 150. Since then, 29 weeks have elapsed to the
date of the chart which is 10 November 1972. Another 2 weeks must be
added to bring the estimate up to date as of 24 November, the date of
the daily chart. Thus, the weekly analysis indicates that as of 24 Novem-
ber 1972, 31 weeks have passed since a last known trough of a 24.1-week
wave.

@ Since price movement was in the downward direction prior to the price
low that occurred 7 days ago. It is concluded that the 24.1-week wave
trough either occurred 7 days ago or is overdue.

The phase of this wave should be recorded as 31 weeks, meaning that
the wave is probably in a bottoming area.

© Similar conditions exist for the 46.3-week wave. Note that 50 weeks have
elapsed since the trough at grid line 129 of figure 3-27. The addition of
2 weeks to update this estimate to 24 November results in a phase of 52
weeks on that date. Again, the conclusion is that a trough of this wave
either occurred 7 days ago or is to be expected soon.

The phase of the 46.3-week wave should be recorded as 52 weeks, mean-
ing that this wave also is probably in a bottoming area.

© For the 92.6-week wave, 95 weeks have elapsed since the last firm
trough at grid line 84 of Figure 3-27. Adding 2 weeks to update this
estimate to 24 November results in a phase of 97 weeks. As with the
shorter wave, the conclusion is that a trough of this wave either occurred
7 days ago or is to be expected soon.

© The shortest wave on the daily chart, the 5.9-day wave, is treated some-
what the same as these longer waves in that it is listed as being 7 days
long from the last known trough. This is a simple way of saying that
a trough probably occurred about one day ago but the evidence in price
action is insufficient to warrant placing a phasing diamond as yet.

© To complete the current cyclic model, the direction of action of each

this situation symbolically, a curved arrow is used:

—

‘The bottoming, rounded form of the symbol denotes in bottom area,
while the slightly higher arrow tip denotes the past due phase status.

© The extraction of information from the phasing analysis is now nearly
complete. In practice, this process requires very little time, and the results
are recorded directly in the applicable portion of the analysis form (cur-
rent cyclic model tabulation). As the average wave periods become avail-
able, you should calculate and record the associated FLD number. In
some instances, an FLD number expressed in both weeks and days may
be desirable in order to permit use with both weekly and daily charts.
To do this, obtain the weekly FLD number in the usual manner, divid-
ing the wave periods in weeks by 2 and adding 1. To get the daily FLD
number, first multiply the period in weeks by 7, then divide by 2 and
add 1. However, if the average period is available in days, the daily FLD
is obtained directly and is to be preferred over a number obtained from a
period estimate in weeks. The current cyclic model for the example under
consideration now looks as follows:

© Only the status of the SL quantity is yet missing. This factor will be
dealt with briefly here for the sake of completeness and will be discussed
in more detail in lessons to follow.

@ The symbol FL is read as sigma el. The Greek letter sigma is commonly
used to stand for the process of summation. The capital L stands for
long. The composite symbol represents the sum of all longer period waves.

@ Because there is no known limit to the lengths of price waves, there will
probably always be waves longer than the longest wave that can be defined
by any given amount of data. For a given set of data the trend under-
lying the longest definable wave is the sum of all price waves with still
longer periods. This trend is symbolized as 3L and is often referred to

as sum long.

EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING

© Since these longer waves cannot be phased, it is only possible to make
an estimate of the direction of their combined action at current time.
However, such an estimate is greatly improved by the information con-
tent of a completed phasing analy

 

 

@ As an introduction to this topic, please refer back to figure 3-27 at the
end of section eleven.

© We know that the longest wave capable of being phased on such a weekly
chart is the (nominal) 18-month wave. In figure 3-27, one full cycle of
this wave has been judged to exist between grid lines 84 and 172,

 

© The trend underlying ths wave would be a smooth curve over the interval
between grid lines 84 and 172, and all price oscillations would occur
about this curve as an axis. This trend would, by definition, be the quanti-
ty3L.

@ We note from the initial cyclic model that the 18-month wave is con-
sidered to be weak at the time of the analysis. Observation of price move-
ment certainly confirms this, since virtually no evidence of a price wave
of this period is observable in the time interval between grid lines 84 and
172, Instead, all that can be observed is the 9-month wave fluctuations.
Therefore, a smooth curve about which these oscillations occur must be EL

 

in this instance.

@ Such a curve is a very nearly straight, horizontal line. It is therefore
concluded that 3L for this situation is acting in neither an up nor a down
direction. The arrow symbol for this state of affairs is:

© Adding this estimate of 3L status to the current cyclic model results in
the final completed version shown in figure 3-34.

@ You may have noticed that the FLD numbers shown in the current cyclic
model are not rounded. This is merely a simplification in the calculation,
It does not mean that in plotting the FLD line you must go to a great
deal of effort to place each point exactly at the value indicated. If it is
easier for you to plot the point on the nearest grid line (as if the FLD

 

  

Page 91——


  

    
 

   

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

  

EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING

ANALYSIS continued
number were rounded), by all means do so. It is important to keep in
mind that cyclic concept tools such as the FLD are probabilistic in nature.
‘A small difference in method of calculation or application will have a
slight positive effect on one transaction, but will have a slight negative
effect on another. Considered over a large number of transactions, the
effect to be expected is negligible.

CONCLUSION
© The information content of lessons two and three represents the largest

part of all the new knowledge you need in order to accomplish cyclic
analysis.
© Lessons four through ten will present a large amount of new material,
but this can be introduced at a more leisurely pace. Emphasis will in-
crease steadily in these lessons on workshops and learning by doing.
© You should not expect, at this point, to be able to handle a cyclic analysis
unaided. However, you should feel you have a good grasp of the termin-
ology and concepts studied so far.
@ Also, you should have many questions remaining in your mind. These will
be answered as subsequent lesson material is presented.
© The big hurdles to be cleared in the first three lessons were:
Lesson one —An understanding of the nature of the basic cyclic
concepts.
Lesson two —A general understanding of how to get from basie cyclic
concepts to transaction decisions,

Lesson three—A general understanding of the process of creating the
current cyclic model on which all cyclic analysis depends.

© In lesson number four, all of the concepts and methods learned to date
will be integrated into an improved understanding of the actual setting up
of a transaction. Methods will be discussed that permit very rapid ex-
traction of certain kinds of information without going through the formal
procedures of lessons two and three. And experience will be gained in
the art of phasing analysis via workshop exercises.

© You should proceed now to a study of the summary and glossary for
lesson three. Then take the self-examination.

© A current cyclic model is a tabulated summary of the characteristics of
the individual price waves that sum to form price movement.

     

© A current cyclic model is constructed in three steps:
1. Generation of an initial cyclic model
2. Completion of a phasing analysis

3. Extraction of information from the phasing analysis to permit as-
sembly of a current cyclic model.

© The Principle of Nominality and the interpretation of a Periodogram
are involved in the construction of an initial cyclic model.

© A Periodogram is a single, graphical diplay of the multiple wave nature of
‘a composite wave such as price movement.

@ Each wave represented on a Periodogram appears as a single vertical line.
The height of the vertical line represents wave amplitude, and the hori-
zontal position of the vertical line represents wave period.

@ A price-movement Petiodogram consists of numerous vertical lines,
equally spaced. When these lines increase in height to a peak and then
diminish in height, the Periodogram is indicating that a wave exists. The
period of this wave is assumed to be the period of the vertical line at the
peak as read from the Periodogram horizontal period scale.

© In interpreting a Periodogram, the longest-period peaks are tabulated
first. This is primarily an aid in forming the habit of always thinking in
terms of the longest waves first, a vital factor in the correct approach to
phasing analysis.

All Rights Reserved

@ A Periodogram will often display extraneous peaks. These are deleted
from further consideration by comparing them with the periods to be
expected from the nominal cyclic model and by applying the Principle

© An initial cyclic model tabulates:
1. Wave periods from the nominal cyclic model
2. Wave periods from the interpreted Periodogram

3. Comments on the relative strength of each wave as indicated by the ze

© The phase of a wave can be very usefully and conveniently expressed as e 2
the time elapsed since the last trough of the wave. 38
as

‘This measure of phase also implies the current direction of action of the 3
wave. a

@ A phasing analysis is the means by which the most accurate available
estimate of wave periods and phases is obtained.

© The initial cyclic model is an indispensable guide in the process of phas-
ing analysis, but a link between this model and actual price movement
is needed. The Dominancy Envelope and its centerline provide this link.

@ The Dominancy Envelope is constructed so as to just contain the fluc-
tuations of the most visually apparent wave in the price movement. This
wave is called the dominant wave, ot the dominant component. ‘

@ The Dominancy Envelope acts as a wave-period divider. It does this by
splitting the complex sum of all price waves that constitute price move-
ment into two simpler sums.

 

@ One of these sums consists of the dominant wave plus all shorter waves.
This sum is contained by the Dominancy Envelope.

© Thus, the composite wave within the Dominancy Envelope is less com-
plex than the composite wave of total price movement. And it is there-
fore more easily interpreted in terms of individual wave action.

 

© The other sum consists of all waves longer than the dominant wave. This
sum constitutes the Dominancy Envelope centerline.

© ‘The shortest wave in this sum is the wave just longer than the dominant
wave and is called the pre-dominant wave.

© The Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuates generally in sympathy with
the pre-dominant wave.

© The Dominancy Envelope centerline represents the trend underlying the
dominant wave.

© FLD’s may be used in past time to gain information regarding the time

locations of price lows and highs associated with wave troughs and crests.

 

For this purpose, the FLD number can be simplified to merely one-
half of the estimated wave period; it need not be rounded, and it need not
have one time unit added to

 

This FLD usage plays an important role in phasing analysis.
© The process of phasing analysis takes place in three steps:
1. Entry
2. Extension
3. Completion.

@ The phasing analysis for a given equity always starts with the chart having
the longest time span and with one of the longest-period components of

the initial cyclic model.

@ Entry to a phasing analysis can take place in several ways:

1. Use of the Dominancy Envelope centerline oscillations as evidence
of the time locations of pre-dominant wave troughs and crests

2. Direct comparison of initial cyclic model period estimates with
Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuations or with price movement

 

3. Application of FLD's in past time to establish initial estimates of
long-wave trough locations.

 

@ Extension is accomplished in four steps:

1. The combined effects of the Principles of Synchronicity and Har-
monicity are used to enable placement of next-shorter-wave phas-
ing diamonds at the same time positions as those of the longer waves

already phased.

2. Phasing gaps between troughs coincident with consecutive next-
Jonger wave trough positions are filled by applying the Principle of
Harmonicity and using measuring strips for direct comparison of

cyclic model information with price movement.

 

 

 

Page 98


CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  
 

re

3. Price is explored to the left and to the right of next-longer wave
troughs for which no consecutive diamond is available in the direc-
tion explored. Visual evidence in price is noted using period esti-
mates augmented by the period samples located previously as a

guide.

 

rr) mee

     

4, Measuring strips are used for direct comparison with price move-
ment to locate the more obvious of the remaining unidentified

troughs of the wave being phased.
These steps are carried out in the order shown, and ambiguous situations
are deferred for resolution later in the phasing analysis.
© Completion is accomplished in two steps:

1, Filling all phasing gaps of the shortest wave phased

2. Working upward through successively longer waves by filling the
harmonic gaps (applying the Principle of Harmonicity).

@ Information is extracted from a phasing analysis as follows:

1. Wave period-samples are averaged for each wave phased.

2. Period averages and the period-sample variations are laid out on
measuring strips which are then used to measure on the chart from
last known trough positions into future time. Period averages and
ranges of variation are then transferred to the chart at expected

future trough locations. This process provides visual indication of
the times at which future nests-of-lows are most likely to occur.

 

3. The phase of each wave is recorded as the elapsed time since the
last trough.
4. The direction of action of each wave is inferred from the phase
information.
@ Information extracted from the phasing analysis is tabulated as the cur-
rent cyclic model.

@ The information contained in a current cyclic model is:

1, Nominal-model wave periods

2. Best estimate, or actual, wave periods
3. The phase of each wave at present time
4, The direction of action of each wave at present time
5. The FLD number for each wave based on actual periods.
© Phasing analysis is not an exact process; it is an art. However, the guide-
lines available make it a readily learned art.

It is quite possible for two analysts to perform a phasing analysis on the
same price data and arrive at somewhat different results. However, it is
seldom that such differences are great enough to result in different con-
clusions regarding a potential transaction.

© Part of the art of phasing analysis lies in the ability to recognize that
precise or unambiguous resolution of some situations is not necessary
when such resolution would not alter the needed transaction conclusions.

On the other hand, part of the art of phasing analysis lies in the ability
to recognize those situations that must be resolved with care if poor trans-
action conclusions are to be avoided.

© The art of phasing analysis is best learned by doing, and ample oppor-
tunity to learn in this way will be provided in subsequent lessons.

 

© Time translation is the shift in the time positions of composite wave lows
and highs by comparison with the time positions of associated component

wave troughs and crests.
@ Time translation is caused by trend. When troughs and lows are involved,
it is referred to as /ow translation. When crests and highs are involved,
it is referred to as high translation.
© When trend is down, low translation is to the right and high translation
is to the left. When trend is up, low translation is to the left and high
translation is to the right.
© Weekly prices for commodities are not assembled in the same way as

weekly prices for stocks. As a result, care must be used in the interpre-
tation of phase information from weekly commodity charts.

ACTION SIGNAL A price break of a VTL or FLD indicating a strong
likelihood that an anticipated price move is under way.

AMPLITUDE. The size or amount of the price movement involved as a
price wave goes ftom trough to crest.

AUGMENTED MEASURING STRIP A measuring strip that has had
period samples added to the original nominal-model and Periodogram
wave period estimates.

AVERAGE PERIOD A wave period estimate formed by averaging two or
more trough-to-trough measurements.

BAR-CHART A common way of plotting, or graphing, equity prices. The
high and low prices for a day are joined by a vertical straight line to show
the range over which prices traded for that day. The consecutive set of
such lines is called a daily bar-chart of that equity. Similarly, the high
and low prices for a week can be compiled to form a weekly bar-chart
for the equity.

BOTTOMING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a
trough.

BREAK Prices break a VTL ot an FLD when they pass from one side of
the VTL or FLD to the other (from above to below, or vice versa).

CASCADE A rippling, or one-thing-followed-by-another, effect. In cyclic
theory, the tendency under certain circumstances for an FLD break to
predict prices which will break another FLD, thus predicting still greater
price moves, ete.

COMMONALITY A term indicating that price movements of negotiable
equities have similar qualities in spite of the diversity of visual appear-
ances of price charts.

COMPLETION As applied to phasing analysis, the process of filling
phasing gaps after the entry and extensions have been accomplished.

COMPONENT A single wave in price movement or in the composite wave
formed from several single waves.

COMPOSITE WAVE The single new form resulting from the combin-
ation by summation of two or more price waves,

CONSECUTIVE TROUGHS Wave troughs from consecutive cycles of a

given wave.
CREST or WAVE CREST The highest point on a component price wave.

vy ag PAM SBN TY
9U ‘894d SORT F161 @ 1YBIKdo>

CURRENT CYCLIC MODEL A tabulation of wave periods, phases, and
directions of action. The best available estimate of the values for these

characteristics of the waves active in the price movement of a given
equity at a given time. Usually includes FLD numbers for easy reference.

CYCLE One complete movement of a price wave from one trough to the

next consecutive trough.
CYCLICALITY (Derived from the word cycle.) A generic term referring
to the wave phenomena in the price action of equities.

CYCLIC MODEL See current cyclic model.

DIRECT COMPARISON A technique whereby available period estimates
are laid out on a measuring strip which is then visually compared with

Price action.
DOMINANT WAVE or DOMINANT COMPONENT The longest wave

contained by the Dominancy Envelope.

DOMINANCY ENVELOPE A computer generated aid present on Cyclitec
charts. A constant vertical height envelope containing most of price move-
ment. Contains the dominant wave and all shorter waves. Serves as a

 

wave-period divider.

DOMINANCY ENVELOPE CENTERLINE A smooth curve central to
the Dominancy Envelope. The sum of all waves longer than the dom-
inant wave.

DOWNTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price
highs. The later price high is lower than the earlier price high so that the
line slants downward to the right.

BL Read sigma el. Stands for sum long. Consists of the sum of all waves
longer than the longest wave identified and phased.

1g a phasing analysis. The placement of the

first phasing diamonds of a phasing analysis.

EQUITY or NEGOTIABLE EQUITY A generic label for securi
modity futures contracts, etc., traded on an auction market.

 

EXTENSION _ As applied to a phasing analysis, the means of phasing
successively shorter waves after an entry has been effected.

FLD An acronym for Future Line of Demarcation. An FLD is a forward-
shifted replica of past price averages and has useful properties.

FLD NUMBER A number used in constructing an FLD. It is found by

dividing the period of the wave on which the FLD is based by two, then
adding one.

 

FUTURE TIME POSITION A place on an equity price bar-chart corres-
ponding to some time in the future; that is, some time to the right of the
last known price bar on the chart.

GAP Ina row of phasing diamonds, a space too long to represent a period

sample. On a Cyclitec chart, a space where no price bar is shown because
the market was closed that day.

GRID LINE NUMBER Refers to the time scale reading on a Cyclitee
chart corresponding with a given vertical grid line on the chart. A conven-
ient reference for various activities displayed on a Cyclitec chart.

HARMONICITY (Derived from the word harmonious or harmonic.)
Means that wave periods are related by small, whole numbers.

HIGH TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price
highs versus wave crests.

INITIAL CYCLIC MODEL A tabulated set of wave periods derived from
Periodogram interpretation and the Principle of Nominality. Used as a
guide in setting up a current cyclic model.

LONG To go long in an equity is to purchase it in the expectation of sel-
ling it later at a higher price.

LOW TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price
ows versus wave troughs.

MEASURING STRIP A strip of paper with estimated periods laid out on
Used in phasing analysis.

i

Also, a strip of paper with an FLD number laid out on it. Used for FLD
measurements.

MOST SIGNIFICANT PRICE LOW Means most visually evident. Applied
to the coarsest price movement between two consecutive wave troughs. A
price low that is made up of more coincident wave troughs than are ad-

 

 

jacent price lows.
NEIGHBORING As applied to waves, refers to two waves in a set of
waves whose periods are closest to being the same.
NEST-OF-LOWS A time when two or more component price waves form
simultaneous troughs.

NOMINAL MODEL or NOMINAL CYCLIC MODEL _ A list of the periods
of the price waves to be expected in price movements.

 

NOW TIME Synonymous with current, or present, time. The time at
which an analysis is being made. The latest time for which price data is
available.

PAST-TIME FLD An FLD plotted in the chart area representing time
prior to current time. Usually used to aid identification of wave trough
location, Uses a simplified FLD number consisting of merely one-half
of the wave period estimate.

PAST-TIME POSITION A location on an equity price bar-chart corres-
ponding to some time in the past; that is, somé time to the left of the
last known price bar on the chart.

 

PERIOD The length of time that elapses between a trough of a price
wave and the next consecutive trough of the same wave.

PERIODOGRAM A convenient means of presenting information about
many price waves simultaneously. A plot of wave amplitude versus wave
period. Consists of a series of vertical lines, each representing a potential
wave. Line height indicates amplitude, while horizontal line position
indicates period.

PERIOD SAMPLE A measurement of elapsed time between estimated
positions of consecutive wave troughs.

PHASE The time relationship between two price waves.

PHASING ANALYSIS A procedure for isolating the wave components
present in a complex composite wave. A series of methods whereby wave
trough positions are estimated. The means of refining an initial cyclic
model into a current cyclic model.

PHASING DIAMOND A diamond-shaped mark placed on a chart to
denote the best estimate of the time location of a wave trough.

POTENTIAL Short for potential profit. The difference between the entry
and exit prices of a transaction expressed in price units.

POTENTIAL PERCENTAGE Anticipated profit (potential) expressed as a
percentage of the transaction entry commitment.

PR_ An acronym for Potential-Risk ratio. The ratio of potential to risk;
that is, potential divided by risk.

 

PREDICTION An estimate of something expected to happen in the future.
Never exact, always an expected high likelihood.

PRE-DOMINANT WAVE or PRE-DOMINANT COMPONENT The
wave just longer than the dominant wave. The shortest wave that is part
of the sum of waves comprising the Dominancy Envelope centerline,

PRICE ACTION or PRICE MOVEMENT _ Refers to the fact that equity
prices change constantly as time passes. The motion of prices as time

goes on.

PRICE-MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM A Periodogram constructed by
analysis of price data. Interpreted by noting the period of the tallest
line of each set that forms a peak in the Periodogram shape.

PROBABILISTIC Having to do with probabilities. Dealing with like-
lihood stronger than possibility, but falling short of certainty.

PROPORTIONALITY or PROPORTIONAL TO _ relationship between
two quantities such that as one increases, the other also increases, and by

the same percentage. In cyclic concepts, relates to the amplitude and
period of the various waves in a cyclic model.

PZ An acronym for Pause Zone. A price region in which one or more
FLD’s tend to congregate in a horizontal band. A price region where
prices tend to slow-down or reverse a cascade movement.

RANGE OF PERIOD ESTIMATES A time span corresponding to the
difference between maximum and minimum estimates of the period of a
given wave.

 

 

RISK Short for /oss risk. The amount that will be lost in case the trans-
action is terminated because prices penetrate the stop-loss level.

SHORT or SELL SHORT To sell borrowed stock in the expectation that
the price will subsequently go down. If the price does go down, a profit
is made by replacing the borrowed stock with stock purchased on the
open market at the reduced price.

paasasay S13 TV
-ouy ‘ssaag S9PE1] EL6T @ WEIRD

STOP-LOSS LEVEL A special kind of action signal. A price level at
which a transaction is terminated because the probabilities no longer
favor a profitable outcome. This action usually results in a loss on the
transaction.

SUMMATION The act of summing, or adding.

SYNCHRONICITY (Derived from the word synchronous ot synchronize.)
Means to occur simultaneously in time. In cyclic theory, the term refers
to the tendency of the troughs of one component wave to occur at the
same time as the troughs of other component waves in the same com-
posite wave.

 

TIME-ALONG A term used as a convenient measurement of wave phase.
Means time elapsed since last known wave trough.

TIME SCALE On a Cyclitec chart, a series of numbers laid out hori-
zontally and corresponding to a count of the vertical grid lines. A con-
venient means of referring to specific grid lines. Also a convenient means
of measuring elapsed time between marks on a measuring strip.

TIME TRANSLATION The shift that underlying trend causes in the time
position of price lows and highs so that they no longer coincide with
associated wave troughs and crests.

TOPPING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a crest.

TREND Generally, the strength and direction of price movement if short
term fluctuations in price are ignored. In cyclic theory, the sum of all
ptice waves longer in period than the specific wave for which trend is
being defined.

 


 

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Ine.

    

CYCLITEC Wy UU I l rey
zesiaes [leet le
Cournsee LLIN Ute

TREND LINE A line drawn across specially selected price highs (or
price lows).

TROUGH or WAVE TROUGH The lowest point on a component price
wave.

UPTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price lows.
The later price low is higher than the earlier price low so that the line
slants upward to the right.

VALID TREND LINE A trend line drawn according to specific rules so
that the result has meaning in terms of cyclic concepts. Abbreviated
VTL.

VARIATION _ In cyclic concepts, refers to the fact that actual local wave
conditions differ from the nominal, or long-term average, expectation.

VOLATILITY INDEX A number appearing in the heading line of Cycli-
tec charts. Gives a measure of the relative volatility of the issue; that is,

the percentage rate, or speed, with which prices for the issue tend to
change.

WAVE As applied to price movement, a repetitive fluctuation in price
with well defined, measurable characteristics.

WAVE DIRECTION The best estimate of the direction in which a given
price wave is tending to move prices at present time. Spoken of as
being up, down, flat, bottoming, or topping.

WAVE-PERIOD DIVIDER A means of separating the sum of all waves
forming a given price movement into two simpler sums of waves. Each of
the two sums consists of contiguous waves of the cyclic model. The
Dominancy Envelope and its centerline function as a wave-period di

 

der.

WAVE PHASE An indication of the location in time of the wave shape.
Conveniently measured as time elapsed from the most recent wave trough
to current, or present, time.

It is suggested that you go all the way through the examination a first time

without referring to the cassette or text material. In doing so, mark answers
only to the questions you are sure of; don’t guess. Leaving an answer un-
marked will flag that question for further attention on the second pass
through the exam.

   

After you have finished the first pass, check the answers you have marked
against the answer key, circling any that are wrong. Then go back through
the examination and refer to the text material to refresh your memory on
those questions that you answered wrong or left unanswered.
