HT LESSON
REVIEW

This is the introductory review for Cyclitec Services Training Course lesson
number three, which is entitled Setting Up the Cyclic Model.

In the first lesson, a set of cyclic concepts were described that permit judging
the outcome of a transaction before entering it. This process consists of
determining when in future time prices are most likely to reverse; how far
prices are likely to go when they do reverse; what price level, if reached,
signals that success odds have been degraded; and what future price action
indicates that the price reversal expected is probably underway.

 

With this degree of foreknowledge of the probable nature of a transaction,
a logical decision can be made as to whether or not it is desirable to enter
the transaction.

The second lesson described how cyclic concepts can be used to identify
various trends according to the price waves each consists of. Knowing these
component waves and having determined their phase status, the likelihood
of trend reversal in the near future can be estimated.

Also, lesson number two described how trend lines can be associated with
each wave in a current cyclic model in such a way as to make the trend
lines more useful. Such trend lines, drawn according to specific rules, are
called valid trend lines, ot VIL's. Valid trend lines are used to find price
lows and highs associated with wave troughs and crests, and can serve as
action signal generators,

Future lines of demarcation, or FLD's, are another valuable graphical
construction. FLD's are lines drawn in future time and consisting of past
average prices shifted forward. An FLD can be drawn for each wave of
a current cyclic model.

FLD’s, like valid trend lines, can be used to detect wave troughs and crests
in price movement and can also serve as action signal generators. However,
unlike VIL's, FLD's can be used to estimate the extent of price moves.

The set of all FLD’s which correspond to all of the waves of a current

cyclic model form patterns in future time. Such patterns provide added
information as to what prices are most likely to do in the future.

—_—. ge 19


— Page 120.

 

 

 

  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  
 

REVIEW continued

A cascade of future price movements is indicated when FLD's are arranged
in a parallel, sloping pattern and cyclic analysis confirms that it is time
for a price reversal.

A pause zone is established when one or more FLD's are entangled in a
horizontal price region. Such a pause zone is an indication of future price
slow-down or pull-back and can signal the end of a cascade of price move-
ments.

FLD’s and VTL's can be used to determine when an expected price move
is likely to be underway. Used in this manner, they are called action signal
generators. They can also set logical stop-loss levels for potential trans-
actions. When an action signal occurs and if FLD patterns have set a price
move objective, a candidate transaction has been set up.

Given a stop-loss level and a price objective, a transaction entry price can
then be found which provides an acceptable ratio of potential profit to
loss risk and an acceptable percentage gain from the estimated potential
profit.

Such potential-risk ratios and potential percentage computations are
valuable aids in selecting the least-risk transactions from those that are

available.

Because of the importance of these concepts from the previous lessons,

the principle definitions, rules, and methods of interpretation are succinctly
summarized in the accompanying text. Please turn your tape unit off now
and turn to page one for a thorough review before continuing.

INTRODUCTION

The concepts of trading and investing discussed and exemplified in lessons
one and two are dependent on the ability to separate out the individual
wave components currently responsible for price change and to identify
the characteristics of each. The process of isolating such waves is called
phasing analysis, and the tabulated assembly of the resulting wave char-
acteristics is called a current cyclic model.

To accomplish a phasing analysis we make use of any and all of the cyclic
concepts available as well as techniques and methods derived from these

 

paarasoy smary IV

“DUT 'ss24q S49PEL] E161 @ WBUKdOL)


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
‘All Rights Reserved

 
 

 
 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 

 

 

INTRODUCTION | continued

concepts. Some of these methods were discussed in lesson two, but several
more are required before you are ready to undertake your first phasing

analysis.

Phasing analysis is not a mechanical process. The general procedures
can be stated, and the aids to the process can be described. But the process
itself is one of selecting the procedures most useful for application to a
given case. The basic cyclic concepts provide a guiding preconception of
what to expect. Then knowledge of basic concepts and specific techniques
are used to extract evidence that variations from basic concept expectations
exist.

‘The processes involved are not difficult to learn and quickly become instinc-
tive. However, more familiarity with, and knowledge of, the phenomena of
cyclicality is required in phasing analysis than in any other part of a com-
plete eyclic analysis. As a result, considerable detail will be presented in
this lesson, and a great deal of opportunity to learn by doing will follow

in the workshops of succeeding lessons.

 

A curtent cyclic model is created in three steps. In the first step, an initial
cyclic model is formed. The second step is a phasing analysis, And the third
step extracts information from the phasing analysis needed to refine the
initial cyclic model into a final current cyclic model.

In this lesson, you will first be exposed to several new concepts and methods
needed in one or more of these three steps. Then the process of constructing
an initial cyclic model will be described. Following this, phasing analysis
will be treated by discussion and extensive exercise. After these topics are
thoroughly covered, the methods of extracting information from the phas-
ing analysis to form the current cyclic model — and for other purposes —
will be explained.

‘You should turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section two.

THE CYCLIC MODEL CONCEPT REVISITED

On several occasions now, the idea of a cyclic model has been discussed.

In lesson one, the nominal cyclic model (or just nominal model) was des-
cribed. Then in lesson two this concept was extended to include the current
cyclic model of an individual equity. A current cyclic model has been defined

 

 

 

rT)

3

Page 121—

 


    
 
 

  

  

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

red Tet UAHA tT
3 rth Mi ft

THE CYCLIC MODEL CONCEPT REVISITED continued

as a table of wave periods and phases. It is time now to turn attention in
detail to the nature, meaning, and methods of arriving at a current cyclic
model.

    

 

paasasoy sy3i
-ouy ‘ssaag Sx9pery EL61 @ 1YBIAKdOD

When we speak of the nominal model, we refer to the average expected
periods of waves present in all negotiable equities. Thus the nominal model
consists only of a tabulation of wave periods; wave phases are not included.

However, the Principle of Variation tells us that the cyclic model in effect in
the price action of a given equity at any given time can vary considerably
from the nominal expectation. It is when we have used all of the methods at
our disposal to determine what the best estimate is of the wave variables

at work at a given time in an equity that we rename the result to avoid
confusion with the nominal model. Thus, we speak of the current eyelic
model for that equity at that time.

For our purposes we need to know much more about the waves at work in
price than just the period of each. Specifically, we need to know the phase
of each of the active waves. Therefore, a current cyclic model consists of a
tabulation of estimated wave periods and phases as of a given date.

It is recalled from lesson one that phase is a measure of where, in time, the
general shape of a wave is placed. For example, if we were to say that a
given wave had a trough thirteen days before Christmas day of 1913, then,
knowing the wave period, we could locate all earlier and later troughs. How-
ever, we could just as well have chosen any other reference time we so
desired, and our ability to locate that particular wave in time would have
been just as complete.

‘The simplest time reference for us to choose when considering the phase of
a wave is the time of the most recent trough of the wave. For example,
suppose that “now” time, or the date of the most current price-bar on a
chart we are analyzing, is Friday, the 16th of March, 1973. Then suppose
we find that the last trough for a wave with a 19-day period occurred just
four days earlier, on the 12th of March. We could say that now, on the 16th
of March, this wave is just four days along from its last trough. We have
then identified the phase of the wave in a most useful manner.

—— Page 122-

 


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
All Rights Reserved

  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

| ll WN HW LESSON
CE x

THE CYCLIC MODEL CONCEPT REVISITED continued

We know that such a wave forms a crest just one-half of its period along
from a trough; and we know that between the trough and the following crest
the wave is going up. Thus we see that by defining wave phase in the manner
described, we have immediately also implied the direction of the wave at
now time. In the example used, it was found that a wave with a 19-day
period was just four days along from its last trough. One-half of nineteen is
nine and one-half, which is the number of days this wave should rise from

a trough before forming a crest. Since it is only four days since the last
trough, this wave is about one-half way up from a trough to a crest and is
expected to rise for about another five and one-half days.

 

Now we can see more clearly what is needed in the tabulation of a current
cyclic model. Normally, such a tabulation is headed by an identification of
the equity involved and the date of the last price-bar on the tight of its price
chart. Then the nominal model wave periods are listed in a column to the
left. Opposite these nominal periods are listed the best estimates of current,
actual wave periods that correspond to the nominal periods. A third column
provides the phase information in the form of the time-along, or time
elapsed since the last wave trough. A fourth column contains arrows showing
whether each wave is estimated to be acting in an up or in a down direc-

tion at the time of the analysis. Finally, the far column on the right con-
tains the FLD number for each wave based on the actual wave periods.

You should turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section
three.

THE PERIODOGRAM CONCEPT

Having defined phase in the manner of the last section, it becomes clear
that all that is needed in order to set up a current cyclic model is the loca-
tion of the price lows corresponding to all the price-wave troughs in the
price movement. Marking these lows with phasing diamonds permits us to
average a series of trough-to-trough period samples for each wave, resulting
in a best estimate of actual periods. Then the time location of the last trough
for each wave provides the necessary phase information to complete the
current cyclic model.

Once again, all that is needed to set up a current cyclic model is identifization

 

  

Page 123


 
 
  

    

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

THE PERIODOGRAM CONCEPT continued

of the time positions of all the troughs of all the price waves present. This
identification process is called phasing analysis.

 

This sounds quite easy, but in practice it requires all the knowledge of
cyclic concepts and methods available. The basic techniques will be pre-
sented in this lesson, and subsequent lessons will afford you ample oppor-
tunity to put your learning into practi

   

Itis nearly impossible to do effective phasing analyses without some com-
putational assistance. The large digital computers available today are ideal
for this purpose. Several of the figures of lessons one and two showed infor-
mation derived in this way in addition to the basic price-bar plots.

One of these computer generated assists is called a Periodogram. A thor-
ough understanding of the Periodogram is essential because it is one of
our most valuable tools for working out the phasing analysis of a price
bar-chart.

If we visualize a single price wave we have little difficulty in thinking of it
and its effects. We can even sketch out the appearance of such a wave if
we are given its amplitude, period, and phase.

However, when we consider all of the waves of a cyclic model, the process
of visualizing all of these waves at once, or of sketching them all out, be-
comes laborious and clumsy.

 

‘The Periodogram solves many of these problems for us.

A Periodogram is simply a plot of wave amplitude versus wave period. Am-
plitude is plotted on the vertical axis of a graph, and period is plotted on
the horizontal axis.

Usually, a Periodogram is plotted as a series of vertical lines. Each line
represents one possible price wave. The height of the line represents ampli-
tude, and the position of the line on the horizontal axis represents period.
Thus, any size set of price waves can be represented by a single, simple
plot.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section four.

THE PRICE-MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM AND INITIAL
CYCLIC MODEL

In the last section you were introduced to the idea of a Periodogram as
applied to a set of ideal waves. First you saw how known waves could be
represented in Periodogram form. Then you saw how a Periodogram could
be read so that the waves indicated could be laid out for visual inspection
and graphic manipulation.

fIK

   

All of this so far has had to do only with ideal waves. As might be expected,
the situation is similar but not quite so simple when applied to the complex
composite of many non-ideal waves that constitute price movements.

What we would like to have is a Periodogram that tells us exactly what
price waves are active in a given equity at a given time. What is achievable
is a Periodogram that provides best estimates of the wave situation at hand.
As a result, a Periodogram for price movements is a powerful aid in setting
up a cyclic model but must be considered as nothing more than this. It
serves as an independent source of information as to what waves are active
in price movement at a given time — as opposed to being an absolute
indicator.

  

The process of producing such a Periodogram is so complex as to be beyond
the possibility of manual computation, so the computations required are
normally accomplished by a computer. The computer-generated Period-
ogram does not provide a single line on the display for each price wave. In-
stead, a series of equally spaced lines is produced. These lines vary in height,
and it is the peak in a series of lines as they first build up in amplitude

toa maximum and then dimihish that provides an indication that a price
wave exists.

Such displays are not perfect and will often show smaller peaks that are
rather close together. In such cases prior knowledge of what is to be expec-
ted is of great aid. Both the nominal model and the Principal of Harmonicity
constitute such prior knowledge.

In interpreting a price-movement Periodogram, tabulate each peak (or even
a reasonable hint of the presence of a peak), starting with the longest per-
iods. In doing this you are fully aware that more waves may be tabulated
than actually exist. The strongest and most obvious peaks should be

marked in the tabulation with a comment such as “strong” or “very strong”.

Page 125 ——

 


     
 

LESSON

3

 

—— Page 126

  

 

TT

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

hil

THE PRICE-MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM AND INITIAL
CYCLIC MODEL continued

From this list of Periodogram peaks it is now necessary to select a set of
periods that make sense in relation to the nominal model and the Principle
of Harmonicity. Start with one of the strongest peaks present and note
which period in the nominal model is closest to the period of this peak.
Starting a column just to the left of the column of Periodogram peaks,
enter the appropriate nominal-model period beside the entry of the corres-
ponding strong peak. Knowing approximately what the period of the next
longer and shorter waves should be (from the nominal model), it is usually
a simple matter to select the next tabulated Periodogram peak to corre:
pond. Also, if an intervening tabulation is under consideration, the Prin-
ciple of Harmonicity can be used to help resolve the problem.

  

 

 

The resulting tabulation of nominal and matching Periodogram periods is
called an initial cyclic model. It is seldom that the cyclic model formed in
this way can be directly used as the final current cyclic model needed. How
ever, results will always be produced that are sufficiently close to being
correct to allow the process of refinement by other methods to begin.

Please turn your tape unit off now, and return to the accompanying text
at section five.

SOME FEATURES OF CYCLITEC STOCK AND
COMMODITY CHARTS

By now you may have realized that the display of vertical bars along the
bottom of the large charts used in lessons one and two were actually Period-
ograms, not plots of trading volume as you might first have supposed.
Because trading volume is not very useful in cyclic analysis, the usual plot
of trading volume is deleted. The space thus released is utilized for the

plot of the Periodogram, which is, of course, very useful.

‘As we continue this discussion, please refer to the typical Cyclitec Ser-
vices chart shown in figure 3-14 at the end of section six of the text.

A word of caution: It is very important to realize that the location of the
Periodogram plot on the chart page should not be taken to imply any re-
lationship between a given vertical line in the Periodogram plot and the
price bar that may happen to lie above it on the same vertical grid line.
The price-bar plot and the Periodogram plot are completely independent

 

wo

paasasoy svyara IV
“wuy ‘ssaug S49 €L61 O WSUACOD


8, Inc,

Copyright © 1973 Traders Pres:

 

    

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  

iti

SOME FEATURES OF CYCLITEC STOCK AND
COMMODITY CHARTS continued

of each other, with entirely different meanings assigned to their respective
vertical and horizontal axes. The horizontal axis of the Periodogram
represents wave period; the horizontal axis of the price-bar plot represents
calendar time, The vertical axis of the Periodogram represents relative wave
amplitude; the vertical axis of the price-bar plot represents stock or com-
modity price. The two displays could just as well be on two separate sheets
of paper; but the existing arrangement is, of course, more convenient,

One characteristic of the Cyclitee charts that you may have become curious
about is the occurrence of one- and two-day gaps in the daily price-bar
plots. These gaps represent holidays and weekends. It is a curious fact
that cyclic principles operate in calendar time, without regard for whether
the market is open or closed. As a consequence, failure to provide a time
space for the days on which a market is closed would result in distortion
of the wave information. This distortion would be particularly pronounced
for the waves with shorter periods.
You also may have become curious about the number labeled VOL that
appears in the upper right corner of the charts. This number is the volatility
index, which is a measure of the relative rapidity with which prices tend
to change for the equity being plotted.
We have already spent considerable time discussing the Periodogram be-
cause it is so important to us in setting up the initial cyclic model. Another
Cyclitee chart feature that will be very useful to us in phasing analysis is
the Dominancy Envelope.
‘This envelope is a pair of smooth dotted curves, on the upper and lower
sides of price movement, that enclose most of the price bars within a
constant-height channel, It is so constructed as to contain within it the wave
movements in the price action that are most visually evident, that dominate
the visual appearance of the price plot.

A solid centerline is provided for the Dominancy Envelope. From what has
been learned about trend in lesson two, and from the manner of construc-
tion of the Dominancy Envelope, it can be seen that this centerline tends to
represent the trend underlying the dominant price wave for each chart.

 

    

   

 

 

 

The Dominancy Envelope serves as a bridge between the initial cyclic

 

 

 

Page 127


       

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

SOME FEATURES OF CYCLITEC STOCK AND
COMMODITY CHARTS continued

model and actual price movement. Recalling the goal of this lesson, which
is to learn to locate the price lows corresponding to wave troughs of the
current cyclic model, we see that the Periodogram provides a guide as to
what wave periods to look for in the price data. But we still need a transi-
tion from this kind of information to specific knowledge of wave phase
obtained by locating wave troughs in actual price movement. The Domin-
ancy Envelope and its centerline are the principle aids in this transition
process.

At times locating wave troughs in price movement is a relatively simple
task. But at other times it is quite difficult and we must use all the infor-
mation and techniques at our disposal. The Dominancy Envelope and its
centerline perform an invaluable service in this regard.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the accompanying text
at section six.

  

 

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD’s IN PAST TIME
You have been taught how to formally construct precise FLD’s, and have
seen that one of their major uses is to indicate when an expected wave
trough has been passed.

This trough-location capability of the FLD is available for use not only in
future time, but in past time as well. Used in past time this way, FLD’s
become a powerful aid in the phasing analysis process.

 

Let us suppose that we have an initial indication of a wave period from
the nominal model, the Periodogram, or any other source. Even if this
initial estimate is considerably in error by later comparison with the final
best period estimate, it can still be accurate enough to produce an FLD
that will provide evidence of the occurrence of price lows and highs corres-
ponding to wave troughs and crests.

To use an FLD in this way, the FLD must be observed in past time; that
is, in relation to price bars over the entire body of the chart rather than

just the most recent price bars. As a result, the procedure is somewhat
different than that previously described.

First of all, since the period estimate used is only indicative of the actual
wave period involved, the addition of one to the result of dividing the period

Page 128

poussoy sang HV

“Duy 'ssorg suapes |, gx6t @ Iyskdo


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Ine.

‘All Rights Reserved

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 
    

  

Tl | Oy red
ATT i]

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD’s IN PAST TIME continued

by two when computing the FLD number represents an unwarranted
accuracy assumption. Therefore, the FLD number used for this purpose is
just one-half of the period estimate.

Secondly, the purpose of rounding the FLD number is to permit transfer
of past data to an appropriate future chart grid line. If we are only inter-
ested in past data which clearly forms an FLD break, this nicety also is not,
necessary.

A third difference arises from the fact that we are not using the FLD either
for a precision price projection or to determine the precise time of a break
(as when FLD’s are used as action signal generators). Thus, there is no need
for the path of the FLD to be plotted with precision. Only the more gross
aspects of the movement are of interest.

Finally, we do not want to go to the time and effort of actually drawing
multiple FLD’s over the entire body of a chart,

All of these differences from FLD usage as previously described can be com-
bined into one very simple, fast, and effective method for gaining evidence
of wave trough locations in past time.

Simply lay out on a strip of scratch paper a span of time equal to one-half
of the roughly estimated period. Do not bother with rounding this number,
and take the time measurement directly from the time scale above the
Periodogram on a Cyclitec chart.

Place the left-hand mark on the paper strip on any price average in the
past, keeping the paper strip essentially horizontal. Note with the eye where
the right-hand mark falls in relation to prices. Slide the strip to the right,
generally following the price averages with the left-hand mark — and
retaining the strip in its essentially horizontal position. Continue to note
the movement of the right-hand mark relative to prices until itis clear

that if this FLD had been drawn in at the time location indicated, a price
break would have occurred.

Make a short, light mark on the chart at the approximate time and price
of the indicated break. Then use the time span marked on the paper strip
to measure back the one-half period in which a price low or high associa-
ted with a wave trough or crest is likely to be located.

 

Page 122——


    
 
 

  

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

* |(ll Hh Ht :

TROUGH LOCATION BY FLD‘s IN PAST TIME continued

With a very small amount of practice you will find this method capable of
producing invaluable information with very little time expenditure.

    

 

 

You will not, of course, be able to prove all, or even most, wave troughs
and crests in this manner. Nor is it necessary to do so. As with all other
cyclic methods, we accept whatever information results from use of the

method; and when information is not forthcoming, we apply a different
method.

tinuing, so please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at

INTRODUCTION TO PHASING ANALYSIS

You have now been exposed in a gross way to nearly all of the methods

and knowledge needed to produce a current cyclic model. Needless to say,
there are many subtle bits and pieces to be added, but these are best learned
by exercise.

In section five it was seen that the process of interpreting a Periodogram
consists of combining the expectations of basic cyclic concepts in the form of
nominality, harmonicity, and proportionality with information about the
present situation obtained from the Periodogram. The result is an initial
cyclic model.

 

In section six you were introduced to the concepts of the Dominancy En-
velope and its centerline, and it was stated there that the Dominancy
Envelope serves as a link between the initial cyclic model and price data.

Finally, in section seven it was demonstrated that the initial cyclic model
can provide crude wave period estimates which, through the use of FLD's in
past time, can lead to the identification of the time locations of some wave
troughs in price movement.

With this background, you are prepared to proceed with the phasing an-
alysis process. The general procedure for phasing analysis is as follows:

From the charts available for a given equity, select as the starting point
the chart that covers the longest period of time. Thus, using the Cyclitee


 

 

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc,
Rights Reserved

l] HT] it ] | T ry
cht TALE 3

INTRODUCTION TO PHASING ANALYSIS continued
charts, a phasing analysis begins with the weekly (as opposed to the daily)
chart for all stocks and commodities. The Dow Jones Industrial Average
analysis would begin with the monthly chart. This is an important proced-
ural point and is based on the nature of the cyclic phenomena and the
methods to be used in the phasing analysis process.

 

Similarly, for a given chart analysis begins with the longer period waves.

An entry into the phasing analysis process can be effected in several ways
and results in the time location of one or more price lows associated with
long-period wave troughs.

Once this is done, the Principles of Synchronicity and Harmonicity assure
us that all shorter waves should exhibit troughs at these same time loca-
tions. It is important to note that the reverse of this conclusion is not true.
That is, if a trough for a given wave is located, it does nor follow that
troughs of longer waves should occur at the same time. This is the primary
reason why phasing analysis is started with the longer waves.

 

Having identified one or more trough locations for a long-period wave, we
are then justified in assuming that these same locations define troughs of
the next shorter wave. Then, the expectations of the initial cyclic model are
combined with the composite-wave simplifying effects of the Dominancy
Envelope and its centerline in order to identify the most visually apparent
additional trough locations of this next shorter wave.

One of the most important principles of phasing analysis is this: Only
the most visually apparent troughs are identified initially. The nature of
the complex wave that is price movement is such as to obscure wave trough
evidence in many cases. Rather than making an initial (and perhaps in-
correct) attempt to resolve such ambiguities, the proper technique is to
move on to the process of phasing the next shorter wave. It will be found
that the buildup of cyclic evidence from the non-ambiguous cases will
eventually permit a high confidence solution to the ambiguous ones.

The output of a phasing analysis for a given chart is a best estimate of
the time location of every trough shown on the chart of every wave in the
initial cyclic model. Such information is recorded on the chart in the form

of horizontal rows of diamond shaped marks, with each diamond representing

the estimated time location of a wave trough. The upper row of diamonds
represents the longest wave phased on the chart, and each successively
lower row represents a successively shorter wave in the cyclic model.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to secticn eight of the text
for a study summary of the general rules of phasing analysis.

ENTERING A PHASING ANALYSIS

A phasing analysis is entered (or started) by finding evidence that one or
more price lows are associated with troughs of one of the longer waves on
a given chart. This can be accomplished in several ways.

For example, suppose it is a weekly chart that is under analysis. Inspec-
tion will show that the Cyclitec weekly chart consists of 180 price bars
representing 180 weeks of price action.

Knowing this, it is clear that a full cycle of the nominal $4-month wave
can never be seen on this chart because something more than 234 weeks
would be required to do so. But a nominal 18-month cycle can readily show
two full cycles, since 18 months is only 78 weeks. Thus, in this example it

is a near certainty that one, and perhaps two, of the most visually sig-
nificant price lows on the chart will be associated with an 18-month wave.
Once these price lows are tentatively identified, the initial cyclic model

can be used to provide period estimates so that confirming evidence can be
gained using FLD’s in past time as described in section seven.

 

 

Similar reasoning is useful on monthly or daily charts.

Another possible entry starts with the initial cyclic model. The nominal
and Periodogram estimates of a suitably long wave are laid out on a strip
of scratch paper using the time scale just above the Periodogram as a
measure. Three marks should appear on the strip. One at the left serves
as a common reference mark. Two on the right are spaced from the ref-
erence mark by the number of time units equal to the period estimates
from the nominal model and Periodogram, respectively. This impromptu
measuring strip is then moved horizontally along under price bars, and
the visually outstanding price lows separated by time intervals closest to
the period estimates on the strip are marked for further study.

ENTERING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

Or; phasing analysis entry may be accomplished by starting right off with
FLD's in past time based on periods taken from the initial cyclic model.

   

The most useful, and also the most mechanical, method of entry employs
the Dominancy Envelope and its centerline. Recall that the Dominancy
Envelope was described as being so constructed as to contain the move-
ments of a visually dominating price wave. More accurately, the Dominaney
Ennvelope contains within itself the sum of all price waves equal to, and
shorter than, a specific given wave. It follows, then, that the Dominancy
Envelope centerline is the sum of all longer waves.

From this it is seen that the Dominancy Envelope and its centerline function
to divide price movement into two parts, each of which is a composite

wave formed by summing consecutive waves of a cyclic model. As a result,
each part is a vastly simpler composite wave than total price movement.
This explains why this envelope can serve as a link between the initial

cyclic model and total price movement. Its function is to simplify the com-
posite wave of price movement, permitting easier visual identification and
separation of the component waves indicated by the initial cyclic model.

 

‘The Dominancy Envelope centerline is most useful in aiding in the identi-
fication of longer waves. The price oscillations about the centerline, and
contained by the envelope itself, are most useful in identification of the
shorter waves. In fact, the Dominancy Envelope centerline is seen to be a
representation of the trend underlying the longest wave contained by the
Dominancy Envelope. This interpretation leads to the realization that the
fluctuations of this centerline are most likely to correspond to the effects
of the wave just longer than the longest wave contained by the envelope.
This conclusion is the basis for the phasing analysis entry using the Dom-
inancy Envelope centerline.

This method involves noting the most major centerline fluctuations. Then
using a strip of scratch paper, several sample measurements taken roughly
from low to low and high to high of these fluctuations are compared to

the time scale on the chart. If the range of these period measurements
corresponds reasonably well with nominal model expectations and with the
shape of the Periodogram in this period region, additional confidence is
estab’ished in the value of the observations. In such cases, the lowest price

Page 133

ENTERING A PHASING ANALYSIS | continued

low in the vicinity of each of the centerline lows can be marked as a
strong contender for association with a long wave trough.

All of the methods described in this section, as well as other methods,

can be combined to provide strengthened evidence of wave trough locations.
However, in this lesson, emphasis will be plaged on phasing analysis entry
via the Dominancy Envelope centerline.

You should turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section
nine for examples and exercises in entering a phasing analysis.

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS

In the last section, attention was concentrated on the oscillation of the
Dominancy Envelope centerline as an aid to phasing analysis entry. It is,
the purpose of this section to demonstrate the techniques of extending the
phasing analysis effort.

‘The function of the Dominaney Envelope and its centerline has been des-
cribed as wave-period division. This concept should be firmly understood
in order to gain maximum benefit from this useful computerized aid. We
can make use of your newly acquired knowledge of Periodograms to help
provide this understanding,

 

Imagine a price motion Periodogram with a vertical line drawn on it
the valley between two peaks. Then the Dominancy Envelope centerline
is the sum of all waves to the right of the vertical line, including all those
too long to be shown on the Periodogram. Similary, the price movement
within the envelope is the sum of all waves to the left of the vertical line,
including those too short to appear on the Periodogram.

 

In making the phasing analysis entries in the last section, the initial cyclic
model was used as a guide to interpreting the fluctuations of the Dominancy
Envelope centerline, and the price movement within the envelope was
ignored. In extending a phasing analysis, the fluctuations of the centerline
are ignored while attention is focused on price movement within

the envelope. Again, the initial cyclic model is used as a guide to wave-
period expectations, and direct comparison of these expectations with

price movement is employed.

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS | continued

A phasing analysis extension takes place in four steps. First, advantage is
taken of the fact that the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity
require troughs of all waves shorter than a given wave to occur at the same
time as the troughs of the given wave. The reverse, of course, is not true.
That is, given the trough locations of one wave, it does not follow that all
longer waves have troughs at the same times. But, since phasing starts with
a long wave and proceeds to successively shorter ones, direct advantage
can be taken of the combined effects of Harmonicity and Synchronicity

by immediately placing phasing diamonds for the next shorter wave in the
time locations of each diamond for the longer wave phased just earlier.
Obviously this effortless location of such wave troughs results in steadily
increased ease in component separation as a phasing analysis continues.

In this first extension step, all of the troughs of the longer wave used for
analysis entry may not have been located as yet. As phasing of successively
shorter waves proceeds, evidence will build up for eventual complete phas-
ing of the longer wave.

In the second extension step, attention is concentrated only on the part of
price movement between each set of consecutive trough locations of the
wave phased just earlier. The Principle of Harmonicity would lead to the
expectation of finding a trough of the next shorter wave approximately half
way between these locations. In addition, the period estimates of the initial
cyclic model can be used to help in the search for such troughs. This results
in a series of trough identifications with a maximum confidence level. In
turn, these trough locations supply wave-period samples that are used to
improve the probability of correct choices in succeeding steps. As usual,
ambiguous situations are left for later resolution,

When there are no more sets of consecutive troughs of the next longer
wave to be examined, the third step of phasing extension is taken. The
method used is direct comparison (by means of a measuring strip) of initial
cyclic model expectations augmented by the period sample results of step
‘two. Such comparison explorations are made to the right and left of next-
longer wave diamonds for which no consecutive diamond is available in the
direction explored. Again, period samples are obtained to aid the following
step, and ambiguous cases are left for later resolution.

 

Page 135——

tn | at i

EXTENDING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

When no further work can be done in the manner of step three, direct com-
parison is employed against raw price data. Phasing diamonds are placed
only for the most obviously correct situations.

When a given wave has been completely phased or when the methods of all
four steps have been exhausted in attempts to phase the wave, the whole
process is repeated for the next-shorter wave.

Performing the extension of phasing analysis in the steps just described
has a definite purpose. This method permits work to progress in a manner
which always employs a maximum of component separation aid and which
permits a body of evidence to mount to support phasing of the more sus-
pect situations.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section ten
for examples and exercises.

COMPLETING A PHASING ANALYSIS

The entry and extension of a phasing analysis involve subjective judge-
ments on the part of the analyst based upon knowledge of basic cyclic
concepts and aided by computer generated displays. By contrast, completing
a phasing analysis is a nearly mechanical procedure.

First, the shortest wave phased must have all of its troughs located. If
this has not been accomplished in the extension effort, then one of two
courses of action should be followed.

If only a few obvious gaps exist between phasing diamonds for this short-
est wave because of lack of clear-cut price-movement evidence of the ex-
pected wave troughs, then these gaps must be filled in, arbitrarily if neces-
sary. This action is justified because we have already accumulated a large
body of evidence that this wave exists in the price data and we know that
the complexity of composite price waves is fully capable of obscuring occas-
ional wave trough positions.

If the gaps in the phasing of this shortest wave are numerous, then a still
shorter wave must be phased in order to provide additional information
with which to locate the missing diamonds.

COMPLETING A PHASING ANALYSIS continued

Ordinarily it is sufficient to phase a weekly chart down to and including
the nominal 10-week wave, as in the copper example of the last section.
Similarly, a daily chart would ordinarily be phased down to and including
the nominal 10-day wave. However, on occasion it will be found necessary
to continue the extension effort to include the nominal S-week or 5-day
wave in order to obtain a fully phased wave.

 

‘There may even be times when the S-week or 5-day wave cannot be com-
pletely phased. However, it is impractical to attempt to phase waves shorter
than 5 weeks on a weekly chart or 5 days on a daily chart. Even so, in such
situations it is usually not necessary to abandon the analysis, The consid-
erations involved and the means employed in handling these occurrences
will be discussed in a later lesson, But for the present discussion, let us
assume that if the extension has been driven to the 5-week or 5-day wave,
this wave can be fully phased.

Once the shortest wave is fully phased, the task of completing a phasing
analysis consists of a mechanical process called filling the harmonic gaps.
For each case except that involving the 18-month and $4-month waves, a
trough of a given wave is assumed to exist at every other trough of the next
shorter wave. Thus, the Principle of Harmonicity is applied directly to

the completion of the phasing analysis. Missing diamonds for successively
longer waves are supplied on the evidence of shorter wave trough locations
coupled with knowledge of harmonic p relationships.

 

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section eleven
where this process will be applied to the copper example of section ten.

In the last section you participated in the process of phasing analysis as.
applied to a weekly Cyclitec chart,

‘You have seen how the process of phasing analysis consists of an entry,
successive steps of extension, and completion. There are several modes of
entry possible and several successive steps to extension aad completion.

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS continued
Throughout the process, basic cyclic concepts and Periodogram informa-
tion (in the form of an initial cyclic model) are linked to the composite

price wave through the price-movement simplifying function of the Dom-
inaney Envelope and its centerline.

You will be exposed to the phasing analysis of the daily chart of the copper
example in a few minutes, but first, let's add a few new ideas that will fur-
ther increase your skill in phasing analysis.

 

The process of phasing analysis is an inexact art — and full recognition of
this fact is essential to success. It is a physical impossibility to have at
hand perfect information on both wave phase and price-time movements
at one and the same time. This is not due to faults in methods, but is re~
lated to mathematical law.

As a consequence, the process of phasing analysis — which is the process
of isolation of individual wave components and generation of information
regarding their phase status — can never be exact. The purpose of a phas-
ing analysis is to form best estimates as to which waves are active and
whether they will be acting to elevate or depress prices in the near future.

 

The point to be made is that the most workmanlike and effective job of
phasing analysis that is possible should be done, but that excessive con-
cern regarding the absolute correctness of the results should be avoided.

Experience shows that a wave equivalent to each wave in the nominal model
is always at work in price movement. The Principle of Variation tells us
that there will be times when one particular wave is weak and ineffectual,
but this does not negate its presence. As a consequence, phasing must
always be accomplished for a current-time equivalent of each wave in the
nominal model, A chart should never be phased as though one or more of
these waves were not present.

 

 

Remembering that the objective of phasing analysis is to estimate the time
positions of wave troughs by visually identifying them in price action, the
analyst must always be aware of the distinction between wave troughs

and crests and the associated price lows and highs. The composite wave

that is price movement is very complex. The combination of simple price
waves by summation to form total price movement results in time differences

CONSIDERATIONS AFFECTING PHASING ANALYSIS continued
between wave troughs and price lows, and between wave crests and price
highs.
This phenomenon of time differences is called time translation. When time
translation is related to wave troughs and price lows it is called low ¢rans-
lation. When related to wave crests and price highs it is called high transla-
tion. Time translation will be discussed and exemplified in the text and
can be of considerable use in the process of phasing analysis.
You should turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section
twelve.
EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING
ANALYSIS
‘The purpose of this lesson is to provide you with the basic capability of
setting up a current cyclic model. The process involves interpreting a Period-
ogram; combining this information with the Principle of Nominality to
obtain an initial eyclic model; entering, extending and completing a phas-
ing analysis; then extracting information from the phasing analysis to
permit formation of the current cyclic model.

 

Information taken from a phasing analysis is of two kinds; that which
pertains to the imminence, type, and desirability of a potential trade, and
that which results in a current cyclic model. The information extracting
process is much the same for both.

The first step is to determine the average of the wave-period samples found
in the phasing analysis. This is best done by starting with the shortest
wave phased because it will have numerous wave-period samples available.

    

To find the average sample length, note the grid line number of the phasing
diamond furthest to the right. From this, subtract the grid line number of
the phasing diamond furthest to the left. Divide the result by the number
of period samples represented by that particular row of diamonds.

The result is the best available estimate of the current period of that wave
and is suitable for inclusion in the current cyclic model.

This first step is repeated for each wave phased.

The second step provides estimates of trough locations in future time for

SS Pre 139


—— Page 140.

     
    

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

EXTRACTING INFORMATION FROM A PHASING
ANALYSIS | continued

each of the waves phased. The average period for a given wave is laid out
on a measuring strip. Then the length of each period sample for the wave
i also laid out on the strip, one on top of the other, measured from a com-
mon reference mark. When complete, this strip contains the length of the
average period and an indication of the range of expected variation. The
reference mark on the strip is then placed at the most recent phasing
diamond of the wave, and, measuring into future time, the average period
and range of variation are transferred to the chart. This is repeated for
each wave, and the result is a graphical presentation of the future times

at which nests-of-lows of varying effectivity are likely to occur.

Finally, the time elapsed from the most recent phasing diamond for each
wave to current time is noted and entered as the phase of the wave in the
current cyclic model. These entries permit notation (by arrow) of the phase
status or current impetus of each wave.

You should turn your tape unit off now, and return to the text at section
thirteen for an example and discussion of the process of extracting infor-
mation from a phasing analysis.

CONCLUSION

Now that you have completed your assignments for lesson three, a cele-
bration is in order! Lessons two and three comprise the bulk of the new
knowledge needed to make use of the powerful tools of eyclie analysis in
trading and investing. You have been exposed briefly to a large body of
new ideas, and you probably wonder whether it is ever possible for you to
actually learn to apply these ideas in an efficient and effective manner.

Rest assured; it is not as large a task as it seems.

In lesson two you gained an overall picture of how cyclic concepts can be
used to arrive at transaction decisions. But that lesson left you with the
“spark-plug” missing — how to form a current cyclic model.

So it was necessary in lesson three to provide you with a basic understand-
ing of this important process.

You do not need to feel that you must have gained a facility for perform-
ing all the operations described in lessons two and three at this time. This

REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION

© Lesson one described the existence and characteristics of waves in equity
price movement.

@ Lesson two demonstrated how knowledge of these waves and their char-
acteristics can be used to generate transaction-decision information —
provided that a current cyclic model is available.

© Lesson three presented the techniques for assembly of the current cyclic
model.

© The material presented in these three lessons constitutes an overview of
cyclic concepts as applied to the price movements of negotiable equities.
It is far from complete, however, and applications to actual transactions
should be postponed until the entire course has been completed.

© The material of lessons one, two, and three is very fundamental and you
should be able to draw upon it by instinct. The overview summary of the
following paragraphs is intended to help you place the ideas studied so far
in proper perspective. You should augment your study of this section by
referral to previous lessons as you find it necessary or desirable to do so.

@ The price movements of all negotiable equities have a great deal in com-
mon. This is the basis for the very important Principle of Commonality
on which the entire process of cyclic analysis depends.

 

© These common elements are described by seven fundamental principles
which should be memorized. They are the Principles of Cyclicality, Sum-
mation, Harmonicity, Synchronicity, Proportionality, Nominality, and
Variation.

 

© The essential characteristics described by these principles can be stated in
condensed form as follows:

1. All price movement consists of the sum of individual price waves.

2. All price waves tend to have periods that are related by a small,
whole number; tend to have amplitudes that are proportional to per-
iod; and tend to have simultaneous troughs wherever possible.

3. A specific set of waves tends to appear in the price movements of all
negotiable equities.

tr.

th H A

 

 

4

 


 

  

reer

4

  

   

   

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued
4. The waves active in a given equity at a given time tend to exhibit
variation from ideal wave expectations.

© The basic principles do not provide transaction information directly.
Instead, they give rise to a set of methods and techniques known as cy-
clic analysis.

© Cyclic analysis is the means whereby a potential transaction can be com-
pletely evaluated before transaction entry. Such evaluation is best accom-
plished before the price reversal that makes the transaction possible.

© A transaction evaluation is achieved through a succession of intermediate
processes:

1. Separation of price movement into component price waves

2. Assembly of a current cyclic model

3. Determination of the transaction price objective and stop-loss level
4

. Determination of a transaction entry price consistent with an accep-
table potential-risk ratio and potential percentage profit

5, Selection of an action signal which suitably confirms expected price-
wave action.

© The tools of cyclic analysis include a new definition of trend; the rules for
construction and use of valid trend lines (VTL's); and the rules for con-
struction and interpretation of future lines of demarcation (FLD's).

 

© The principal use of trend is to determine whether an equity should be
bought or sold short.

© A primary use of VTL's is locating wave troughs in price movement.
© A primary use of FLD’s is making price projections.

© In addition to these primary usages, each of these analysis tools has many
subsidiary uses.

© Price movement is separated into component waves by formal phasing
analysis, or FPA for short.

REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued

© FPA is accomplished using an initial cyclic model, the Periodogram, the
Dominaney Envelope, and the Domirancy Envelope centerline.

©@ An initial cyclic model is a tabulation of wave period pairs derived from
the nominal cyclic model and interpretation of the Periodogram.

© The Periodogram is a plot of wave amplitude versus period. It is inter-
preted with the aid of the Principles of Nominality and Harmonicity.

 

© The Dominancy Envelope and its centerline function as a period-divider.
They serve as a bridge between the initial cyclic model and raw price data
by focusing attention on simplified composite price waves.

© An FPA provides a best estimate of the time positions of all troughs of
all waves of the initial cyclic model. This is done in four ordered steps:

1. Entry

2. Extension

3. Completion

4, Information extraction.

© An FPA entry is effected by locating the time positions of one or more
troughs of a relatively long wave. The two most commonly used entry
methods are:

1. Use of oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline to locate
troughs of the pre-dominant wave

2. Use of FLD’s in past time to identify trough locations of any rela-
tively long wave.

© FPA extensions take place in four ordered steps:
1. Direct application of the Principle of Synchronicity

2. Search for wave troughs between consecutive troughs of the next
longer wave

3. Search for wave troughs to either side of the time positions of isola-
ted wave troughs of the next longer wave

ATT]
Ha

 

 

ery

Br

 

 


 
 
   

   

CYCLITEC
SERVICES

TRAINING
COURSE©

ne

REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued

4, Search for wave troughs by direct comparison of best wave period
estimates with raw price data.

@ FPA completion takes place in two ordered steps:

1. Gap filling for the shortest wave phased to obtain a complete set
of trough position estimates for this wave

2. Harmonic gap filling for successively longer waves.
© EPA information extraction takes place in four steps:

1. The wave-period samples are averaged to obtain an estimate for the
period of each wave.

2. This period estimate is used in conjunction with last known wave
trough positions and the period-sample variations from average
period to establish the time of probable future nests-of-lows.

3. Wave phase is determined as the elapsed time from the last known
wave trough position to present time.

4, Current-time wave direction is derived directly from wave phase in-
formation and known wave characteristics.

© Three general rules apply to FPA effort:

1. FPA extension always proceeds from long waves to successively
shorter ones.

2. FPA completion always proceeds from the shortest wave phased to
successively longer ones.

3, The wave trough samples for which the most supporting evidence ex-
ists are always phased first. Ambiguous situations are left for resolu-
tion later in the analysis when more supporting evidence may be
available.

© An FPA results in three cyclic analysis essentials:

1. A graphical representation of the estimated time positions of all
wave troughs in the form of phasing diamonds

3. The information required for assembly of the current cyclic model.

@ A current cyclic model is an organized tabulation of most of the informa-
tion extracted from an FPA. It consists of:

3.

4.
5.

The estimated direction of ¥L, which is the trend underlying the
longest wave phased in the FPA

The nominal cyclic model wave periods paired with the wave-period
sample averages of the FPA

‘The phase of each wave in terms of time elapsed since the last known
wave trough

The current-time direction of action of each wave

The FLD number for each wave based upon the FPA average period.

© The current cyclic model provides immediate information related to the
imminency and magnitude of potential price reversals.

© Even more important, the current cyclic model provides the basis for iden-
tification of valid trend lines, for FLD construction, and for future trend
estimation.

© The current cyclic model permits a complete VTL, FLD analysis, includ-
ing multiple FLD pattern interpretation. This results in establishment of a
transaction objective and a logical stop-loss level.

© Once an objective and a stop-loss level have been established, a transac-
tion entry price can be determined for which potential-risk ratio and gross
potential percentage are acceptable.

 

© The FPA, the current cyclic model, and specific transaction conditions
are used to determine the most effective action signal.

© The cyclic analysis processes which follow FLD pattern interpretation are
referred to as setting up the transaction.

TWO KINDS OF RISK
© There are two kinds of risk associated with a transaction:

1. Risk potential, which is an estimate of the amount of capital that
will be lost if a transaction is stopped out instead of proceeding as
expected to produce a profit

2. Transaction risk, which is the percentage probability that the trans-
action will be stopped out at a loss instead of terminated at a profit.

© Risk potential can be estimated with reasonable accuracy before transac-
tion entry.

© Transaction risk cannot be evaluated as clearly and objectively. Never-
theless, there is much the cyclic analyst can do to obtain strong impres-
sions of the degree of transaction risk involved in a given situation,

@ There are two basic approaches to assessing transaction risk:

1, For each wave situation that is considered during the cyclic analysis,
examine evidence produced by several different analysis methods,
Conclusions drawn from well confirmed evidence lead to lower risk
transactions than do conclusions for which some evidence is con-
flicting.

2. Take into account the types of wave situations that give rise to low
or high transaction risk conditions.

© Before discussing some of the wave situations that affect transaction risk,
it is necessary to define the terms edge-band and mid-band. These terms
originated with early cyclic analysis methods. The methods are now obso-
lete, but the terms persist and are useful, as explained in the following
paragraphs.

@ Each wave in a cyclic model can be used as the basis for an envelope
similar to the Dominancy Envelope (and similarly functioning as a period
divider). Such envelopes have a constant vertical width when drawn on a
chart and are also called channels. Each such channel just contains nearly
all of the price movement of the price wave on which it is based.

@ The constant vertical width of such a channel is called a band.

 

 

Pr

Page 6 —


  

  

   

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

a nt

TWO KINDS OF RISK continued

© When price movement is at or near the top or bottom of a channel it is
said to be in an edge-band state.

   

© When price movement is at or near a point half way between the top and
bottom of a channel it is said to be in a mid-band state.

© The following wave situations are among those that affect transaction risk.

1. Edge-band transactions are subject to more risk than mid-band
transactions

 

2. Long transactions entered near nests-of-lows that contain long waves
wolve increased transaction risk. If the nest contains waves that are
eighteen months and longer, the risk is particularly high.

3. Short transactions near expected price tops involve an especially
high degree of transaction risk.

© Transaction risk is also a function of the skill of the eyclic analyst and
the degree and quality of emotional control that the trader or investor is
able to exercise during a transaction.

© Situations affecting transaction risk will be pointed out and emphasized
throughout the workshops of this and subsequent lessons.

HOW TO PREDICT TREND

© Once a current cyclic model is in hand, it is theoretically possible to esti-
mate the current and future direction of the trend underlying all of the
waves in the model. It is seldom useful to do this, but it is often desirable
to form such an estimate for one or several of the waves of the model.

 

The means of estimating trend is provided by a simple technique based on
the Principle of Proportionality.

© The technique makes use of a particular phenomenon, stemming from
proportionality, that is demonstrated in figure 4-1.

© In this figure, four ideal, proportional waves are shown. The waves selec-
ted are also harmonically related, as they would be in a cyclic model; but
this is not a necessary condition for the phenomenon under discussion.
‘The important factor is that the waves are proportional. For the purpose
of the illustration, the phases of the waves are adjusted so that they all
cross the zero axis at the same point (P).

© The slope, or slant, of each wave as it passes through point P is a meas-
ure of the largest price change per unit of time (day, week, month, etc.)
that each wave is capable of producing. It can be seen from the figure that
this slope is exactly the same for each wave no matter what the wave am-
plitude and period is.

© This means that a 7-day ideal wave with an amplitude of 5 price units is
capable of producing just as rapid a price change, say, as a 70-day wave
with an amplitude of 50 price units. (Note that proportionality has been
carefully maintained in this example.)

 

© The resulting technique used in estimating trend is not seriously in error
when applied over a full half cycle of each wave, as indicated by the
dashed line of figure 4-1. In fact, the error involved is less than that expec-
ted to occur as a result of variation from Harmonicity and Proportionality.

For an estimate of trend at current time, the technique is used as follows:

1. From the current cyclic model, find the direction of action of each
wave longer than the wave the desired trend underlies.

2. Find the difference between the number of up arrows and down


     
 

  

  

 

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

Pn

HOW TO PREDICT TREND continued

arrows, ignoring bottoming or topping symbols and considering only
the waves longer than the wave the desired trend underlies.

 

3, If there are more up arrows than down arrows the desired trend is
considered to be up.

4, If there are more down arrows than up arrows, the desired trend is
considered to be down.

5. The strength of the trend is measured by the net number of arrows

left after the number of arrows in one direction are subtracted from
the number of arrows in the other direction.

© Given a current cyclic model, an estimate can be formed for any desired
trend at any desired future time by the following procedure:

1. Add the desired number of days, weeks, or months of future time to
the phase of each wave that is longer than the wave the desired
trend underlies.

2. Using this modified phase information, reevaluate each wave direc-
tion at the new time.

3. To establish the new estimate of trend direction, find the difference
between the number of up arrows and the number of down arrows
at the new time.

4. Repeat this process for as many future times as needed.

© As an example assume that 20 days is the period-sample average for one
of the waves of a current cyclic model. Suppose further that at present
time this wave is seen to be 6 days along from the last known trough.
Then current and near-future wave directions would be estimated as fol-
lows:

1. At current time the 20-day wave is 6 days along. Wave direction is +.

2. Two days in the future the 20-day wave is 8 days along. Wave direc-
tion is +.

3. Four days in the future the 20-day wave is 10 days along. Wave
direction is ~~.

4. Six days in the future the 20-day wave is 12 days along, Wave direc-
tion is}.

© For each wave longer than a selected wave, this process can be completed
for several future times over the desired future time interval. Then, for
each future time investigated, the difference between the up and the down
arrows (including the 2L arrow) can be determined. From these differ-
ences, an estimate can be made of the changes in trend strength and di-
rection over the desired future time interval.

duced below.

© In your estimates you should have ignored ¥L (in this instance) since its
direction is now flat and is not expected to change during the next six
weeks. (The shortest wave in the 3L composite is at least twice as long
as 18 months and is therefore changing very slowly.)

@ In the current cyclic model, the 24.1 week, 46.3-week, and 92.6-week
waves are shown with phase times longer than their average periods. This
means that the probability is high that the troughs have actually occurred
and that by the time another week has passed, the bottoming action will
have been completed. We therefore consider that at the + 1 W time, the
direction of these three waves is up and will remain so for one half cycle.
To carry this assumption forward into the additional future weeks, we need
to re-express the phase of these waves in terms of the new assumption. We
find that we have two alternatives. One is to assume that the troughs
occurred at the same time as the 95.3-day trough. The other, which is less
favorable to the transaction because the next crests will occur earlier,
is to assume that the troughs occurred an average wave period from the

last known low. The latter assumption is used because it is more con-

© The 95.3-day wave presents no special problem since it is shown at now

© For the first four weeks into the future, all four waves longer than the
47.7-day wave are estimated to be up, providing a net result of + 4 (four
up arrows). This result implies a very strong upward trend underlying the
47.7-day wave during this future time interval,

 

© In the fifth week, the 24.1-week wave is topping and is therefore making
no contribution to trend, either up or down. The net result is + 3 (three up
arrows and one flat arrow).

© In the sixth week, the 95.3-day and 24.1-week waves have topped out and
are now down, but the 46.3-week and 92.6-week waves are still up. The
net effect is zero (two up arrows and two down).

© In considering these results, we must remember that trend (as a compos-
ite wave) shows rounded tops just as total price movement does. (There-
fore, it is not likely that the trend we are analyzing will be strongly up for
five weeks and then abruptly change to flat, We conclude that inherent
inaccuracies in our rule-of-thumb technique are resulting in an inexact
picture. The best we can say is that for the next three or four weeks the
trend underlying the 47.7-day wave is likely to be strongly up. Then for
the next few weeks the upward strength is likely to diminish until the
trend is nearly flat.

© The estimate just discussed would have been made on the 24th of Novem-
ber 1972. Then, as new wave troughs came about and were identified, the
estimate would have been revised. In this way, by the time trend was due
to slow down or reverse, estimates of greatly increased accuracy would
have been available.

ACTION SIGNALS REVISITED

@ The concept behind an action signal is that future price movement must
confirm that predicted wave events are actually coming about before ac-
tion is initiated in a transaction,

@ An action signal can consist of any price movement that an analyst is
willing to accept as sufficient evidence of the confirmation of expected
wave events.

© There are many types of future price events that can be chosen to serve
as action signals, but the most commonly used are:

1. A price break of a VTL
2. A price break of an FLD
3. A price break of a horizontal price line.

© Action signals may be used in many ways. A stop-loss level is an action
signal. Other price events can be used for buy signals, sell-short signals,
protect-profit signals, or just for alerting, warning, or giving assurance.

@ For example, a decision could be made to enter or terminate a transac-
tion when an action signal is triggered (or as soon thereafter as possible),
Or the decision might be made to let the action signal serve as an alert
to reassess the situation and to initiate transaction action only if certain
additional criteria are met.

© In general, risk evaluation is associated with action signal selection. The
action signal concept is itself intended to reduce transaction risk but this
is sometimes accomplished at the expense of potential-risk ratio,

© Numerous examples of action signal selection will be presented in the
workshops along with the cyclic logic that leads to the proper choice.

THE TWO-FINGER TECHNIQUE

© Formal cyclic analysis techniques such as those presented so far in the
course are essential to the transaction decision process. But methods of
extracting information much more rapidly are also needed even if the re-
sults are not so precise.

© Rapid methods are necessary for several reasons, Among these is the need
to scan the charts of a number of equities in a short space of time in the
search for situations which warrant the expenditure of additional analysis
time and effort.

© A method called the nvo-finger technique is common to all rapid informa-
tion extraction processes. The technique consists of using two fingers of
either hand to measure time or price activities on a chart instead of the
more accurate but slower measuring strip. A little practice is needed, but
the necessary skill is quickly acquired.

© Approximate averaging of several time or price samples is obtained by
making finger position adjustment as the hand is moved from sample to
sample.

  

© The two-finger technique is primarily used in the processes of:
1. Determining the trading cycle
2. Extracting information from past-time FLD objectives
3. Rapid scanning in either of two modes.

© These processes will be discussed in detail and separately in sections six,
seven, and eight.

© In figure 4-2 the two-finger technique is illustrated as being used to obtain
rapid wave-period estimates. The finger positions shown are measuring
the time interval between the nests-of-lows at about grid line 26 and the
nest-of-lows at 56, The hand would then be moved to compare this meas-
urement with the next period sample between grid lines 56 and 84, adjus-
ting finger spacing to accomplish rough averaging in the process. This
would be repeated for all other visually evident samples, and then the
hand would be moved to the time scale to note the resulting average
period estimate in time units.

THE TWO-FINGER TECHNIQUE continued
©@ In this case the estimate would be read as “about 32”. A glance at the
Periodogram confirms the fact that this is a reasonable estimate.

© Figure 4-3 demonstrates the two-finger technique as applied to estimating
the period of the next longer wave. The Ist and 4th fingers are used here.

@ Still longer time measurements are made as shown in figure 4.4.

© Figure 4-5 shows the two-finger technique being used to measure the price
move associated with the composite wave consisting of the 32-day wave
(identified in figure 4-2) and all shorter waves. Such measurements would
be averaged by finger spacing adjustment, and the result would be trans-
ferred to the price scale to determine the number of price units repre-
sented. This process will be further described in section six in connection
with trading cycle selection.

© In figure 4-6 the two-finger technique is being used to trace FLD’s in
past time. This procedure will be further described in section seven which
covers the use of past time FLD objectives in the transaction decision-
making process.

  

 

© The two-finger technique is easily learned but does require some practice
in holding the fingers at a constant spacing. Once acquired, this skill
will be found indispensable in a whole series of rapid information extrac-
tion modes.

THE TRADING CYCLE CONCEPT

© Once a current cyclic model is available, it is theoretically possible to es-
timate the future trend underlying each wave in the model. Practically,
however, it is not necessary to do this.

© There is always one particular wave in a cyclic model which has special
meaning in relation to a potential transaction. This wave is called the
trading cycle, and it is most important to identify this wave and to esti-
mate the future trend underlying it.

@ The trading cycle is the longest wave in the composite wave that is de-
pended upon to move prices from the transaction entry value to the price
objective. The trend underlying the trading cycle is then the sum of all
longer waves in total price movement, and this trend either aids or oppos-
es the price move required to meet the objective.

© In most cases a.minimum acceptable gross price-move percentage must be
set before the trading cycle can be defined. This can be any value consis-
tent with investment goals but should not be less than 20 per cent.

 

© The process of selecting the trading cycle varies slightly depending upon
the state of the cyclic analysis at the time a selection is made.

©@ Ifa trading cycle is to be selected prior to a phasing analysis, two-finger
techniques are used to identify several key waves and their troughs.

© Then two-finger techniques are used to estimate the percentage price
moves associated with candidate trading cycles until one is found that
provides the required minimum gross price-move percentage. For any
gross price-move percentage within reason, the Principle of Proportion-
ality assures us that if a given wave will not fulfill the requirements, a
longer one can be found that will.

© If a phasing analysis is available, the process of selecting the trading cycle
proceeds as before except that two-finger identification of wave compon-
ents and their troughs is not needed.

© If a price objective has been set, the procedure is slightly different; the
minimum gross price-move percentage criterion has already been ap-
plied. In this case, information from the phasing analysis and current cyc-
lic model and the wave characteristics used to establish the price objectives

 

Page 22mm

  


     

  

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

2 etl

THE TRADING CYCLE CONCEPT continued
are employed to determine the longest wave responsible for producing the
predicted price move. This longest wave is then accepted as the trading
cycle.

 

 

© Once a trading cycle has been selected, the following policies take effec

1. A shift to a longer trading cycle should not be made during the
course of any one transaction.

2. The future course of the trend underlying the trading cycle should be
used as an aid in assessing transaction risk.

3. The trading cycle is used as the basis for setting the stop-loss level.

4. If there is evidence that the price low or high associated with the
trading cycle trough or crest that was to produce the price objective
has passed without the price objective having been met, transaction
termination procedures should be initiated immediately.

   

© Figure 4-8 (in section nine) is a daily chart of Homestake mining as of
22 December 1972. Use this figure to practice the two-finger techniques
for determining a trading cycle in the absence of a phasing analysis as
follows:

1. Identify a wave component having a period greater than 20 days and
less than 40 days. Form an estimate of the wave period and of the
trough locations.

Form an estimate of the average percentage price move associated
