USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS
© FLD’s in past time are used for several purposes:

1. To help locate wave troughs in price movement during a phasing an-
alysis (as described in lesson number three, section seven)

2. To determine whether past FLD price objectives exist which can in-
fluence future transaction decisions

3. To provide evidence of trend direction that may influence future
transaction decisions.

© Past time FLD's used for such purposes are constructed according to
slightly different rules than are future time FLD’s. These differences are
due to a lessened need for accuracy. Construction and usage are as fol-
lows:

1, When possible, FLD numbers are based on the best estimate from
the current cyclic model.

2. In computing the FLD number, the one is dropped. That is, the
FLD number is simply the wave period divided by two.

3. The FLD number is not rounded to a whole number.

4. A length of time equal to the FLD number is laid out on a measur-
ing strip.

5. This strip is moved horizontally in past time with the left-hand mark
passing generally through the price average marks. Then the path of
the right-hand mark on the strip traces out where the FLD would
normally be drawn if we were working in future time.

6. The two-finger technique described in section five may be used in
place of the measuring strip method whenever appropriate.

7. The FLD is not drawn on the chart. Instead its path is simply ob-
served until price intersection occurs, Then the time and price at the
intersection are marked on the chart.

8. In this way, FLD location of past wave troughs and crests can be
swiftly accomplished, and past time FLD price objectives can be
noted.

pe 4


         

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

LESSON

-

  

fh

USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS continued

9, Extreme accuracy is not necessary in such a search, so small oscilla-
tory price movements can be ignored.

  

© FLD price objectives in past time are classified as either retired or out-
standing. A classification of retired means that the objective in question is
unlikely to influence future price moves. A classification of outstanding
means that the objective in question may still influence future price
moves.

  

© FLD price objectives in past time are retired when evidence is obtained
that the price high or low associated with the wave crest or trough that
was to drive prices to the objective has been passed. Such evidence is ob-
tained in one or more of three ways:

1. The FLD that produced the price objective is subsequently broken
by prices in the opposite direction. This evidence is strengthened if the
break occurs during the time period when it would normally be expee-
ted if the crest or trough in question has indeed been passed.

2. A valid trend line based on the wave just shorter than the wave on
which the price-projecting FLD is based is broken in such a way as to
indicate that the wave crest or trough in question has been passed.

If trend is very strong, a full cycle of the wave on which the FLD is
based may pass without a VTL or FLD break occurring. When such

a full cycle has taken place and is confirmed by the phasing analysis,

the evidence is considered sufficient to retire the FLD price objective

in question. This evidence is strengthened if prices then proceeded to
form a higher high or a lower low than the price assumed to be associ-
ated with the wave crest or trough that was to drive prices to the FLD
price objective in question.

 

(If the strong trend is aiding the move, it is quite likely that the ob-
jective will have been met or exceeded.)

© All past time FLD price objectives that cannot be retired by any of these
three criteria must be considered as outstanding at present time.

© The impact of all outstanding FLD price objectives on future price

USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS continued

movement is noted and made a part of the analysis involved in setting up the
transaction.

@ At the same time that past time FLD price objectives are categorized as
retired or outstanding, useful trend information can be obtained.

dual FLD

 

© This is due to the fact that trend affects the accuracy of indi
price projections. The applicable rules are:

1, Ifthe trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based is flat,
then an FLD objective is likely to be rather exactly met by price.

2. Ifthe trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based opposes
the price move projected by the FLD, the price move is likely to fall
short of the FLD objective.

3. If the trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based favors
the price move projected by the FLD, the price move is likely to over-
shoot the FLD objective.

© These rules can be inverted and used to extract trend information from
past time FLD projections in the following manner.

© First, determine all past FLD price objectives for the wave of interest over
the past time interval of interest. Then note whether or not actual price
movement undershot, met, or overshot the objectives. Draw the appropri-
ate conclusion about the direction of the trend underlying the wave of inter-
est using the rules just given. Compare the conclusion with estimates for
this trend direction derived in other ways.

  

© Trend direction information obtained in this manner is made a part of the
analysis involved in setting up a transaction. It is very useful for resolving
ambiguities in a phasing analysis, estimating EL, and detecting evidence
of trend reversals not discernable in other ways.

RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION METHODS

© A complete cyclic analysis can require up to one or one-and-one half hours
and should always be accomplished before entry into a transaction.

 

© Itis generally impractical to do a complete cyclic analysis each time infor-
mation is desired on an equity. This is true for two reasons:

1. A number of equities must be kept under analysis at all times in order
to provide enough transaction opportunities to keep funds working.

 

2. Analyses must be redone on a regular basis for each equity followed
so that estimates of wave status can be revised and improved on the
basis of new price events and updated analysis aids such as the
Periodogram.

© For these reasons it is desirable to have methods available for extracting a
large part of the wave information needed in each situation in a less exact,
but much more rapid manner.

© Such methods are generally called rapid scan techniques, and there are two
distinct versions available:

1. Very rapid scan, spoken of as VRS analysis, requires on the order of S
minutes to complete and is most often used for selecting from a group
of equities those on which additional effort is warranted.

2. Rapid scan, spoken of as RS analysis, requires on the order of 20
minutes to complete and is used to identify those equities in which a
potential transaction is near at hand.

@ The process referred to so far as complete cyclic analysis is often abbrevi-
ated to CCA. CCA is most useful for two purposes:

1. Gaining the experience and expertise needed to permit extraction of
a maximum of information from RS and VRS.

2. A final evaluation in the process of setting up a transaction, to be
completed prior to transaction entry.

© Expertise in cyclic analysis is gained in much the same way as in activities
requiring complex muscular coordination. Formal, step-by-step procedures
are learned and practiced until they become instinctive, At some point the

RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION METHODS continued zz
whole process “clicks”, and wave status insight becomes available with ze
ever decreasing expenditure of time and effort. i :

© Through consistent practice in CCA, more and more effective results are as
obtained from RS and VRS. 3

@ VRS procedures are summarized as follows: F

1. Two-finger measurements are taken from low to low and high to high
of the Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuations.

2. This rough measurement is transferred to the chart time scale, and
the approximate pre-dominant wave period is read from the scale.

3. This period estimate is checked for reasonableness by mental compar-
ison with the nominal cyclic model and by a glance at the Period-
ogram. )

4. Then this same measurement is employed as the FLD number for the
wave next longer than the pre-dominant wave.

5. Using two fingers instead of a measuring strip, the past time and
future time FLD’s for this wave are traced on the chart.

6. NoFLD marks are made on the chart except for a very rough indi-
cation of the FLD track in future time.

7. As past time breaks are noted, the associated troughs and crests are
marked, rough price projections are made (still using two fingers),
and the outstanding price objectives determined.

8. This process is repeated for the pre-dominant wave using an FLD
number of one-half the period estimate.

9. By the time these two passes are complete, the wave structure becomes
quite evident, and key VTL’s can be quickly sketched in place.

10. Potential pause zones and FLD cascades are noted, as well as situa-
tions where outstanding FLD objectives imply potential VTL breaks.

11. The analysis results are examined for implications of a possible fu-
ture transaction. This leads to one of two possible conclusions: The

RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION METHODS continued

equity should be laid aside for the time being or should be scheduled
for more extensive analyses by RS or CCA.

@ RS procedures are summarized as follows:

1.
2

An initial cyclic model is prepared as for a CCA.

The initial cyclic model is used as a guide to perform a very trun-
cated phasing analysis. A phasing entry is effected via the Dominancy
Envelope centerline fluctuations, and two-finger, past-time FLD's are
used to locate key wave troughs.

Only the very most obvious and important wave troughs are located,
and no attempt is made to fill phasing gaps. The objective is to identi-
fy several clear-cut period samples for each wave.

|. Wave period samples are not averaged numerically. Instead, sample

Iengths are built up on a measuring strip which is compared to the
chart time scale. An estimate is formed of the period average by visual
inspection of the cluster of right-hand marks on the measuring strip.

|. These estimated period averages are then used as the basis for FLD

numbers.

Past-time FLD breaks are noted using a measuring strip, and the
associated wave troughs, crests, and price objectives marked. A deter-
mination is made of the price objectives still outstanding and of their
future price-change implications.

Future time FLD’s are sketched in place using the measuring strip
and ignoring price fluctuations due to significantly shorter waves. The
resulting FLD pattern is analyzed for potential cascades, pause zones,
price reversals, etc.

The diamonds of the abbreviated phasing analysis are used as a guide
for placement of key VTL's which are sketched into place. Potential
FLD-VTL interrelationships are noted (that is, situations in which

an FLD break may imply the break of an important VTL, or vice
versa).

‘The appropriate trading cycle is selected, and rough potential-risk

ratios and gross percentage profit potentials computed. Meaningful
action signals are noted, and the logical stop-loss level is selected.

9, An assessment of the transaction risk is made and compared with
that of other potential transaction candidates. A decision is made as
to whether or not the prospects warrant a CCA.

 

@ Even the first time through, it is usually surprising how firm a grasp is ob-
tained of the wave situation using VRS and RS techniques. A skilled an-
alyst using RS methods can reach the necessary transaction decisions in
most cases even without conducting a CCA.

However, it is good practice and good insurance to conduct a CCA prior
to entry of each transaction.

@ The more CCA experience an analyst has, the more information he will be
able to obtain using rapid scan techniques, and the more nearly exact the
information will be.

@ These techniques will be illustrated in the workshops to follow, which will
take the place of exercises for this section.

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE

© Most of the rest of this lesson will be devoted to learning-by-doing. This
will be the first of the course workshops. In these workshops you will exper-
ience the whole range of cyclic analysis methods applied in a wide variety of
situations,

 

 

 

© This will be the pattern of all lessons to follow: First there will be a review
of material already presented. Then new methods and concepts will be dis-
cussed. And finally, the workshops will provide the opportunity for you to
see all the cyclic analysis methods in action, The self-examinations will be
broadened to cover not only the new material of a given lesson but to re-
fresh your memory and reinforce your retention of earlier concepts as well.

© The amount of good you receive from the course is going to depend greatly
on the way in which you handle the workshops sessions. A workshop unit
will be presented to you with instructions as to what you are to do. Study the
instructions carefully, then follow them to the best of your ability at the
time. Do not be discouraged if you find this difficult at first. Do the best you
can, and then study the discussion and the correct results shown in the
figures.

 

Learn from these discussions and correct results what you should have
done, and then proceed to the next workshop unit. As you continue in this
manner you will find that the understanding you need will grow until you
can accomplish the assigned workshop tasks with ease.

@ It’s the week before Christmas, 1972. You have just opened the Cyclitec
chart mailing of 22 December. You place the new charts in your binder and
file the displaced charts away for reference.

© Now you are ready to scan the equities in the Cyclitec stable searching for
upcoming potential transactions. The word scan is used to describe a rapid
search without detailed analysis of any one situation. And the word stable
refers to the 27 stocks or 15 commodities for which graphical cyclic anal-
ysis aids are provided by Cyclitec at any one time,

© Itis clear that the rapid information extraction methods described in sec-
tion eight are ideally suited for scanning, especially the VRS approach.

© As you go through the stable applying VRS to each equity, most of the
results will be negative at any given time. That is, VRS results will indicate

© You should have started your VRS analysis on the weekly chart.

© On this chart you first observe the oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope

centerline to gain information about the pre-dominant wave. You note a
distinct low at grid line 28, an attempt at a low at 70 to 72, and another
distinct low at 127. The oscillation highs occur at 92 and 157.

@ You do not note these positions by grid line; this takes time. Instead, you

measure (roughly) between the successive lows and highs with two fingers
(of either hand). As you do this, you adjust finger spacing slightly so that
a crude average of the pre-dominant wave period is obtained.

© Next you hold the fingers rigid while dropping them to the chart time scale

for a pre-dominant wave period reading. In this case you observe that your
fingers are spread about 50 weeks apart.

@ You recognize that this is most likely the manifestation at this time, for this

stock, of the 9-month wave of the nominal cyclic model. You make a mental
note that wave periods may be running long in this situation. Further, you
glance at the Periodogram and notice a very decisive peak in the region of a
50-week period, thus confirming the validity of your two-finger measure-
ments.

he direction of this 9-month FLD is topping out about now and de-

should be bottoming out about now and should then be tending to

do not produce any more in the way of results for this wave.

© You then make an attempt to phase the 5-7 week wave. The results of this
effort are not definitive because of the unusually large number of ambigu-
ities related to the longer waves and left for resolution until now.

© Here we have a case that is strikingly different than the copper example of
lesson three, The formal rules of phasing do not even allow for completion
of this HM analysis without at least one fully-phased short wave being avail-
able. This is typical of the specific situations that the analyst faces from
time to time for which complete coverage by rules is not practical.

© Incases such as this one, two choices are available:

1. The issue can be dropped from consideration until the situation clears
up (as it will in time).

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO continued
2. We can draw upon knowledge of cyclic phenomena and see whether
enough information can be generated to complete the analysis.
In this workshop we will opt for choice number two,

© One approach would be to look for the shortest wave on which complete
phasing information is at hand, and to consider basing completion on this
wave.

© First it would be observed that the 18-month wave is fully phased, but the
ambiguous lows are bothersome in this case.

 

@ Then it would be noticed that the ambiguities do not prevent placement of a
possible 40-51 week trough at 175 (by Harmonicity, based on the possibles
at 143-148 and 175 for the next shorter wave). With this possible placed,
the 40-51 week wave is fully phased — but again the ambiguities at 23-32
and 120-127 deny us the wave period information needed, and the uncer-
tainty at 175 denies us the phase status information needed.

© Itis only when we come to review the 20-23 week wave information at hand
that a possible solution emerges. If the possible at 32 is chosen in prefer-
ence to the possible at 23, the variance for the period samples of this wave
is minimized. Similarly, the choice of the possible at 127 over the one at 120
results in very acceptable variance if the possible at 148 is selected in prefer-
ence to the one at 143 or the one at 145. To show this selection in figure
4-10, these possibles are enclosed in circles.

‘© By Synchronicity, the corresponding possibles for the 40-51 week wave are
selected and enclosed in triangles. By the same reasoning, choices are also
made for the 18-month possibles. These selections are enclosed in hex-
agons.

 

@ Now we have a reasonable phasing analysis for the weekly chart, But what
about its credibility? After all, a rather weak cyclic argument was used to
arrive at the conclusions involved.

© First of all let's examine the critical nest of possibles located at grid square
175. The last several possible trough location sets for the 10-14 week wave
do not need to be better defined in order to establish that either VTL 1 or

© This line of cyclic logic establishes phase status evidence for the 40-S1
week and all shorter waves that is quite credible.

© Next we'll examine the effect of errors in resolving the 18-month trough
location between the possibles at 120 and 127. Having established the high
probability of a 9-month trough at 175, we realize that any contemplated
near future transaction will take place while the 18-month wave is going
down regardless of how the possibles are resolved. So the estimated phase
status of this wave is independent of our choice regarding these possibles.

© Finally, we look at the impact of ambiguity resolution on period estimates.

For the 20-23 week wave, the only critical elements are the diamonds at 2
and 175. The number of samples used in the average is known, and there-
fore the period estimate is known and is completely independent of ambigu-
ity resolution at any of the locations between these two extreme diamonds.

© With this same reasoning plus the Principle of Harmonicity, we can use
one-half of the 20-23 week average period as the average period for the 10-
14 week wave and the result will be the same as if the phasing for the 10-14
week wave were fully completed.

© Doubling the 20-23 week period estimate provides a satisfactory 40-51 week
average period — again regardless of the 40-51 week trough ambiguity
resolutions affected.

© The 18-month FLD is not going to be critically in error no matter which

© You again note that the Periodogram is indicating that all waves acting
in HM at this time are considerably longer than nominal. You first observ-
ed this in the VRS analysis, then in the weekly initial cyclic model, then
in the weekly phasing analysis, and now in the daily initial cyclic model.
‘The conclusion is inescapable that all evidence points to acceptance of this
fact by now.

© You effect phasing entry via the oscillations in the Dominancy Envelope
centerline. You note a low at about grid line 40, and another at about 142 or
143. With a two-finger check of this pre-dominant wave period, you arrive at
an estimate of about 105 days. This checks well with twice fifty-two (the
next shorter wave indicated by the Periodogram) leading you to feel that,
with this observation added to all the other evidence favoring longer-than-
nominal waves for HM now, the weak 80-day Periodogram indication can
be ignored. So you conclude that the pre-dominant wave on the daily chart
is the equivalent of the nominal 80-day wave and is identical with the wave
indicated by the weekly analysis to be 12.4 weeks (or 86.5 days).

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FOUR
© Now it is time for you to pause and extract as much information as possible
from the phasing analysis and current cyclic model.

 

  

© First of all, take a look at the overall situation. For a number of months,
Prices on the weekly chart have been plunging (in fact, by a total of some
30%).

© If it were not for cyclic theory, you would have no way of knowing that the
small up-move in price over the last month is any more significant than
similar rallies that occurred all through the price decline.

© But you have a very distinct impression from the analysis to date that this is
a significant rally and that itis in fact the start of a major price reversal
which may carry prices up to the region of 34 or even beyond. Such a price
move would result in higher prices than have been seen in this issue for at
least the last 41 months!

 

@ From a wave viewpoint you strongly suspect that you've already seen the
nine-month low and that prices are in the process of completing the first
28.8-day cycle to follow. If this is so, you can expect high translation (to the
right) in the composite price wave consisting of the 28.8-day and all shorter
waves. Looking at the daily chart, you see that this high translation is
indeed occurring.

 

© Also, you saw from your phasing analysis that the valid downtrend lines
based on the 7.2-day, 28.8-day, and 57.6-day waves have all been broken in
the upward direction. This tells you that the 115.2-day (nominal 80-day)
wave and all shorter waves have formed their troughs at grid line 147.

@ Allof these considerations lead you to feel that the prospects for a success-
ful buy in HM are very good. This conviction can be further confirmed by
yet another line of reasoning. Suppose that you were conducting this
analysis of HM as of five weeks earlier. You would still have come to the
conclusion that a nine-month nest-of-lows was due very soon, As a matter
of fact you now believe that the price low associated with this nest occured
the very next Monday. Under those circumstances you could have set up a
transaction and selected an action signal. But in doing so, you would have
been fully aware that the resulting transaction carried a higher risk than
one for which more time had passed so that more definitive evidence could

@ A glance at the phasing analysis shows you that prices as of 22 December
are probably at or very near another 7.2-day. And you expect a trough of the

hard down. Therefore, a buying opportunity is very near at hand, possibly
as near as the coming Monday.

 

@ You briefly consider the problem of an action signal. In this case, you want
to select an action signal that will indicate that the 7.2/14.4/28.8-day
nest-of-lows has just passed. You note that the 7.2-day valid downtrend line
would serve this purpose very well.

© With this general overview, you are now ready to complete the analysis
preparatory to setting up an actual transaction.

@ So as your next workshop assignment, place the pertinent FLD’s on the
weekly chart, noting any outstanding price objectives. Do the same for the

@ Figures 4-12 and 4-13 show the weekly and daily charts with the desired

estimated to be occurring at current time. It is the price rise expected out of
this 3-diamond nest, augmented by the impetus from the 7-diamond nest
just 32 days ago, that arouses interest in a potential transaction.

© At this point in the analysis you begin thinking of the logical action signal
and stop-loss level to be selected if a decision is made to enter a transaction.

 

@ The manner in which the action signal is selected must offer good assurance
that when it flashes its signal to you, the expected three-diamond nest-of-

line were to be chosen, the price break of this trend line would indicate a
high probability that the next longer wave (14.4-day) had formed a trough.
This would indicate an even higher probability that all shorter waves had

© Your evaluation of these two possibilities by means of eyclic logie would

© The next step is to observe the pattern formed by the 49.4-week, 24.7-week,

© Note that the 12.4-week FLD has been broken, and the resulting price pro-
jection should break the 24.7-week FLD, especially since prices are rushing
up to meet a downsloping FLD. The 24.7-week projection should then pre-
dict prices that would break the 49.4-week FLD for the same reasons. The
resulting 49.4-week projection could put prices right in the region predic-
ted by the 98.8-week FLD break.

  

© You should note that this is a case in which well-defined pause zones have
not formed to provide guidance as to price objectives. (The PZ between
prices of 2-5/8 — 25 was of significance before the price low at grid line 175
was formed.) However, the entire set of evidence generated strongly sup-
ports the conclusion that prices are on the threshold of a move to the vi-
cinity of 34. The examination of the weekly chart that we have just condue-

ful trade. This will not always be the case, as you will find in subsequent
workshops. If the evidence shows increasing conflict as an analysis pro-
gresses, the risk associated with the trade increases; and a point can well be
reached at which a decision is made to by-pass the potential transaction.

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FIVE

© HM is now ready for the process of setting up a transaction. This consists
oft

1. Establishing a sell objective
2. Selecting the trading cycle

3. Finalizing decisions regarding the action signal to be used

4, Determining the stop-loss level

5. Determining the maximum price to be paid at transaction entry
6. Estimating the percentage potential

7. Calculating the potential-risk ratio

© The best selling objective estimate available is 34-1/2. We will therefore
use this objective for pre-trade evaluation purposes.

 

© In section 12, some initial thinking was described regarding use of the 7.2-
day valid downtrend line as an action signal. Now that the daily FLD’s are
in place, we see that the 7.2-day FLD would make a very sensitive indica-
tor of the start of the expected price move. However, using this FLD as an
action signal increases the transaction risk over that for the 7.2-day VTL
because the FLD break would imply only the 7.2-day wave reversal as op-
posed to the 14.4-day wave reversal that is implied by a break of the 7.2-day
VIL.

© Also, since an FLD is constructed on the basis of average prices, average
prices must move through the FLD to constitute a meaningful break. Thus,
the occurrence of a break on a given day cannot be determined until after
the close of trading for that day,

© On the other hand, any price move through a VTL constitutes a break, so
a VTL break can be used as an intra-day action signal.

@ Inthe case of this HM transaction, we will elect to set up both action sig-
nals, acting on the one that is triggered first. A break of the 7.2-day FLD
would signal us to enter the transaction on the day following the break. A
break of the 7.2-day VTL would signal us to make immediate transaction
entry on the same day as the break.

 

 

Page 66—


 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FIVE continued
© With these two action signals selected, our next step is to identify the trad-
ing cycle and form an estimate of the trend underlying this important wave.

© Since in this present case we established price objectives prior to trading
cycle selection, we will now base selection of the trading cycle upon the cye-

lic logic leading to the price projection rather than upon percentage price
movements.

© This logic proceeds as follows:

poarosny sw TV
-auy ‘501g S199, 161 @ NBEKECD

1. To the best of our knowledge, 2L will be up throughout the trans-
action.

. The price projection is for the price high associated with the 18-month
wave crest.

We expect this price high to occur in the upcoming second 49.4-week
cycle of the 18-month wave because of high translation to the right
(SLis up). Therefore, we expect the 18-month price high to be associ-
ated with the crest of this second 49.4-week cycle.

4. If no additional evidence were in hand, we would see the 49.4-week
wave as the longest wave of the composite wave responsible for the

expected price objective — and we would therefore call it the trading
cycle.

5. However, throughout the transaction, the 18-month wave will be act-
ing downward. Thus we expect the trend underlying the 49.4-week
wave to be flat (SL $ and 18-month }).

. Because of this flat underlying trend, we do not expect any high trans-
lation for the 49.4-week wave. Consequently, we expect the price highs
associated with the first 24.7-week cycle within the 49.4-week cycle
of interest to be about the same as for the second 24.7-week cycle.
Therefore we expect the objective price for the 18-month wave crest
to occur at the price high associated with the first 24.7-week wave
crest in our second 49.4-week cycle.

7. Finally, we note that all during this first 24.7-week cycle, the 49.4-
week wave will be acting upward. Thus we expect the trend underlying

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FIVE continued

the 24.7-week wave to be up during the transaction (3L t, 18-
month } , 49.4-week # ). We then expect high translation to the right
for this wave, which in turn leads us to expect that the price objec-

tive will be reached in the second 115.2-day cycle of the first 24.7-week
cycle.

8. This pins the trading cycle down to the 24.7-week wave.

© At this point it is possible for us to form an estimate of the transaction
turnaround time. The price objective should be met near the crest of the
second 115.2-day cycle in the current 24.7-week cycle. This would be about
18 weeks from the last trough of the 24.7-week wave which occurred 4.6
weeks ago, leaving approximately 13 weeks to go.

© Now that we know the trading cycle, we can determine the stop-loss level.

© In section 12 we did some preliminary thinking regarding the stop-loss.
level. We made the observation there that cyclic concepts provide two possi-
bilities:

1. Below the price low associated with the upcoming 3-diamond nest-of-
lows

2. Below the price low associated with the 7-diamond nest-of-lows that
occurred 4.6 weeks ago.

@ Let's review the factors involved in making a choice:

1, We believe that prices are currently going down into a 3-diamond
nest-of-lows. This price decline is viewed for the purposes of this
transaction as a buying opportunity within the rise of prices from the
last 24.7-week wave trough 4.6 weeks ago to the price objective. We
recognize this as a mid-band transaction with the attendant low risk.

 

2. Our intent in the transaction is to buy at as low a price as possible,
but to have reasonable assurance that the price-depressing 3-diamond
nest-of-lows has occurred before the buy takes place. And our action
signal selection was based on this line of reasoning.

 

3. We realize that the buy then represents an edge-band buy as far as
the 3-diamond nest-of-lows is concerned. This is associated with in-

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FIVE continued

creased risk since the action signal is less likely to prove that the desir-
ed nest-of-lows has come about.

4, In this case, the added risk of using this action signal is not very im-
portant since the whole transaction is predicated on the abundant
evidence that the 7-diamond nest-of-lows containing the trading cycle
trough has indeed taken place 4.6 weeks ago.

5. However, placing the stop-loss level below the price low associated
with the 3-diamond nest-of-lows—based only on the evidence of the
action signal—means that the stop-loss signal could well be triggered
just before prices turn around and move up to the objective.

6. The proper place for the stop-loss level is, therefore, just below the
price associated with the last 24.7-week wave trough—exactly as trad-
ing cycle theory would indicate.

® This places the correct stop-loss level at 21-3/8. We note that while this
choice decreases the transaction risk, it also decreases the PR ratio. Our
alternative would be to maximize the PR ratio by placing the stop-loss level
below what is believed to be the 3-diamond nest-of-lows, knowing that
doing so increases the likelihood of a false stop-out. We do not believe that
the circumstances of this transaction warrant such a decision, but there will
be times when it is the optimum one.

© We have discussed the choice of stop-loss level at some length in this exam-
ple to emphasize the fact that it should not be placed mechanically. In most
instances, as in this one, the selection of the trading cycle will point to the
optimum stop-loss level. However, the choice should not be made without
thorough review of the alternatives available.

© We have set the sell price at the price objective of 34-1/2, and we have set
the stop-loss level at 21-3/8, We are now in a position to investigate the
range of PR’s, buy prices, and gross percentages available.

workshop. Check your results against the correct results shown in the fol-

they are likely to be sound decisions and are well worth abiding by.

@ Figure 4-14 shows HM as of 6 April 1973. The 7.2-day FLD and VTL chos-
ens an action signal are indicated, as well as the sell objective and stop-
loss level. Let's reconstruct the events following our transaction analysis.

@ The 7.2-day VTL was broken on the Wednesday following the analysis.
During this day a buy would have been made at 24-1/4 to 24-5/8 (we will
assume 24-1/2 as a reasonable probability).

© On Friday prices dropped below the stop-loss level that would have been
associated with the suspected 3-diamond nest-of-lows. The edge-band risk
of such a course of action is now apparent.

@ The following Monday price dropped as low as 23, then reversed dir-
ection (at the true 3-diamond nest-of-lows) and proceeded upward more
or less continuously to the price objective. The price objective was met (and
exceeded) in the second 115.2-day cycle of the 24.7-week trading cycle as
predicted.

© You have just worked your way completely through the process of setting up
a long transaction for the first time. The process will be similar, although
not identical, for every long transaction you will handle. Each step was des-
cribed in great detail, and your first efforts were probably quite time-
consuming. You will find that after you have gone through several such
analyses and gained the experience and practice that they afford, the total
time required for an analysis will drop to about 40 minutes to one hour —
a very small price to pay for success!

@ There are two kinds of risk associated with a transaction:

 

1. Risk potential, which is an estimate of the amount of capital that may
be lost if a transaction is stopped out.

2. Transaction risk, which is the probability that a transaction will be
stopped out at a loss instead of terminated at a profit.

© Transaction risk is an imponderable, but a properly conducted cyclic
analysis can provide insight and “feel” for this quantity.

© Transaction risk is assessed by examining the quantity and quality of the
evidence that supports a given wave situation and by applying knowledge of
the wave situations that heighten or diminish this kind of risk.

@ Transaction risk is increased under the following circumstance:

 

1. Edge-band transaction entry

2. Transaction entry near a nest-of-lows containing long waves. The
Jonger the longest wave in the nest, the greater the risk.

3. Short transaction entry near expected price tops.
@ A transaction is said to be an edge-band transaction if it is entered near

the edge of the envelope that contains the longest wave on which price
objectives are based.

© A transaction is said to be a mid-band transaction ifit is entered near the
center of the envelope that contains the longest wave on which price objec-
tives are based.

@ Transaction risk tends to be low for mid-band transactions.

© Transaction risk is also related to the skill of the analyst and the degree
of emotional control that he exercises during a transaction.

© For ideal, proportional price waves, the maximum price change per unit
of time is the same for all waves, regardless of amplitude or period.

© This characteristic of ideal, proportional waves is the basis of a rule of
thumb used in predicting trend. This rule is:

Price waves acting in opposing directions at a given time tend to contri-
bute little change to price movement.

 

Page 75a


 

 
 

 
 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 
 

© To predict the trend underlying a given wave at some specified future time:

1. Add the specified future time increment to the phase of each wave
that is longer than the given wave.

2. Note the direction of action of each such wave at the specified future
time.

3. Determine the net difference between up action and down action,
ignoring topping, bottoming, or flat wave action directions.

4, This net up or net down action is the required estimate of the trend
underlying the given wave at the specified future time.

 

@ Anaction signal is a future price event that will confirm that anti
wave action is taking place.

pated

© Any future price event that the analyst is willing to accept as su!
evidence of expected wave action can serve as an action signal.

 

© The most effective action signals are:
1. A price break of a VTL
2. A price break of an FLD
3. A price break of a horizontal price line.

© Action signals may be set up for many purposes. Some of these ar

 

1. As stop-loss levels

2. As buy signals

3. As sell signals

As sell-short signals

As cover-short signals

As profit-preservation signals

As warnings to reevaluate a situation

ena es

As an indication that a transaction is doing well.

© Many purposes of cyclic analysis are served sufficiently well by the use of
two-finger time and price measurements. Among these purposes are:

1. Determining a trading cycle
2. Extracting information from past-time FLD objectives
3. Rapid scanning in either of two modes.

© Trading cycle is the name given to one particular wave in a cyclic model
which has special significance for a given transaction.

© The trading cycle is the longest wave in the composite wave that is depend-
ed upon to carry prices from the transaction entry price to the transaction
objective.

© The trading cycle may be determined:

1. Before a cyclic analysis

2. After a phasing analysis

3. After a complete cyclic analysis which has established a price ob-

jective.
© If the trading cycle is being identified prior to a cyclic analysis, two-finger

techniques are first used to identify key wave trough locations. Two-finger
measurements are then made of the average price moves associated with
the indicated nests-of-lows. These price moves are then used as the basis
for selecting the trading cycle.

© Ifthe trading cycle is being identified after a phasing analysis is in hand,
the same procedure is used except that two-finger trough location is not
needed.

© fan analysis has been completed to the point where a price objective is
available, then cyclic logic is used to identify the trading cycle. The current
cyclic model, the phasing analysis, and the wave characteristics used to
establish the price objective are employed to determine the longest wave
responsible for the predicted price change. This longest wave is then estab-
lished as the trading cycle.

© The minimum acceptable gross percentage price move to be used in estab-
lishing a trading cycle is a function of investment goals but should not be
less than 20 per cent.

ge


CYCLITEC
SERVICES ‘

ra

© Once a trading cycle has been selected:

1. There should be no shift to a longer wave during a given transaction

2. The trend underlying the trading cycle should be used to assess trans-
action risk

3. The trading cycle should be used as the basis for setting the stop-
loss level

4, If there is evidence that the price high or low associated with the trad-
ing cycle crest or trough that was to produce the price objective has
passed without the price objective having been met, transaction ter-
mination procedures should be initiated immediately.

© Past-time FLD price objectives can influence future trading decisions.

@ Trend underlying the wave on which an FLD is based can influence the
achievement of the FLD price objective.

1. If trend supports the price movement to the objective, the objective

is likely to be overshot.

If trend is flat, the objective is likely to be met rather exactly.
3. If trend opposes the price movement to the objective, the objective
is likely to be undershot.

‘© Observation of the manner in which recent past FLD price objectives have
been met can provide valuable information regarding trend, particularly

=L.
© Past FLD objectives are either outstanding or retired.
@ A past FLD objective is retired if:
1. The FLD that produced the price projection is subsequently broken
in the opposite direction during the expected time period.

2. A VTL based on the wave just shorter than the wave on which the
FLD is based is broken so as to indicate that the wave crest or trough
that was to have driven prices to the objective has been passed.

3. A full cycle of the wave on which the price-projecting FLD is based
passes without a VIL or FLD break taking place.

© Information can be extracted very rapidly for some purposes. The method
used is called rapid scan and there are two modes of rapid scan available:

1. Very rapid scan, or VRS, requires approximately 5 minutes and is
used to determine whether additional analysis time is warranted.

2. Rapid scan, or RS, requires about 20 minutes and is used to positive-
ly identify nearness of a potential transaction.

© VRS procedures include:

1. Two-finger identification of pre-dominant wave trough locations and
measurement of pre-dominant wave period

2. Use of the pre-dominant period estimate as the FLD number for the
next longer wave

3. Two-finger past time FLD tracings, and rough future time tracings
4, Repeat of these processes for successively shorter waves as needed
5. Summarization of the wave information gained in the VRS passes.
@ RS procedures include:
1. Preparation of an initial cyclic model
2. A partial phasing analysis based on obvious wave-period samples.
3. Rough sketched FLD’s and VTL’s
4

|. Measuring strip passes to extract information from past-time FLD’s
and their objectives

5. Trading cycle selection and rough PR ratio and gross percentage prof-
it potential determination.

© Acomplete cyclic analysis is spoken of as a CCA and is used to finalize
transaction analysis before entering into a transaction.

© The more experience an analyst gains in the use of CCA, the more infor-
mation that analyst can correctly extract from VRS and RS.

© Inconducting a cyclic analysis, whether VRS, RS, or CCA, the specific
wave questions to be answered should be kept consciously in mind.

© The most valuable process of cyclic analysis is that of building evidence.
Such evidence is developed through the application of successive and inde-
pendent analysis methods.

 

@ The application of cyclic logic is another invaluable analytical aid. The pro-
cess consists of asking the question: “In the light of basic cyclic concepts,
what does this wave evidence mean in regard to the wave problem being

solved?”

 

© The process of cyclic analysis is never mechanical. Rules and techniques
are used as guides but problem situations must be handled by falling back
on the basic cyclic principles and knowledge of price wave characteris-
ties.

 

© Difficult situations should give rise to two questions:

1. “Ifall analysis results so far are reevaluated, would there be enough
information for a transaction decision?” That is ““Does the difficult

situation need to be resolved?”

2. “If the situation must be resolved, is there any other way to apply
cyclic knowledge to solve the problem?”

@ A large part of the skill in cyclic analysis lies in the ability to discriminate

between which analysis results should cause concern and which should not.

‘A question that often helps is: “Does this wave question really have to be
resolved?”

@ Just by continuing an analysis the analyst can often resolve a question-
able situation without the need for a special effort.

ACTION SIGNAL A price break of a VTL or FLD indicating a strong like-
lihood that an anticipated price move is under way.

AMPLITUDE The size or amount of the price movement involved as a price
wave goes from trough to crest.

AUGMENTED MEASURING STRIP A measuring strip that has had peri-

od samples added to the original nominal-model and the Periodogram wave
period estimates.

 

 

AVERAGE PERIOD A wave peried estimate formed by averaging two or
more trough-to-trough measurements.

BAR-CHART A common way of plotting, or graphing, equity prices. The
high and low prices for a day are joined by a vertical straight line to show the
range over which prices traded for that day. The consecutive set of such lines
is called a daily bar-chart of that equity. Similarly, the high and low prices
for a week can be compiled to form a weekly bar-chart for the equity.

BREAK Prices break a VTL or an FLD when they pass from one side of the
VTL or FLD to the other (from above to below, or vice versa).

BOTTOMING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a
trough.

CASCADE A rippling, or one-thing-followed-by-another, effect. In cyclic
theory, the tendency under certain circumstances for an FLD break to predict
prices which will break another FLD, thus predicting still greater price moves,
ete.

CCA See complete cyclic analysis.

COMMONALITY A term indicating that price movements of negotiable
equities have similar qualities in spite of the diversity of visual appearances
of price charts.

COMPLETE CYCLIC ANALYSIS orCCA The most extensive cyclic analysis
possible. Used prior to each transaction to assure that no wave information of
importance has been overlooked. The primary means by which transaction
risk is evaluated.

COMPLETION As applied to phasing analysis, the process of filling phas-
ing gaps after entry and extension have been accomplished.

 

Page &2.

 


 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  

[Teta
ere

COMPONENT A single wave in price movement or in the composite wave
formed from several single waves.

COMPOSITE WAVE The single new form resulting from the combination
by summation of two or more price waves.

CONSECUTIVE TROUGHS Wave troughs from consecutive cycles of a giv-
en wave.

CREST or WAVE CREST The highest point on a component price wave.

CURRENT CYCLIC MODEL A tabulation of wave periods, phases, and
directions of action. The best available estimate of the values for these char-
acteristics of the waves active in the price movement of a given equity at a
given time. Usually includes FLD numbers for easy reference.

CYCLE One complete movement of a price wave from one trough to the next
consecutive trough.

CYCLICALITY Derived from the word cycle. A generic term referring to the
wave phenomena in the price action of equities.

CYCLIC ANALYSIS The means by which cyclic concepts are used to pro-
duce transaction-decision information.

CYCLIC MODEL See current cyclic model.

DIRECT COMPARISON A technique whereby available period estimates
are laid out on a measuring strip which is then visually compared with price
action,

DOMINANT WAVE or DOMINANT COMPONENT The longest wave con-
tained by the Dominancy Envelope.

DOMINANCY ENVELOPE A computer generated aid present on Cyclitec
charts. A constant vertical height envelope containing most of price move-
‘ment. Contains the dominant wave and all shorter waves. Serves as a wave-

period divider.
DOMINANCY ENVELOPE CENTERLINE A smooth curve central to the

Dominancy Envelope. The sum of all waves longer than the dominant wave.

DOWNTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price highs.
The later price high is lower than the earlier price high so that the line slants
downward to the right.

 

EDGE-BAND TRANSACTION A transaction that is entered at a major
nest-of-lows near the edge of the trading cycle channel.

EL Read sigma el. Stands for sum long. Consists of the sum of all waves
longer than the longest wave identified and phased.

ENTRY The means of starting a phasing analysis. The placement of the first
phasing diamonds of a phasing analysis.

EQUITY or NEGOTIABLE EQUITY A generic label for securities, com-
modity futures contracts, etc., traded on an auction market.

EXTENSION As applied to a phasing analysis, the means of phasing suc-
cessively shorter waves after an entry has been effected.

FLD An acronym for Future Line of Demareation. An FLD is a forward-
shifted replica of past price averages and has useful properties.

 

FLD NUMBER A number used in constructing an FLD. It is found by d
ing the period of the wave on which the FLD is based by two, then adding
one.

FLD PRICE PROJECTION The process by which an analyst can, at the time
of an FLD price break, form an estimate of the extent of the existing price
move.

FLD PRICE OBJECTIVE The price to which an analyst expects prices to go
as a result of an FLD price projection.

FORMAL PHASING ANALYSIS A complete phasing analysis with a full set
of phasing diamonds, as distinguished from a more cursory phasing analysis,
that might result from VRS or RS.

FPA. See formal phasing analysis.

FUTURE TIME POSITION A place on an equity price bar-chart corres-
ponding to some time in the future; that is, some time to the right of the last
known price bar on the chart.

_ — Poge 84,


remy CYCLITEC ©

SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

 

GAP Ina row of phasing diamonds, a space too long to represent a period
sample. On a Cyclitee chart, a space where no price bar is shown because the
market was closed that day.

GRID LINE NUMBER Refers to the time scale reading on a Cyclitec chart
corresponding with a given vertical grid line on the chart. A convenient ref-
erence for various activities displayed on a Cyclitec chart.

HARMONICITY Derived from the word harmonious or harmonic. Means
that wave periods are related by small, whole numbers.

HIGH TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price highs
versus wave crests.

INITIAL CYCLIC MODEL A tabulated set of wave periods derived from
Periodogram interpretation and the Principle of Nominality. Used as a guide
in setting up a current cyclic model.

LONG To go long in an equity is to purchase it in the expectation of selling
it later at a higher price.

LOW TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price lows
versus wave troughs.

MEASURING STRIP A strip of paper with estimated periods laid out on it.
Used in phasing analysis.

Also, a strip of paper with an FLD number laid out on it. Used for FLD
measurements.

MID-BAND TRANSACTION A transaction that is entered at a minor nest-
of-lows near the middle of the trading cycle channel.

MOST SIGNIFICANT PRICE LOW Means most visually evident. Applied
to the coarsest price movement between two consecutive wave troughs. A price
low that is made up of more coincident wave troughs than are adjacent
price lows.

NEIGHBORING As applied to waves, refers to two waves in a set of waves
whose periods are closest to being the same.

NEST-OF-LOWS. A time when two or more component price waves form
simultaneous troughs.

RELL

NOMINAL MODEL or NOMINAL CYCLIC MODEL A list of the periods
of the price waves to be expected in price movements.

NOW TIME Synonymous with current, or present, time. The time at which
an analysis is being made. The latest time for which price data is available.

 

PAST-TIME FLD An FLD plotted in the chart area representing time prior
to current time. Usually used to aid identification of wave trough location.
Uses a simplified FLD number consisting of merely one-half of the wave per-
iod estimate.

PAST-TIME POSITION A location on an equity price bar-chart corres-

ponding to some time in the past; that is, some time to the left of the last
known price bar on the chart.

PERIOD The length of time that elapses between a trough of a price wave
and the next consecutive trough of the same wave.

PERIODOGRAM A convenient means of presenting information about
many price waves simultaneously. A plot of wave amplitude versus wave
period. Consists of a series of vertical lines, each representing a potential
wave. Line height indicates amplitude, while horizontal line position indi-
cates period.

 

PERIOD SAMPLE A measurement of elapsed time between estimated posit-
ions of consecutive wave troughs.

PHASE The time relationship between two price waves.

PHASING ANALYSIS A procedure for isolating the wave components pres-
ent in a complex composite wave. A series of methods whereby wave trough
positions are estimated. The means of refining an initial cyclic model into a
current cyclic model.

PHASING DIAMOND A diamond-shaped mark placed on a chart to denote
the best estimate of the time location of a wave trough.

POTENTIAL Short for potential profit. The difference between the entry
and exit prices of a transaction expressed in price units.

POTENTIAL PERCENTAGE. Anticipated profit (potential) expressed as a
percentage of the transaction entry commitment.

=

PR An acronym for Potential-Risk ratio. ‘The ratio of potential to risk;
that is, potential divided by risk.

PREDICTION An estimate of something expected to happen in the future.
Never exact, always an expected high likelihood.

PRE-DOMINANT WAVE or PRE-DOMINANT COMPONENT The wave
just longer than the dominant wave. The shortest wave that is part of the sum
of waves comprising the Dominancy Envelope centerline.

PRICE ACTION or PRICE MOVEMENT Refers to the fact that equity
prices change constantly as time passes. The motion of prices as time goes
on.

PRICE-MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM A Periodogram constructed by
analysis of price data. Interpreted by noting the period of the tallest line of
each set that forms a peak in the Periodogram shape.

PROBABILISTIC Having to do with probabilities. Dealing with likelihood
stronger than possibility, but falling short of certainty.

PROPORTIONALITY orPROPORTIONAL TO A relationship between
two quantities such that as one increases, the other also increases, and by the
same percentage. In cyclic concepts, relates to the amplitude and period of
the various waves in a cyclic model.

PZ An acronym for Pause Zone A price region in which one or more FLD’s
tend to congregate in a horizontal band. A price region where prices tend to
slow-down or reverse a cascade movement.

RANGE OF PERIOD ESTIMATES time span corresponding to the dif-
ference between maximum and minimum estimates of the period of a given
wave.

 

RAPID SCAN or RS. A simplified method of cyclic analysis requiring about
twenty minutes.

RISK Short for Joss risk. The amount that will be lost in case the transaction
is terminated because prices penetrate the stop-loss level.

RS See rapid scan.

SHORT or SELL SHORT To sell borrowed stock in the expectation that the
price will subsequently go down. If the price does go down, a profit is made
by replacing the borrowed stock with stock purchased on the open market at
the reduced price.

STABLE A group of equity charts that are updated, followed, and analyzed
regularly.

STOP-LOSS LEVEL A special kind of action signal. A price level at which a
transaction is terminated because the probabilities no longer favor a profit-
able outcome. This action usually results in a loss on the transaction.

SUMMATION The act of summing, or adding.

SYNCHRONICITY Derived from the word synchronous or synchronize.
Means to occur simultaneously in time. In cyclic theory, the term refers to the
tendency of the troughs of one component wave to occur at the same time as
the troughs of other component waves in the same composite wave.

TIME-ALONG: A term used as a convenient measurement of wave phase.
Means time elapsed since last known wave trough.

TIME SCALE Ona Cyclitee chart, a series of numbers laid out horizontally
and corresponding to a count of the vertical grid lines. A convenient means
of referring to specific grid lines. Also a convenient means of measuring
elapsed time between marks on a measuring strip.

TIME TRANSLATION The shift that underlying trend causes in the time

position of price lows and highs so that they no longer coincide with assoc-
iated wave troughs and crests.

TOPPING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a crest.

TRADING CYCLE The longest wave of the composite wave relied upon to
take prices from the transaction entry price to the objective.

TRANSACTION RISK The probability of a transaction terminating at a loss
as opposed to terminating with a profit.

TREND Generally, the strength and direction of price movement if short
term fluctuations in price are ignored. In cyclic theory, trend is the sum of all
price waves longer in period than the specific wave for which trend is being
defined.

TREND LINE A line drawn across specially selected price highs (or price
lows).

TROUGH or WAVE TROUGH The lowest point on a component price
wave.

UPTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price lows. The
later price low is higher than the earlier price low so that the line slants up-
ward to the right.

VALID TREND LINE A trend line drawn according to specific rules so that
the result has meaning in terms of cyclic concepts. Abbreviated VTL.

VARIATION In cyclic concepts, refers to the fact that actual local wave
conditions differ from the nominal, or long-term average, expectation.

VERY RAPID SCAN or VRS A simplified method of cyclic analysis re-
quiring about five minutes.

VOLATILITY INDEX A number appearing in the heading line of Cyclitec
charts. Gives a measure of the relative volatility of the issue; that is, the per-
centage rate, or speed, with which prices for the issue tend to change.

VRS See very rapid scan.

WAVE. As applied to price movement, a repetitive fluctuation in price with
well defined, measurable characteristics.

WAVE DIRECTION The best estimate of the direction in which a given
price wave is tending to move prices at present time. Spoken of as being up,

down, flat, bottoming, or topping.

WAVE-PERIOD DIVIDER A means of separating the sum of all waves
forming a given price movement into two simplér sums of waves. Each of the
two sums consists of contiguous waves of the cyclic model. The Dominancy
Envelope and its centerline function as a wave-period divider.

 

WAVE PHASE. An indication of the location in time of the wave shape. Con-
veniently measured as time elapsed from the most recent wave trough to cur-
rent, or present, time.

Itis suggested that you go all the way through the examination a first time
without referring to the cassette or text material. In doing so, mark answers
only to the questions you are sure of; don’t guess. Leaving an answer unmark-
ed will flag that question for further attention on the second pass through the
exam.

After you have finished the first pass, check the answers you have marked
against the answer sheet, circling any that are wrong. Then go back through
the examination and refer to the text material to refresh your memory on
those questions that you answered wrong or left unanswered.

SECTION ONE REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION

Lessons one, two and three were designed to provide you with an overview
of cyclic concepts as they apply to the price movements of negotiable
equities.

 

To accomplish this purpose, lesson one described the existence and char-
acteristics of waves in equity prices. Lesson two demonstrated how know-
ledge of these waves and their characteristics can be used to generate trans-
action-decision information — provided a current cyclic model is available.
Then, in lesson three, methods of generating the current cyclic model were
discussed.

 

To achieve such an overview, a great many of the ideas and methods needed
to apply cyclic concepts in trading and investing were omitted or described
with considerable brevity. It is the purpose of the remaining lessons of the
course to round out the picture and to provide you with the all-important
application experience needed.

However, the importance of the basic concepts of the first three lessons can
scarcely be over-emphasized. You should be able to draw upon them by
instinct before the course is completed. To aid in your absorption of these
basics, we will be referring to them repeatedly in the remaining lessons,
starting with the review in this section. You are most strongly urged to study
the review with care even if you feel you already know the contents by heart.

You have learned that the price movements of all negotiable equities have a
great deal in common despite the apparent diversity in appearance displayed
by plots of price versus time. These common elements are the basis for the
all-important Principle of Commonality on which the entire process of cyclic
analysis depends.

(It should be remarked that these common elements have nothing at all to
do with those things normally thought of in conjunction with price move-
ment, such as price-earnings ratio, potential earnings growth, etcetera. It is
not that such considerations do not influence price movement, for they do,
as will be discussed in a later lesson.)

  

‘The elements that all negotiable equities have in common are described by
seven basic cyclic principles which you should commit firmly to memory.
‘These are the Principles of Cyclicality, Summation, Harmonicity, Synchron-
icity, Proportionality, Nominality, and Variation,

 

Page 9——

 

  


   

  

   

   

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

SECTION ONE REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued

The essential characteristics described by these fundamental principles can
be stated in condensed form as follows:

ry

One. All price movements consist of the sum of individual price waves.

‘Two. All price waves tend to have periods that are related by a small,
whole number; tend to have amplitudes that are proportional to
period; and tend to have simultaneous troughs whenever possible.

Three. A specific set of waves tends to appear in the price movements of
all negotiable equities.

Four, The waves active in a given equity at a given time tend to exhibit
variation from ideal wave expectations.

‘These basic principles do not provide transaction-decision information
directly. Instead, they are used to justify a body of methods and manipula-
tions collectively called cyclic analysis. The end product of cyclic analysis is
the complete evaluation of a transaction, obtained before transaction entry.
Itis perfectly possible, and indeed is the normal procedure, for such an eval-
uation to take place before the price reversal occurs that makes a trans-
action possible.

A transaction evaluation is achieved by cyclic analysis through a succession
of intermediate processes.

First: Price movement is separated into its component price waves.

Second: This separation permits the assembly of a current cyclic model.

Third: The current cyclic model makes possible an analysis which pro-
vides an estimate of the extent of the expected price move and
locates the proper placement of a logical stop-loss level.

Fourth: The availability of the price objective and the stop-loss level per-

mit selection of a transaction entry price for which the potential
for gain and the potential for loss are in acceptable balance.

Fifth: The current cyclic model also makes possible an analysis which
establishes the nature of future price movements that will con-
firm wave expectations and serve as an action signal for trans-
action entry.

SECTION ONE REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued

These results of cyclic analysis are achieved by the use of methods and ideas
firmly grounded in the basic cyclic principles. Included among these meth-
ods and ideas is a new and important definition of trend; the concept of val-
id trend lines (called VTL's) and the rules for their construction and use;
and the all-new idea of future lines of demarcation (called FLD’s) and the
rules for their construction and interpretation.

 

‘Trend is most useful in determining whether an equity should be bought or
sold short, VIL's are primarily used as an aid in establishing the location of
wave troughs in price movement. FLD’s are useful for this same purpose,
but also have the important capability of providing estimates of the extent of
price moves. In addition to these primary uses, these two important cyclic
analysis tools are used in many lesser ways.

‘The process by which price movement is separated into its component waves
is called formal phasing analysis, or FPA for short. An FPA is accomplished
with the aid of an initial cyclic model and two computerized graphic

plays, the Periodogram and the Dominancy Envelope (with its centerline).

 

The initial cyclic model consists of a tabulation of pairs of wave period esti-
mates derived from the nominal cyclic model and the Periodogram.

The Periodogram is a visual display of wave amplitude versus wave period.
Periodogram interpretation is accomplished with the assistance of the Prin-
ciples of Nominality and Harmonicity.

‘The Dominancy Envelope and its centerline serve as a period-divider which
focuses attention on two composite price waves which are simpler than the
total price movement they comprise. During the process of FPA, the Domin-
ancy Envelope and its centerline serve as a bridge between the initial cyclic
model and actual price movement.

‘An FPA results in a best estimate of the time positions of all troughs of all
waves of a cyclic model. These results are obtained through four, ordered
steps as follows:

First: An Entry into the FPA
Second: Several steps of extension of the FPA
Third: Completion of the FPA, and

 

 

Page 101


CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

   
  

a tit

SECTION ONE REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued
Fourth: Extraction of informtion from the FPA.

‘An entty into the FPA consists of the determination of the time position of
one or more troughs of a relatively long wave. Several entry techniques are
possible. The two most common are:

 

One. Use of the oscillations of the Dominaney Envelope centerline to
locate troughs of the pre-dominant wave, and
Two. Use of FLD’s in past time to identify probable trough locations
of a relatively long wave.
of a relatively long wave.
FPA extensions take place in four ordered steps as follows:

First: Direct application of the Principle of Synchronicity

Second: Search for wave troughs between consecutive troughs of the next
longer wave

Third: Search for wave troughs to either side of the time positions of
isolated troughs of the next longer wave, and

Fourth: Search for wave troughs by direct comparison of best wave-
period estimates with raw price data.

FPA completion takes place in two ordered steps:

First: The phasing gaps for the shortest wave phased are filled to ob-
tain a complete set of trough-position estimates for this wave.

Second: The harmonic gaps are filled for successively longer waves.

In extracting information from an FPA, we first average wave-period
samples to arrive at best estimates of wave periods. Then these best esti-
mates, along with the time positions of the last known wave troughs, are
used to establish when (in future time) nests-of-lows are likely to occur.
Finally, measurements of time elapsed from the last known wave trough
positions to current time provide the necessary wave phase estimates. Cur-
rent-time wave direction is derived directly from wave phase information.

Several general policies apply in the conduct of an FPA. For example,
in extension of an FPA, work always proceeds from long waves to succes-
sively shorter ones. In completion of an FPA, work always proceeds from

ee

paasassy sq3ry IY

“aur ‘ssoug ss0pEA] EL61 @ IBLAKAOL


‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

CYCLITEC

SERVICES rT y

SECTION ONE REVIEW AND INTRODUCTION continued

the shortest wave phased to successively longer ones. Those wave troughs
for which the most supporting evidence exists are phased first. Ambiguous
situations are left for resolution later in the analysis when additional sup-
porting evidence may be available.

 

An FPA results in the placement of phasing diamonds at the estimated time
positions of all wave troughs; in the graphical location of the expected time
of future nests-of-lows; and in information required for the assembly of a
current cyclic model.

A current cyclie model is an organized tabulation of most of the information
extracted from an FPA. It includes the direction of sigma el, which is the
trend underlying the eighteen-month wave; the nominal cyclic model wave
periods paired with the average of FPA wave-period samples; the phase of
each wave in terms of time elapsed from the last known wave trough to the
present; the current direction of action of each wave; and the FLD number
‘of each wave based on the average period.

Some transaction-decision information is immediately available from the
current cyclic model. An important example of such information is an esti-
mate of the likelihood of the imminence of a price reversal. However, the
most essential functions of the current cyclic model are to establish current
wave-phase status; to make identification of VTL’s possible; and to serve as,
the basis for FLD construction.

Having completed the FPA with its current cyclic model, we can then pro-
ceed to develop VTL's and FLD's. A complete VTL and FLD analysis in-
cludes FLD pattern interpretation and results in establishment of a trans-
action objective and a logical stop-loss level.

Once an objective and a stop-loss level are established it is possible to deter-
mine a transaction entry price with acceptable potential risk ratio and gross
potential percentage. We then determine the nature of the most effective ac-
tion signal, taking the transaction conditions into account and making use
of the current cyclic model.

This completes the overview summary of the major concepts of the preced-
ing lessons. Many details have been omitted from this summary for the sake
of clarity of presentation. For this reason, as you study the accompanying

text version of the summary, you should refer to previous lessons as nec-

Now that the basic cyclic concepts have been presented, the format of the
lessons must change slightly. Each lesson will still begin with a review and
end with a study summary and self-examination. New material will follow
the introductory review but there will be fewer exercises and steadily increas-

points that have been discussed, whereas a workshop demonstrates an entire
cyclic analysis.) In these workshops you will be led to apply ever more com-

you will eventually face in the market place.

In the past three lessons you have been required to absorb a large amount of

with the minimum of exposure needed to be effective in extracting transac-
tion decision information from price movement by the use of cyclic methods,
and there are seven more lessons to go. While it is true that the bulk of the
factual material to be learned is now in your hands, there are a number of
new concepts yet needed. But, most important of all, you must gain experi-
ence in applying cyclic methods in a wide variety of price situations. You are
unequivocally urged to restrain all temptation to trade until you have com-

SECTION TWO TWO KINDS OF RISK

‘The concept of risk has been briefly discussed in previous lessons. In the
context chosen, the word risk was used to indicate risk potential, or potential
for loss. The meaning of the word risk in this sense is a measure of the

 

pasiasay swydna TY

“uy ‘ssaug $39pes E161 @ WUKdoD


‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

   
 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

dW
TH

      

Hl ] | | art
CTT TT
SECTION TWO TWO KINDS OF RISK continued

amount of capital that may be lost if a transaction is stopped out instead of
proceeding as expected to produce a profit.

‘The word risk must also be used in a second sense in connection with a
transaction, and this second kind of risk must also be evaluated in so far as
possible. It will be spoken of as transaction risk.

‘Transaction risk is a measure of the probability that a given transaction will
produce a loss instead of a gain.

It has been seen how risk potential can be satisfactorily evaluated before a
transaction is entered. Unfortunately, the state-of-the-art in applying cyclic
concepts to trading and investing does not as yet provide a similarly precise
method of predetermining transaction risk. This is primarily due to the in-
valvement of such factors as analytical skill and the ability to control the
‘emotions during a transaction.

However, cyclic concepts and analytical methods do provide a reasonable

approach to this problem; and there are two distinct ways in which the gen-
eral nature of transaction risk can be assessed.

 

The first of these involves a technique that has been touched upon previous-
ly. It consists of building up evidence that a given wave situation in price
movement has been evaluated correctly. All through the process of cyclic
analysis, as you apply each analysis technique you should be alert for evi-
dence that either confirms or refutes evidence previously obtained by some
other technique. By the time an analysis is completed, you should have a
pronounced “feel” for the transaction being considered. You may feel
strongly that the essential features of the analysis hold together well, or you
may feel that the situation is a confused and conflicting one. If the latter

is the case, transaction risk must be considered large, and it is best to trans-
fer attention to another transaction opportunity.

 

The second approach to an evaluation of transaction risk has to do with the
nature of the transaction under study. For example, if prices have just
rounded a bottom and are in a position to rally from a nest-of-lows, the
onger the longest wave in the nest, the higher the transaction risk. This is
because the longer waves require more time to reverse direction, thus pro-
viding increased opportunity for shorter waves to cause prices to break a
stop-loss level before proceeding to the transaction objective.

LESSON

4

Page 106

  

lah

SECTION TWO TWO KINDS OF RISK continued

The interaction of the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity also
affects transaction risk. As demonstrated in lesson one, these principles
cause prices to tend to form clear, sharp lows, but rounded highs. The same
two principles also cause high translation effects to be greater than low
translation effects. These two facts make short-selling transactions near
price tops inherently more risky than long transactions near price lows. This
consideration and the methods of dealing with it will be discussed in a later
lesson.

Another important concept related to transaction risk is that of edge-band
versus mid-band transaction entry. These phrases originate in the curious
fact that price wave characteristics are such that a constant vertical width
channel can always be found which just contains virtually all of the price
movement of any given wave in a cyclic model. Thus, such a channel can be
drawn for each identified wave. A channel is another word for envelope, and
the channels described here are similar in appearance to the Dominancy
Envelope you are already familiar with. (The Dominancy Envelope just con-
tains virtually all of the price movement of the dominant wave.)

 

The vertical width of such a channel is sometimes called the band of the
channel. Therefore, when price movement is at the lower or upper edge of
the channel, it is spoken of as being in an edge-band state.

Similarly, when price movement is about half way up from the bottom or
down from the top of such a channel, it is said to be in a mid-band state.
The advent of Cyclitec Services eliminated the need to actually construct
such channels, but the expressions edge-band and mid-band persist and are
still useful.

  

It is intuitively and theoretically evident that a mid-band transaction has less
transaction risk than an edge-band one, assuming all other measures of
transaction risk are equal.

There are other results of cyclic analysis that contribute to the evaluation of
transaction risk. These will be brought up and discussed at the appropriate
times. For the moment, it suffices to call attention to the existence of this
important type of risk and to the fact that, while cyclic analysis does not pro-
vide a way to attach numbers to this risk, there are methods whereby a
skilled analyst can do much in the way of evaluating it.
