SECTION THREE HOW TO PREDICT TREND
In lesson two it was learned that many trends can be in existence at any one
time. Literally, a trend is associated with each wave of a cyclic model by the
fundamental definition of trend,

This concept was put to good use in lesson three in the discussion of the
quantity sigma el. As pointed out in that lesson, no current cyclic model can
ever be complete since there is no known maximum wave period. Therefore,
there will always be some composite wave consisting of the sum of waves
longer than the longest wave for which phase information can be obtained.
This composite wave is spoken of as sigma el and is a trend, namely, the
trend underlying the longest wave for which phase information is obtainable.

The concept of trend was also put to good use in the explanation of the
meaning of the Dominancy Envelope centerline. Here the centerline was
shown to be the sum of all price waves longer than the dominant wave. Thus
the Dominancy Envelope centerline was shown to be the trend underlying
the dominant wave.

Now it is time to extend the meaning and use of the concept of trend.

Once a current cyclic model is in hand, it is theoretically possible to esti-
mate future trend underlying each wave in the model. It is not necessarily
productive to do so, but it is often desirable to form such an estimate for one
or several waves.

The means of estimating future trend is provided by a simple technique
based upon the Principle of Proportionality. This principle, when applied to
a cyclic model made up of ideal, proportional price waves, produces a re-
markable result. No matter how long or short the period of the wave, or how
large or small the amplitude, each wave of such an ideal cyclic model can
move prices just as rapidly as any other wave.

In our study of ideal waves in lesson one, we saw that at the exact bottom
and top of a wave, the wave is momentarily not causing prices to change at
all. On the other hand, when a wave is exactly half way between a trough
and a crest, it is causing prices to move up by more price units per day or
week ot month than at any other time in the wave cycle. Similarly, when a
wave is halfway between a crest and a trough, it is causing prices to move
down faster than at any other time in the wave cycle.

 
 

 

 

 

4

Page 107%

 


     

  

 

CYCLITEG j
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

Pa tt

SECTION THREE HOW TO PREDICT TREND continued
It is to these two times of maximum wave influence that we direct our
attention. The crucial fact is that for any set of proportional ideal waves, this
largest price change influence is exactly the same for each wave.

 

pews sai ty

“Duy ssa SI9PEL] EL6T O ANBLUKMO

This is not an intuitively evident state of affairs, but the effect will be clari-
fied by an illustration in the accompanying text.

For practical purposes the result is that a wave in a current cyclic model
that is found to be acting in a downward direction at a given time is likely to
be causing a downward effect in prices that is nearly equal to the upward
fect of any other wave found to be acting upward at that same time. The two
effects would tend to cancel even though the two waves might be greatly
different in period and amplitude.

  

‘As a further example, assume a set of four proportional waves in which three
waves are found to be acting upward while only one is acting downward. The ¢
net effect of all four waves would be estimated as up and roughly equal to

that of only two upward acting waves. And this would be true no matter

what the various amplitudes and periods of the four waves might be — pro-

vided that the waves were proportional.

 

The estimate is, of course, relatively crude since actual price waves are non-
ideal and the Principle of Variation implies that they may not even be truly
proportional at a given time. Nevertheless, the technique described is of con-
siderable value in cyclic analysis when it is desired to estimate future trend.
‘As an example of how this technique is applied to a cyclic analysis situation,
suppose it was considered useful to estimate the direction of trend under-
lying a ten-week wave in the current cyclic model of a stock. The current
cyclic model already shows (by arrow) the estimated direction of action of
each wave at present time. For all waves of the cyclic model longer than the
ten-week wave, the number of up arrows and the number of down arrows
would be noted. Arrows shown topping or bottoming would not be counted
since it is assumed that waves associated with these symbols are not influ-
encing prices appreciably at this particular time. If all such arrows were up,
it would be inferred that trend underlying the ten-week wave is very strongly
up. If most of the arrows cancel each other, but the net difference is still up,
this trend would be considered to be mildly up.

But this evaluation is for the direction of trend at present time only, since

e086 $$ —$—$—5— $$ $ $< $$$


‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, In.

 
 

 
 

  

Secures li HAA ‘A
Padinin) TTT

 

SECTION THREE HOW TO PREDICT TREND continued

this is the time for which the arrow directions were chosen when construc-
ting the current cyclic model. It is often more useful to estimate trend at
some future time during the probable period of a potential transaction. To
make such an estimate, it is only necessary to add an increment of time to the
phase of each wave in the cyclic model, redetermine the direction of action
of each wave under the new condition, and find the net sum of up and down
arrows as before. This can be repeated for as many such increments of time
as the analyst considers useful. In this way any desired trend can be esti-
mated for any near future time once a current cyclic model has been as-
sembled.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section three

for examples and illustrations of this technique.

SECTION FOUR ACTION SIGNALS REVISITED

Action signals have been touched upon in connection with the copper exam-
ple of lesson three but should be more thoroughly understood. The basic
idea behind the action signal concept is that price movement in the

future must be allowed to demonstrate that anticipated price wave events
are actually taking place before action is taken on a transaction.

 

Within the context of this concept, an action signal can consist of any price
movement that the analyst is willing to accept as being indicative of the
actual existence of wave events he has anticipated. Once an equity has been
analyzed, the analyst expects certain future price movement to occur as a
result of the wave situation determined by the analysis. He is interested in
devising a test that will reasonably assure him that the expected wave action
is actually underway. A positive result from this test would then be ac-
cepted as a reasonable basis on which to enter a transaction. This type of
action signal is spoken of as a buy signal or a sell-short signal, depending on
the type of transaction.

 

Action signals are not limited to transaction entry, however. A stop-loss level
is a form of action signal. In effect, the analyst is saying that if prices cross a
horizontal line drawn on a chart at the stop-loss price, he is willing to accept
this as evidence that the wave situation is probably not as envisioned and
that the likelihood of turning a profit on that particular transaction has
dropped to an unacceptably low level calling for termination of the
transaction.

——. rg 10


 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

    

SECTION FOUR ACTION SIGNALS REVISITED co
‘An action signal may also be devised as a trailing protect-profit signal, as a
sell or cover-short signal, or merely as a signal that a given wave event has
occurred. This latter signal may simply serve as an indication that all is
going well with the transaction,

 

inued

 

While it is theoretically possible to select any of a large variety of future
price events to serve as action signals, there are three types that are the most
generally useful. These are:

One. Price interaction with a valid trend line
Two. Price interaction with a future line of demarcation, and
Three. Price interaction with a horizontal price line.

Whaat is desired in all three types is evidence of the occurrence of predicted
wave events, However, in selecting, for any given transaction situation, which
type of action signal and which of several available signals within the type,

an evaluation of risk is also involved. For example, suppose a long trans-
action is contemplated on the basis of an impending nest-of-lows which
includes a five-week wave as the longest wave. Suppose further that the

trend underlying this wave is estimated to be strongly up during the first half
cycle of the wave, implying that FLD objectives based on this wave are likely
to be met or overshot. In this instance, we could minimize transaction

risk by selecting a future price break of the five-week FLD as an action
signal. But in doing s0 it is quite possible that we would reduce the poten-
tial-risk ratio to about one. Thus, transaction risk would be diminished at
the expense of potential-risk ratio. It is evident that action signal selection
must be coupled with good judgement in so far as risk factors are concerned.
These considerations will be well treated in the workshop sessions of the
Course.

 

‘The following is an example of a case in which a horizontal price line might
be considered for use as a buy signal. Assume a mid-band situation with a
very strong uptrend. A smalll price drop into a minor nest-of-lows has taken
place. This nest is being considered as a possible buying opportunity based
on FLD projections associated with much longer waves.

Itis strongly suspected that the powerful uptrend underlying the longest
wave in the minor nest-of-lows, and the attendant extreme high translation

Pra

po2soy sia IV
“buy Ssauq S1opEAL E261 © WBINKdOD


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

All Rights Reserved

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 

SECTION FOUR ACTION SIGNALS REVISITED continued

to the right, may prevent formation of any VTL before swift resumption of
the upward price movement. Therefore, it might well be decided that a
higher price than the one just preceding the nest would constitute sufficient
evidence that the anticipated minor nest-of-lows had been passed. In such
an instance, this price action would be acceptable as a buy signal, and a
price line would be drawn horizontally across the price high just preceding
the nest-of-lows. The decision would then be made to buy if and when prices
had just passed through this line from below to above.

It is most important to note that the triggering of an action signal does
not necessarily mean that transaction orders must be placed. The analyst
may well decide to set up an action signal to serve as an alert after which he
is to reassess the situation and then decide what to do. This might then
involve selection of a second action signal to govern placement of trans-
action orders.

It should further be noted that instant action is generally not possible upon
the break of an FLD. In order for the break of an FLD constructed from
time-shifted price averages to be meaningful, the average price for a day (or
week) must break the FLD. Such an average price is not available to the an-
alyst until the day (or week) has ended.

There is, of course, nothing particularly essential about basing FLD’s on
price averages. A buy signal could be based on an FLD formed from daily
price highs. Or a sell-short signal could be based on an FLD formed from
daily price lows. In these latter two instances, any move of prices through the
FLD would constitute a valid action signal,

Such an FLD might be chosen as an action signal in a situation in which
the analyst felt that because of a very strong underlying trend there was
danger of his missing a desirable transaction unless he could get in with the
earliest possible action signal. But it is important to keep in mind that get-
ting in early does not necessarily mean getting in at the best price. The anal-
yst should recognize that if he uses a price-high FLD (or a price-low FLD) to
achieve same-day transaction entry, in all probability he will be left with a
lower potential-risk ratio than he might otherwise have had. Taking this fact
into account, he might still elect to use the price-high or price-low FLD
action signal in certain situations in order to guard against the risk of losing
out on a transaction entirely.

SECTION FOUR ACTION SIGNALS REVISITED continued
Setting up action signals is very like playing a game of chess. The player
has at his disposal all of the rules of the game plus the potential effects of
many different move strategies. These can be documented in books, but it
remains up to the player to assemble them in a winning fashion in any one
particular game.

for a study summary of the concept of action signals.

SECTION FIVE THE TWO FINGER TECHNIQUE

Emphasis up to this point in the course has been on knowledge of cyclic
concepts and methods and on formal manipulative techniques. Still more
effort will be spent in these areas later, but it is now time to look into ways
of extracting useful wave information from price data with a minimum ex-
penditure of time and effort.

Such techniques are desirable because practical interest is not restrained to
one, or even several, equities. A typical portfolio management program
might allocate a certain percentage of available capital for short transaction
time trading in commodity futures. The remaining, and larger, percentage of
available funds might then be divided between short-term and medium-term
trading in common stocks. For both stocks and commodities, a number of
equities must be kept under analysis at all times in order to keep available
capital invested for a reasonable percentage of the time. These concepts will
be discussed in detail in a later lesson and are brought up now only to indi-
cate the need for rapid information extraction methods.

Several specific modes of cyclic analysis that use rapid methods are available
and will be treated in section eight. However, one technique is central to all
such analysis. It is called the two-finger technique and is the topic of this
section.

‘The two-finger technique is a way of making measurements with two fingers
instead of measuring strips. It is rough but surprisingly effective for some
special purposes.

For ease of discussion the index finger will be called the first finger, the next
finger the second finger, the ring finger the third finger, and the little finger
the fourth finger.

 

poasasay s1qsra IV
vwiskdog

“say 804d 9P¥AL EL6T


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
AU Rights Reserved

sees
EAM

SECTION FIVE THE TWO FINGER TECHNIQUE continued
For short measurements, most people find the first and second fingers to be
the most useful measuring instruments. For longer measurements, the first
and fourth fingers can be used. For still longer measurements, the thumb
and fourth finger are best.

 

Both time and price measurements are useful, as will be seen in the sections
to follow. As you gain a little bit of practice in this method, you will even

find yourself accomplishing rough averaging in this way. For example, sup-
pose you are checking the period of the pre-dominant wave by observation of
the oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope. The first and second fingers are
extended rigidly and used to measure a low-to-low sample of these oscillations.
Then, holding the fingers in the same position, the hand is moved to

measure another low-to-low sample. Suppose this is found to be larger than
the first. The fingers are then spread farther to approximately one-half of

the visually noted difference in the sample lengths. This process, when re-
peated for several samples, provides an average finger tip spacing, and the
average so produced is adequate for many purposes.

After obtaining an average period sample length in this manner, the hand is
transferred to the time scale of the chart without changing the finger spac-
ing. A direct reading is then possible of the approximate wave period in-
volved. A glance at the Periodogram and a mental comparison with the
nominal model usually suffices to establish recognition of the component
wave.

From a word description of this process, the potential errors may seem in-
tolerable for procedures as precise as cyclic analysis. But, as we shalll see, the
results are wholly adequate for some purposes and can be obtained in a very
small fraction of the time otherwise required.

In other applications, measurements are made of a series of price move-
ments instead of time differences. In these cases, comparison of the results
with the price scale of the chart is used instead of comparison with the time
scale.

Three principal uses are made of the two-finger technique. These are the sel-
ection of a trading cycle, the extraction of information from past time FLD
objectives, and implementation of the two rapid-scan procedures. Each of
these uses will be dealt with separately in following sections. The text

and demonstrates its application in price and time measurements as well as

in past-time FLD tracings.

SECTION SIX THE TRADING CYCLE CONCEPT

In section three it was seen that, once a current cyclic model is formed, it is
theoretically possible to identify the trend underlying each wave in the model
and to estimate the future direction of each such trend. Fortunately, how-
ever, it is not necessary to do this for each wave.

On the other hand, there is always a single wave that has special meaning
with regard to a potential transaction and for which it is most important to
make a trend estimate. This wave, called the trading cycle, is selected on the
basis of price movement in a manner to be discussed presently. But first we
must bring out a very important point.

Transactions made on the basis of cyclic analysis are dependent on estimates
of the future behaviour of waves. This future behaviour can always be con-
sidered as due to two sets of summed waves; one being the trading cycle plus
all shorter waves, and the other being the sum of all waves longer than the
trading cycle. This latter composite is recognized as the trend underlying the
trading cycle.

 

We need to emphasize this important fact: A transaction resulting from cyc~
lic analysis is predicated on price movement due to a composite wave con-
sisting of the sum of the trading cycle and all shorter waves — augmented or
opposed by the trend underlying the trading cycle.

When transaction elements are summarized in this way, it is readily appar-
ent that it is imperative to be able to select the trading cycle intelligently and
to evalute the future direction of the trend underlying it.

In order to select a trading cycle, a minimum acceptable gross price-
move percentage must first be determined. The methods for this determin-
ation are described in a later lesson where investment goals are treated.
However, the minimum gross price-move percentages chosen are normally
not less than twenty per cent.

6 ————————

poasasoy swg8rt TV

“su ‘ssaug S19pEAL CGT O WBLEKAOLD


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

Al Rights Reserved

  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

 
  

  

SECTION SIX THE TRADING CYCLE CONCEPT continued

There are some circumstances where such a minimum price move need not
be pre-selected, but these are the exception rather than the rule. For exam-
ple, a mutual fund manager would probably be very content if he could buy
near price lows associated with the eighteen-month wave troughs. In this
case, gross price-move percentages are likely to be large enough not to cause
a problem, and the trading cycle would automatically become the eighteen-
month wave.

   

Generally, however, the process of selecting the trading cycle for a given
equity consists of finding that composite wave which consistently produces
gross price moves equal to or greater than the minimum gross price move
acceptable. The longest wave in this composite is the trading cycle.

‘There are three different points in the process of cyclic analysis at which it
may be found desirable to select the trading cycle: Before a phasing anal-
ysis is available; just after the phasing analysis; or after a price objective has
been set.

Itis generally most desirable to select the trading cycle after the price objec-
ive has been set, and while this is usually after the phasing analysis, it could
be before. Sometimes there will be good reason for establishing the trading
cycle before the price objective has been set (for example, in a search for a
fast turn-around transaction). But whatever the most desirable time turns
out to be, one of the following methods of trading cycle selection will apply.

 

To select the trading cycle before a phasing analysis, at least several key
waves must be identified. This is done using the two-finger technique des-
cribed in section five. With several waves tentatively identified in this way,
they are then investigated as possible trading cycles. The two-finger tech-
nique is again used to arrive at estimates of gross price-move percentages
associated with a given composite wave, and the effects of underlying trend
are noted. If a wave being considered for the trading cycle does not produce
the required minimum price moves, then a longer wave is tested. The Prin-
ciple of Proportionality assures us that sooner or later a wave can be found
that will meet any preselected price-move percentage requirement (within
reason).

 

If a phasing analysis is available, the selection process can start directly with
two-finger price-move percentage estimates.

pe


    
 
 

  

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

ea tl

SECTION SIX THE TRADING CYCLE CONCEPT continued

In the case where the analysis has progressed to the point where a price
objective has been set, the procedure is somewhat different. Here the mini-
mum price move criterion has already been applied. The phasing analysis,
the current cyclic model, and the wave effects used in setting the price ob-
jective are then used to find the longest wave in the composite wave res-
ponsible for the projected price move. This longest wave is then accepted as
the trading cycle. Once selected, the trading cycle sets several transaction
policies.

       

First of all, there is the matter of emotional involvement. The trading cycle
is selected prior to transaction entry, when emotional involvement is at a
minimum. Since emotional involvement can have a tremendous negative im-
pact on the profitability of investment operations if not kept under control,
do not allow emotional involvement to cause you to shift to a longer wave as
the trading cycle during the course of a transaction.

 

Secondly, with a key wave identified in the form of the trading cycle, it be-
comes possible to estimate the likelihood of a change in direction of the
trend relied upon to support attainment of a price objective.

‘Thirdly, suppose there is evidence at hand indicating that the price low or
high associated with the trading cycle trough or crest that was to produce
the price objective has gone by but the price objective has not been met. This
is to be construed as cause for initiation of transaction termination pro-
cedures.

Finally, the trading cycle becomes the prime criterion on which the import-
ant idea of a stop-loss level is based. If the trading cycle is the longest wave
of the composite wave depended upon to take prices to the objective, then

the recent past price high or low associated with the crest or trough of this

wave becomes the critical stop-loss determination point.

SECTION SEVEN USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS

The use of FLD’s in future time was described in lesson number two. Then
in lesson number three, one use for FLD’s in past time was discussed in con-
nection with locating wave troughs during phasing analysis.

Now we wish to explore a use for FLD’s in past time that forms an essential
part of the process of setting up a transaction.

lg —_—_—_—_—$—$— <<

“aur ss51g S9pE4] EL6T @ MBHKEOD

‘AU Rights Reserved

SECTION SEVEN USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS

continued

 

In the course of a cyclic analysis as described so far, the analyst is aware of
those FLD’s that are likely to be broken by prices in the near future and the
significance of such breaks in relation to a potential transaction. But, what
about FLD’s that have been broken in the past — creating price-movement
projections that may also have an impact on a planned future transaction?

It is not intuitively evident that past price breaks of FLD's can provide infor-
mation of importance with regard to a future transaction, so let's consider
two possible situations of this type.

As a wave of any given period executes a cycle, all shorter waves cause
oscillations of the composite price wave about the path that prices would
have taken if the shorter waves did not exist. Along the way, the FLD based
on the given wave is likely to be broken, creating a projection for prices that
can be achieved anywhere within a wide range of times during the present
cycle because of possible time-translation effects.

 

As time passes, prices fluctuate as a result of the sum of all shorter waves,
perhaps retreating into a minor nest-of-lows. Now if you were planning a
transaction as this nest-of-lows is approached, future time FLD's would alert
you as to price action expected — but based only on the waves involved in
that nest-of-lows. You would remain in ignorance regarding the import of
the longer-wave FLD break which took place in the past unless this break
were investigated by the use of FLD’s in past time.

It is quite possible under such circumstances to be planning a long trans-
action based on a given nest-of-lows while strong downside price objec-
tives exist from previous FLD breaks. Thus, information on all outstanding
FLD price projections from the past can be of vital importance in planning
a future transaction.

To understand the second way in which past FLD price projections can
influence future transactions, the impact of trend on FLD price projections
must be considered.

In figure 2-16 of lesson number two, the rationale behind the capability of
FLD’s to provide price move estimates is demonstrated for the case of ideal
waves. In this figure a single ideal price wave is shown along with its FLD.

ge 1


 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

SECTION SEVEN USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS
continued
In this ideal case it is clear why an FLD break can predict a future price
move. What is not so clear is whether the FLD retains this capability in the
case of actual price movement which is a composite wave consisting of the
sum of many non-ideal waves.
‘The answer is that the FLD does retain this capability, although imperfectly.
Tt actually performs the price projection function incredibly well considering
the complexity of the composite wave involved, thanks to the Principle of
Proportionality. If it were not for the fact that this principle ensures that
each successively longer wave is of such amplitude as to effectively domin-
ate the sum of all shorter waves, the FLD price projection technique would
fall far short of utility.
As it is, individual FLD price projections are imperfect, and the variable
that influences projection accuracy the most is trend. The rules of thumb
that apply in this instance are as follows:

One. If the trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based is
flat, then an FLD price objective is likely to be rather exactly
met.

If the trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based

opposes the price move projected by the FLD break, the price

move is likely to fall short of the FLD objective.

Three. If the trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based
favors the move projected by the FLD break, the price move is
likely to overshoot the FLD objective.

Two.

For these reasons, a single FLD price objective must not be considered indic-
ative unless a great deal is known about the underlying trend. FLD pattern
interpretation as a means of price-move projection (described in lesson two)
is much more reliable than the FLD break when there are uncertainties re-
garding underlying trend.

This tendency of individual FLD price projections to be affected by trend is
a disadvantage in relation to the accuracy of single FLD price objectives, but
it can be used to good advantage in another way.

 

The direction of trend underlying a given wave is difficult to determine in

SECTION SEVEN USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS
continued

many cases and can be of key importance in setting up a trade. The inter-
relationship of trend with FLD price projections can be used to gain much
valuable information related to trend.

‘Suppose that the initial estimate of the direction of trend underlying a given
wave at current time is up. Then suppose that in the recent past, upward
FLD price projections based on this given wave show that the associated
price moves fall far short of the FLD objectives. This would be viewed as
strong evidence that the trend under consideration was down at that time
and is now in the process of changing direction if the current estimate of
up is correct.

Evaluation of such results of FLD projections in past time can be used to
resolve ambiguous phasing situations, help in the determination of the di-
rection of sigma el, and provide early warning of trend change detectable in
no other way.

It is obvious that future price movement cannot be affected by all FLD

price objectives in past time. As a result it becomes important to have a

procedure whereby past FLD price objectives can be categorized as either

‘outstanding ot retired. An outstanding FLD price objective is one that is

capable of influencing future price movement, while a retired FLD price

objective can be dismissed from further consideration insofar as future price

movement is concerned.

When a price break of an FLD occurs, it implies that a specific price wave

is either half way up from a trough to a crest or half way down from a crest

to a trough. The FLD projection zone is based on the expectation that the

wave under consideration will continue to drive prices in the projected direc-

tion until the anticipated crest or trough is passed. Thus, any evidence that

the anticipated wave crest or trough has passed constitutes evidence that the

FLD price objective in question may be retired.

There are three principal ways in which a past FLD price objective can be

retired:

One. If the same FLD that produced the price objective is subsequent-

ly broken by prices in the opposite direction — and during the
time period when such a break should occur if the crest or

Page 119m

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

 
 

al

     

SECTION SEVEN USE OF PAST FLD PRICE PROJECTIONS

continued

trough in question has been passed — then the FLD price ob-
jective can be retired.

Two. Ifa valid trend line based on the wave just shorter than the wave
on which the FLD was based is broken by prices in such a way
as to indicate that the trough or crest in question has been
passed, then the FLD price objective can be retired,

Three. In some instances, usually when underlying trend is very strong,
a full cycle of the wave on which the FLD is based may go by
without either a VTL or an FLD break occurring. When this
happens and is confirmed by the phasing analysis, the evidence
is considered sufficient to retire the FLD price objective in
question.

This evidence is much strengthened if prices then proceed to
form a higher high or a lower low than the actual price associ-
ated with the wave crest or trough that was to drive prices to the
FLD price objective in question.

Past-time FLD's to be used in setting up a transaction are formed exactly
as described in section seven of lesson number three. The FLD number used
is based on the best estimate in hand of wave period from a current cyclic
model and is computed as just one-half of the wave period of interest.

The search is conducted for each wave of the cyclic model over a past time
period equal to at least one full period of the wave. A measuring strip may
be used just as when FLD’s in past time were used to locate wave troughs.
Alternatively, in many circumstances the two-finger technique of section five
is appropriate. The FLD’s are not actually drawn on the chart, but the time
and price of each definitive break is marked.

For each break, the actual price low or high associated with the implied
wave trough or crest is located and labeled. At the same time, the result-
ing FLD price objective is marked on the chart. The actual price low or
high is observed in relation to the projected FLD objective, and conclusions
regarding trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is based are noted
as part of the analysis involved in setting up the transaction.

Finally, the three criteria previously discussed are applied to each price
objective in order to determine whether the objective is retired or out-
standing. The impact of the outstanding price objectives on the transaction
under consideration is noted and made a part of the analysis,

This procedure sounds long and involved when described, but in practice
takes only a very few moments. The quality and utility of the information
gained can greatly reduce the risk of an incorrect transaction decision.

SECTION EIGHT RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION
METHODS
The two-finger technique for rapid extraction of information from equity
price bar-charts was introduced in section five, and a few specific applica-
tions were discussed. It is now time for a more extensive treatment of the
subject of rapid information extraction in the context of a complete but
rapid assessment of price charts.
‘The complete cyclic analysis of price charts can require up to one or one-
and-one-half hours for each equity. This is a very small expenditure of effort
for the preparation of an actual transaction. However, most of the time most,
equities will not be nearing a potential transaction; and the task we are
faced with is to select the few that are from the greater number that are
not. If we can make this selection by rapid methods, then we can concen-
trate the bulk of our effort on complete cyclic analysis of only those equities
for which such analysis will be worthwhile.

Incidentally, complete cyclic analysis is a term we will be using often, and we
will hereafter shorten it to CCA.

The expenditure of even an hour a week on each of a significant number of
equities becomes a burdensome task to be avoided if at all possible. In this
section you will learn to use what are called rapid-scan techniques for the
bulk of all analyses. These rapid information extraction methods are fully
capable of alerting the analyst to upcoming transaction opportunities. Only
when such an opportunity is indicated does the expenditure of effort for a
full-scale analysis become warranted and desirable.

 

 

Page 121,


  

LESSON

re

  

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

  

SECTION EIGHT RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION
METHODS continued

The process of acquiring skill at cyclic analysis is a great deal like the pro-
cess of learning to be skilled at an activity requiring a high degree of muscu-
lar coordination. When learning to dance, for example, the formal step
movements are learned first, and synchronization to the music is crude and
non-satisfying. It is only after conscious thought is no longer required to
direct the foot and body movements that grace and coordination are achiev-
ed; the formal step is almost entirely lost — and full sensory benefit is ob-
tained from the dance.

 

Cyclic analysis is similar in that the formal steps must be learned and metic-
ulously practiced; but once they become instinctive, the analyst will find
himself extracting wave information with a minimum of time and effort. The
rapid-scan methods of this section are the first of several moves in the di-
rection of accomplishing the desired results with a minimum of time and
effort.

There are two distinct modes of rapid information extraction to be discussed
now. These are known as rapid scan, or RS, and very rapid scan, or VRS.

An RS analysis typically requires about twenty minutes for completion while
a VRS analysis requires about five minutes.

VRS is most useful for selection of a few equities from among many for
further analysis. RS is used on the resulting few equities to ascertain whether
a transaction opportunity is likely in the near future, thus warranting the
expenditure of additional time and effort on a CCA.

‘The objective of VRS analysis is to determine as much as possible about
current wave status for an equity in five minutes ot less. No phasing analysis
is attempted, and only minimal attention is paid to the Periodogram. Either
the nominal cyclic model is used for period estimates, or crude estimates
are extracted from price data itself.

For example, two-finger measurements from low to low and high to high of
the oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline may be transferred to
the time scale for a rough reading of the pre-dominant wave period. Mental
comparison with the equivalent nominal model period and a quick look at
the Periodogram usually identify this wave for the analyst as one that is fam-
iliar and expected.

PD

poasason SiH LV
-ouy ssaug S29p84, €L61 © WHBHAAOD


‘AM Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

  

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO©

 

OO

SECTION EIGHT RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION
METHODS continued

Next, to investigate the next longer wave, advantage is taken of the Prin-
ciple of Harmonicity. Provided that it is not the 18-month wave, the next
longer wave is expected to be just twice the estimate in hand, so the two-
finger measurement of pre-dominant wave period serves as the FLD number
for the next longer wave.

This same two-finger measurement is therefore transferred to price data
with the fingers serving as an impromptu measuring strip as described in
section five.

 

In this way critical FLD breaks are noted along with their projected price
objectives and whether or not the objectives are still outstanding — all in
one swift movement of the hand. Further, the approximate location and di-
rection of the future time FLD for this wave is also noted.

During this process key price objectives and crest or trough locations are
roughly marked on the chart.

Now the pre-dominant wave period is mentally halved, and this new FLD
number picked up by two fingers on the time scale. Another pass across the
data provides quick information related to the pre-dominant wave.

By the time these two passes are made, the proper locations for key valid
trend lines can be identifed and the lines roughly sketched into place. Even
future nests-of-lows can be generally located with no real attempt at ac-
curacy.

‘The first time you try this procedure, you will probably be most surprised to
see how comprehensive a picture you obtain as to wave phase status and the
possibilities for an upcoming transaction, either long or short. And the more
experience you gain with complete cyclic analysis, the more information you
will find yourself extracting from a five minute VRS analysis.

 

‘An RS analysis is one step more sophisticated. An initial cyclic model is
assembled and used as a guide to establish key phasing diamond locations.
No attempt is made to fill gaps and the process is continued just long
enough to provide several obvious period samples for each wave.

  


  

eT

4

  

 

 

 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES
TRAINING
COURSE©

  
 

mtn

SECTION EIGHT RAPID INFORMATION EXTRACTION
METHODS continued

To save time, these period samples are not numerically averaged. Instead,
they are laid out on a measuring strip and an approximate period average is
obtained by visual comparison of the sample lengths with the chart time
scale.

‘These rather crude, and quickly obtained, approximations are then used to
form FLD numbers.

‘A measuring strip is used to sketch in past time and future time FLD’s. The
future time FLD's are drawn ignoring short term price oscillations. The
result is an inexact but reasonable FLD pattern array which can be inter-
preted in the same way as in a CCA.

Critical VTL's are noted based on those phasing diamonds that have been
placed. Potential FLD cascades and pause zones are observed. And com-
putations of the potential percentage profitability of possible future price
moves are made where applicable.

The RS effort is restricted to a twenty-minute period and will provide
enough more information than VRS analysis to readily detect upcoming
transaction opportunities.

In fact a good RS analysis is usually adequate, by itself, to base transaction
decisions on. However, before an actual transaction entry is made, the ex-
penditure of another hour to conduct a complete cyclic analysis is not only
good experience but excellent insurance.

‘These rapid information extraction methods are best learned by practice.
So please turn your tape unit off now, and return to the text at section eight
for a study summary and exercises.

The remainder of this lesson is to be devoted to the workshop, which will be
an increasingly larger part of all lessons to follow. A workshop differs from
an exercise in that a workshop covers a complete, integrated cyclic analysis
whereas an exercise covers only one or two isolated points or techniques.

  

‘The purpose of these workshops is to provide a steady stream of actual ex-
perience in the processes of cyclic analysis. As new material is covered in
subsequent lessons, it will be incorporated in the workshops as well until you
are applying the whole array of known methods and techniques to a wide
variety of cyclic circumstances.

It is of the utmost importance that you apply yourself diligently to each of
the workshops. Theory, rules, and descriptions of techniques are all very
well and are certainly needed, but absolutely nothing will take the place of
actual experience in cyclic analysis. Follow the discussions for each work-

SECTION TEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE DISCUSSION

The VRS analysis you have just performed demonstrates several aspects
of cyclic analysis procedures that are of the utmost importance and warrant
additional discussion.

First of all it is very important that the analyst have clearly in mind what,
end results are desired from a given procedure.

In this case, for example, the specified effort was a scanning operation to
determine whether or not a low risk transaction in HM might be likely in the
near future.

SECTION TEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE DISCUSSION
continued

However, cyclic techniques and principles were to be used, and they result in
the answers to wave questions. Only after these answers are obtained can the
implications regarding potential transaction immediacy and risk be de-
termined.

It isa very good practice to get yourself into the habit of always framing
clearly in your mind the wave questions that you are trying to answer as you
go along. As an example, the overall VRS analysis effort is directed at an-
swering the question:

Is the wave status for this issue at this time such that a low-risk trans-
action opportunity is likely in the near future?

‘To answer this question, the principle waves active in price movement must
be identified and roughly phased, and the FLD and VTL implications of

each determined.

‘As a second example, consider the wave questions you were asking, during
the step of the VRS analysis just performed, when you measured the elapsed
time between troughs of the Dominaney Envelope centerline oscillations with
two fingers. There were essentially four wave questions involved:
‘One: What period estimate for the pre-dominant wave is provided by
the fluctuations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline?
Two: Which wave component of the nominal cyclic model corres-
ponds to the pre-dominant wave?
‘Three: Does the Periodogram substantiate the period estimate ob-
tained?
What information, if any, is obtained by this process about the
cyclic state of this issue at this time?
In this instance, the first three wave questions were readily answered with no
conflicts developing. For the fourth question, you learned approximately
when the pre-dominant wave troughs occurred across the chart and that
wave periods for this issue might be running longer than nominal at this

time.

‘As each cyclic analysis method is applied, framing the precise wave questions

for which answers are desired is an excellent way to insure that no critical
wave information will be overlooked.

Another universal aspect of eyclic analysis was demonstrated in this VRS
analysis. This is the process of building evidence. At each step of an analysis,
certain evidence regarding wave status is obtained. However, an open mind
has to be maintained until all the available evidence is in hand. Evidence ob-
tained in one way is sometimes found to conflict with evidence obtained in
another. These conflicts must be resolved by generating all possible evidence
having a bearing on the conflict, weighing the relative credibility of the
various items, and arriving at the most reasonable conclusion.

There was an example of this process in the VRS analysis on HM. You
were obtaining evidence that caused you to suspect that a rally from a nine-
month nest-of-lows was either in the process of starting or would start soon.
Then the nine-month FLD provided evidence indicating that prices were
ely to retreat to 17 or 18 first. This was in conflict with the previous evi-
dence obtained by other means.

 

In this instance you were lead to investigate the trend underlying the nine-
month wave. The results showed that it was reasonable to expect that this
trend would probably prevent achievement of a price low of 17 or 18 as pro-
jected by the nine-month FLD. Then 25-week wave evidence supported the
conclusion that this price low was unlikely before the expected rally took
place.

 

The original evidence was pretty well negated by that which followed, and
the conclusion resulted that probabilities favored a rally from near current
price levels. This is quite typical of the manner in which wave status evi-
dence is compiled and evaluated. No one piece of evidence is ever to be con-
sidered inviolable. It is only the conclusions built upon all of the evidence of
an analysis that are accepted as representative of the most likely course of
price events in the future.

Finally, eyclic logic is employed at each step of an analysis to gain increased
information. As each new bit of wave status evidence comes to light, ask
yourself: “What is the wave meaning of this evidence when related to other
evidence in hand?” The meaning may strengthen propositions already under

SERVICES

SECTION TEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE DISCUSSION

continued

consideration, or it may conflict with them. And in the end, new knowledge
may be gained regarding wave status.

Again, using the conflict in the VRS analysis of HM as an example, when
the nine-month price projection to 17 or 18 came up, the cyclic logic went
somewhat as follows:

So we have an important FLD projection that conflicts with other available
evidence. What do we know about wave effects that might influence the
believability of such an FLD objective.

This calls to mind the impact of trend on the attainment of price objectives,
and immediately the proper course of action is suggested:

A need exists to evaluate the trend underlying the nine-month wave during
the time period when the FLD projection took place.

‘The VRS analysis of HM contains several examples of techniques and ways
of thinking that are applicable in general to all of cyclic analysis. These
include:

One: The importance of setting up specific wave questions to be ans-
swered by analysis procedures

Two. The method of building evidence regarding wave status, and

Three. The use of cyclic logic to complement individual analysis meth-
ods in the extraction of wave information.

Please turn your tape unit off now, and return to the text at section ten for
the second unit of this workshop.

SECTION ELEVEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO
DISCUSSION

In this section of the workshop you have been introduced to a new type of
situation. In following the rules available for the application of a cyclic an-

alysis method you suddenly found yourself unable to continue, at least in an
orthodox manner.

The lesson to be learned is that cyclic analysis is never mechanical. The rules

SECTION ELEVEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO
DISCUSSION continued

and techniques are convenient, and even indispensable, guides — but prob-
lem situations must be handled by falling back on the basic cyclic principles
and your own knowledge of price wave characteristics.

Fortunately, the ability to exercise cyclic logic is readily acquired no matter
how difficult it may seem to the beginner. Alll that is necessary is a thorough
grounding in the fundamentals plus a certain amount of experience. Provid-
ing the latter is, of course, the purpose of the workshops.

When you come across a seemingly difficult situation, you will find it help-
ful to ask yourself the following questions:

One: “If I re-evaluate all of the information now in hand, would the
results warrant a transaction decision?”

Two: “If not, is there any other way to apply cyclic knowledge in order
to get over the hurdle?”

Do not, however, make the mistake of settling upon one or several key ele-
ments of cyclic concepts and expecting these alone to always produce the
needed information. They may, indeed, produce the information required,
but, without the application of the totality of cyclic concepts in the process
of building evidence, the information produced may very well result in poor
or erroneous transaction decisions. And do not fall into the trap of stopping
your search for information when you find a piece of evidence that gives you
the answer you may want to hear. There may be other, overriding informa-
tion available that would produce the opposite answer and thus save you a
painful error.

You should aim all through the course at getting a usable grasp of all
the concepts and methods presented.

It is well to remember also that cyclic analysis can never produce unflawed
knowledge of wave status. This being the nature of things, the analyst must
be satisfied when the preponderance of evidence from multiple sources is in
agreement. The beginner becomes distraught when all does not fit like a
picture puzzle. The expert inquires whether the pieces fit well enough.

SECTION TWELVE WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER THREE
DISCUSSION

There is another important lesson to be learned from the daily phasing anal-
ysis of HM.

Notice first of all how very straightforward this analysis was by comparison
with that of the weekly chart. Certainly, when this task was complete, you
should have been left with a strong feeling of confidence that the analysis,
was correct and meaningful.

By contrast, when the weekly analysis was finished, the question was actual-
ly raised as to whether or not to abandon further effort on the issue for a
time.

In retrospect, it can be seen that the only items of real importance contribu-
ted by the weekly analysis in this situation were:

One. The existence of the troublesome downward price objective pro-
jected by the nine-month FLD.

Two. The existence of an outstanding upward price objective projec-
ted by the cighteen-month FLD.

Three. The critical fact that the important nest-of-lows at grid line 147
on the daily chart includes all waves up through the nine-month
wave.

All other ambiguities and uncertainties on the weekly chart did not require
resolution in order for a transaction decision to be made!

This kind of circumstance occurs repeatedly in cyclic analysis. The newcom-
er to cyclic analysis might very well have become discouraged and discon-
tinued the analysis before completing the weekly phasing effort. The more
experienced analyst would know that many, many times, the analytical prob-
em of the moment goes away as the overall analysis progresses.

This point cannot be made too strongly. You must avoid firm conclusions
along any lines until the analysis processes are complete and have been thor-
oughly evaluated. Above all, do not discontinue an analysis just because it
does not seem to be going smoothly.

A very large part of the skill in cyclic analysis lies in the ability to discrim-
inate between which analysis results should cause concern and which should

not. It is typical of the newcomer to wear himself out trying to resolve

the answer is yes, it may still be to your advantage to leave the question in

answer without special effort on your part.

SECTION THIRTEEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER FOUR
DISCUSSION

In this workshop unit you have progressed your cyclic analysis from a phas-
ing analysis and current cyclic model to a set of almost fully analyzed charts.
In doing so, you developed several points of general significance.

First you noted that pause zones did not occur in this analysis to aid in set-
ting price objectives. This is somewhat unusual in that more times than not
these useful patterns are present.

The significance of this fact is that a complete analysis providing low trans-
action risk assurance was possible even in the absence of PZ patterns. As
with all other aids to cyclic analysis, information is to be extracted from
PZ's when they are present, but their presence does not necessarily imply
that a successful analysis can be completed nor does their absence necessari-
ly imply that a successful analysis cannot be completed.

Next, you saw that no really new and startling information came to light in
the course of the CCA. Most of the key conclusions had already surfaced
during the VRS. The question naturally arises: “Why expend the time and
effort on CCA?”

‘The answer to this question comes in three parts:

First: The CCA accomplished a tremendous amount of good by con-
firming in many different ways the impressions you gained from
the VRS analysis. All of this confirmation was strongly instru-
mental in your arriving at the conclusion that the transaction

was low-risk in nature, that is, had a high probability of produc-
ing a profit rather than a loss.

Second: The CCA assured you that no critical element of cyclicality had
been overlooked.

Finally: The forty minutes to one hour spent on this CCA is a very small
insurance premium to pay when it is hard-earned capital that
will be placed in jeopardy if the analysis is incomplete or in-
correct.

There is one final but vital procedure that must be undertaken before it can
be said that you have been through the full process or cyclic analysis. This
is the procedure that is called setting up a transaction. So please return to
the text at section thirteen.

SECTION FOURTEEN CONCLUSION

In this lesson you have learned about transaction risk, how to predict trend,
how to conduct rapid-scan analyses, the way to take advantage of past FLD
price projections, and how to select and use a trading cycle.

In addition, you have worked your way for the first time through the com-
plete process of analyzing a long transaction.

In the lessons to come you will learn how to conduct yourself in the course
of a transaction, how to make short transactions, how to control your emo-
tions, and what is involved in handling a portfolio. You will learn that there
are various types of market environments to contend with and how this af-
fects your decision-making processes. And, most important of all, you will be
exposed to a variety of transaction analysis situations.

Your expertise will increase to the degree that you will be amused by the
trouble experienced and time expended in this, your first, transaction
analysis!

This is the end of the tape cassette for lesson number four of the Cyclitec
Services Training Course. You should turn your tape unit off now and return
to the text to study the lesson summary and take the self-examination
provided.

REVIEW

© The process of analyzing any transaction using cyclic principles consists
of 14 basic steps:

1. VRS or RS analysis to determine whether additional analysis is war-
ranted

2. An FPA resulting in fully phased charts

3. Construction of a current cyclic model

 

4. Extraction from the FPA and current cyclic model of information
on transaction risk and the nearness of possible action

5. Lay-out of current and future time FLD’s, and construction of past
time VTL's and FLD’s when required to answer specific wave ques-
ns

Interpretation of FLD patterns
Determination of the transaction price objective

Identification of the trading cycle

ee are

Selection of a suitable action signal

10. Determination of the near-future direction of the trend underlying
the trading cycle

11. Selection of the stop-loss level

12. Tabulation of PR ratios and gross potential percentages versus trans-
action entry prices

13. Determination of the maximum buy price or the minimum sell-short

price

 

14, Final reevaluation of transaction risk.

© There are two modes of rapid information extraction used to scan a stable
of equities to determine which ones are most likely to offer transaction op-
Portunities in the near future, These are very rapid scan (VRS), and rapid
sean (RS). VRS analysis requires of the order of 5 minutes, while RS takes
about 20 minutes.

© Transaction risk, unlike risk potential, is not readily quantified. Where risk

potential is an estimate of the amount of capital that may be lost in a trans-
action, transaction tisk is a measure of the likelihood of having to take that
Joss rather than terminating the transaction at a profit. Through experi-
ence, the analyst develops a feel for this kind of risk. As an analysis pro-
gresses, an impression is formed as to whether or not the transaction risk is
acceptable.

 

© Analysis of past-time FLD objectives examines the way price action has be-
haved in the past in relation to single FLD price projections. Outstanding
FLD projections are identified, and information is obtained on trends.

© Action signals are used for a variety of purposes. Among these are stop-
loss levels, buy signals, sell signals, sell-short signals, cover-short signals,

© A trading cycle is the longest wave of the composite wave depended upon

stop-loss level, and the trend underlying the trading cycle is important in
evaluating transaction risk. Once a transaction has been initiated, it is gen-
erally inadvisable to alter the initial choice of the trading cycle.

WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS

© The market price averages and individual equities all show fundamental
cyclic differences between price tops and price bottoms.

© The Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity are largely responsible
for this effect. The combined result of these two principles is to assure a
strong tendency for nests-of-lows to form, and to assure an equally strong
tendency for nests-of-highs not to form, (Please refer to figure 1-15 in lesson
one.)

© Asa result of nest-of-low formation, a number of waves tend to act together
in driving prices into a low, and to reverse themselves simultaneously caus-
ing a sharp price reversal.

© Just the opposite is true in the case of price tops, during which waves tend
to form their crests consecutively rather than in unison. This causes the
characteristic rounded shape of a price top, interrupted only by individual
(usually short) wave oscillations.

© By the definition of trend, the trends underlying a number of waves also
tend to reverse in unison at price bottoms. At tops, quite the opposite is
true with the various trends being mixed and constantly changing in
direction. It is only after a top is passed that trend action becomes uni
again, causing prices to drop sharply toward the next nest-of-lows.

 

© With a mixed set of trend directions during a price top, time-translation
effects also become complex. The price highs associated with the crest of
one wave in a top can be found translated to the left while the price highs
associated with the crest of another wave in the same top may be translated
to the right.

© If trend could be predicted with great accuracy, these effects would not nee-
essarily increase transaction risk. However, the present state-of-the-art of
cyclic analysis permits only rough estimation of future trend. While this is
extremely helpful, the accuracy available does not provide the price-reversal
prediction accuracy at price tops that is achievable at price bottoms.

© The net result is an increase in transaction risk in the vicinity of price tops.
This affects all selling and short-selling operations.

© A price top is caused by a composite wave consisting of the sum of a

 


rd

"section two

2 ©

WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS continued

wave and all shorter waves. The trend underlying the given wave then af-
fects the shape of the top.

© Price action is constantly forming tops. Some of these will be caused by a
composite wave the longest wave of which is very short. These tops will tend
to be rather uncomplicated due to the small number of waves involved.
Such smaller tops are then a part of the structure of tops formed by com-
posite waves containing waves of longer period. These will be more com-
plex, and will form part of the structure of still larger and more compli-
cated tops.

© Figure 5-1, a weekly chart of Global Marine, is a good example of some of
the points just discussed. To aid in your study of this figure, phasing dia-
monds for the 80-, 40-, and 20-week waves have been added at the bottom
of the chart, Also, the midpoints between some of the pairs of phasing dia-
monds have been marked by triangles at the top of the chart to indicate the
positions of the crests of those waves (in the manner of figure 1-15 in lesson
one).

© Notice the sharp three-diamond lows at grid lines 11, 89, and 173; and no-
tice the broader top formations between these lows. Also notice how the
larger tops are made up of several smaller tops, and notice that as you move
from the longer to the shorter waves, the tops become less complex because
there are fewer waves present to create complexity. This latter fact will be
made use of in the treatment of short-selling transactions.

© For the second 80-week cycle, a line has been drawn from each crest tri-
angle to the associated price high. Notice that high translation for the short-
er waves was generally to the right during the first half of the 80-week cycle
and to the left during the second half.

© During the first 20-week cycle of this second 80-week cycle, the underlying
trend was strongly up and, as we could expect, the price high was translated
considerably to the right of the wave crest. During the second 20-week
cycle, trend was still up, but not so strongly since the 40-week wave has
turned down. And we see that the price high was translated to the right,
but not so much as for the first cycle.

© The trend underlying the first 40-week cycle was strongly up and the price

© At the time of the third 20-week cycle, we could have assessed the trend

going as expected.

and the high translation to the left that we would expect did occur. (Notice
the reduced amplitude of this 20-week cycle.)

© Study this figure carefully to make sure you understand these translation-
versus-trend relationships. They are very impogtant in cyclic analysis, par-
ticularly during the selection of the trading cycle.

© Before we leave this section, there is one final observation that should be
made. Like all cyclic effects, the differences between price tops and bottoms
represent strong tendencies, but variation from the norm occurs. Thus,
examples can be found of rounded price bottoms and peaked price tops.
These are the exceptions, however, and the causes will be studied later.

EDGE-BAND AND MID-BAND SHORT SALES

© The basic cyclic differences between price tops and price bottoms result
in an increase in transaction risk for edge-band short transactions over
edge-band long transactions.

 

© The solution is to avoid this added risk by always shorting at mid-band.

© The trading eycle is just as important for short transactions as for long
transactions.

© Once a trading cycle has been selected, then either of the following events
indicates a high probability that the price high associated with the trading
cycle crest has been passed. A valid uptrend line based on the wave just
shorter than the trading cycle must be broken to the downside, or else the
FLD based on the trading cycle must be broken to the downside. (The
phrase to the downside or on the downside means in a downward direction.
Or, when used with upside it means in an upward direction.)

© Shortly after the VTL or FLD break occurs, a minor price rally can normal-
ly be expected. (Note: Because of this rally, the VTL or FLD break should
not be used as an action signal.) The minor price top responsible for the
rally will be less complex than the one associated with the trading cycle, re-
sulting in reduced transaction risk for a short sale.

  

© Ifdesired and applicable, this process of requiring a VTL or FLD break
may be repeated for the minor top to reduce transaction risk even further,

© Figure S-2 shows the price action of Fairchild Camera during a time when a
short sale would have been appropriate. In the figure, the nominal 40-
week wave is assumed to be the trading cycle, and the dashed-line channel
has been drawn accordingly. The mid-band short-selling opportunity can
be clearly seen in the time span between grid lines 60 and 105.

© With the hindsight that this chart allows us, it is easy to see that a short
sale in the price range of $8 to 65 would have been quite profitable. But at
the time this top was forming, such a short sale would have been an edge-
band short transaction, which normally carries a high transaction risk and
is therefore to be avoided in most circumstances.

© However, a mid-band short transaction with a very acceptable transaction
tisk could have been entered during the time between grid lines 100 and 110

—_—_— KK Prope


Saas VHILUT Szeizes
section t TRAINING
COURSEO

EDGE-BAND AND MID-BAND SHORT SALES continued

within the price range of 46 to 50. And this relatively low risk transaction
would nonetheless have produced a quite satisfactory potential percentage.

THE MID-CHANNEL PAUSE

(© For any given trading cycle the probabilities favor the existence of a mid-
channel pause.

© A mid-channel pause is a significant change in the direction of price move-
ment as prices progress from a trading cycle low to a trading cycle high or a
trading cycle high to a trading cycle low. It occurs approximately midway
between the trading cycle high and the trading cycle low.

© The Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity are responsible for the
mid-channel pause.

© The price top and nest-of-lows associated with a mid-channel pause contain
as a longest wave the wave that has one-fourth the period of the trading
cycle.

© The 18-month wave is the only wave of the nominal model that cannot
cause a mid-channel pause. This is because of the 3 to 1 relationship be-
tween the periods of the 18-month wave and the next longest wave, the
54-month wave.

(© Figure 5.3 illustrates several of the features of price tops, including the
mid-channel pause effect.

@ In this figure, waves A, B, and C are harmonious, synchronous, ideal
waves. Waves A and B are proportional as well, but the wave C amplitude
has been exaggerated to accentuate the mid-channel pause effect.

© Between the 3-diamond nests-of-lows shown, a typical price top is formed.
Wave A is the longest wave in the composite wave responsible for the top
formation. Assume that wave A is the equivalent of a trading cycle for this
example.

© The price rise from point 1 to point 4 would form the basis for a long trans-
action, while the price drop from point S to point 8 would form the basis
for a short transaction.

@ In the course of this top formation, note the following high translation
effects:

1. Price high associated with wave A crest: either at point 4 or point 5,
but not at wave crest.

—— <<< ge


, section four

THE MID-CHANNEL PAUSE continued
2. Price high associated with the first cycle of wave B: translation to the
right.
3. Price high associated with the second cycle of Wave B: translation
to the left.
4, Price high associated with the first cycle of wave C: translation to
the right.
5. Price high associated with the second cycle of wave C: Negligible
translation,
6. Price high associated with the third cycle of wave C: negligible trans-
lation.
7. Price high associated with the fourth cycle of wave C: translation
to the left.
This is typical of price tops as described in section two, with mixed trans-
lation effects taking place as the top is formed.
© Now note the channel or band of constant vertical height ENV, shown
in dotted lines and enclosing all of price action. This is an envelope similar
to the Dominancy Envelope, but based specifically on wave A in this exam-
ple. The composite wave is seen to oscillate broadly from bound to bound
of this envelope. A buy at 1 would be considered an edge-band buy associa-
ted with wave A. A sell at 4 would be considered an edge-band sell associa-
ted with wave A. A short at 5 would be considered an edge-band short
associated with wave A. And a cover-short at 8 would be considered an
edge-band buy associated with wave A.
© Now note the combined effect of waves A, B, and C that produces the price
reversal at 2 and the subsequent decline to 3. This is the expected mid-

channel pause on the upside. Notice particularly the nearly centered loca-
tion of point 3 between points 1 and 4. This is where the name mid-channel

pause comes from.

© Similarly, the price reversal at 6 and the subsequent rise to 7 is the mid-
channel pause on the downside.

© In section three it was indicated that evidence of trading cycle crest formation

is required betore shorting, but that the VTL or FLD break producing
this evidence should not be used as an action signal. Figure 5-3 shows the
reason for this admonition. The line marked FLDA is an FLD based on
wave A. This is broken to the downside at 9 providing evidence that the
price high associated with the crest of wave A has passed. But if this were
used as an action signal to short, transaction entry would take place just
prior to the price rally due to the mid-channel pause.

© In figure 5-3, the price tops formed between points 1 to 3 and 6 to 8 are due
to the mid-channel pause effect. These tops will have their own structure
owing to shorter period waves not shown in this example. Alll of the effects
discussed for the top between points 1 and 8 will be repeated in the tops at
2 and 7 with two significant differences:

 

1. The tops will form and be completed on a time scale one-fourth as
~ long.

2. The tops will be less complex since fewer wave components are
involved.

© As stated earlier, wave C is shown with greater than proportional ampli-
tude in figure 5-3 in order to show the mid-channel pause clearly. Many in-
stances in actual price movement will show the same pronounced mid-
channel pause, implying that the Principle of Variation is in operation at
the time and causing non-proportional amplitudes. At other times, the mid-
channel pause may be scarcely distinguishable. In making use of the con-
cept of the mid-channel pause, the analyst should keep in mind the wide
variations possible in extent and nature of the effect.

LESSON

SPECIAL IDIOSYNCRACIES OF SHORT SALES continued

© Short-selling is subject to several inconveniences that must be taken into
consideration during transaction planning. These make short-selling less.
desirable than long transactions, but do not negate the practical need for
the shorting process.

© Stocks cannot be sold short until a 1/8th-dollar up-tick in price occurs,

© The holder of a short position in stocks must pay (to the owner of the stock
that was borrowed for the short sale) any dividends declared by the issuing
company during the transaction.

©@ To generate a given percentage capital gain, prices must move down more

ina short transaction than they must move up in a long transaction. For an
illustration of this point, consider the following example:

Long Situation
Buy price a
Sell price 38
Gross potential percentage
(38-27) x100 - 29.63%
Short Situation
Sell-short price 35,
Cover-short price 2

Gross potential percentage

(35-27) 100 = 22.86%

In this example, the buy and sell prices are exactly the same. Yet the gross
potential percentage realized on invested capital is 29.63% for the long
transaction and only 22.86% for the short transaction.

@ Iti possible to lose more than the initially invested capital in a short trans-
action but not in a long transaction.

$$$ rrge 15s


CYCLITEC
| SERVICES
(TRAINING
Ll COUASEO

rd

 
  

SPECIAL IDIOSYNCRACIES OF SHORT SALES continued
For example, suppose a stock is bought at 10.0 and eventually becomes
worthless. The loss is then 100% of the initial investment (if the purchase
is not made on margin).

But if the same stock were sold short at 10.0 and prices subsequently rose
to 60.0, the loss would be 500% of the initially invested capital (again as-
suming an unmargined transaction).
This is not really a practical concern for the cyclic analyst since stop-
loss orders would normally limit risk potential far below levels mentioned
in this example. Nevertheless, it is well to be aware of such factors and risks.
© Stock is not always available to be borrowed when a short transaction is
desired.
© In the commodity markets, no up-tick is presently required in order to
short, no dividends must be paid, and there is no problem of unavailability.
In general, the process of short-selling is simpler for commodities than it
is for stocks.

section six
©

HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES

© The basic cyclic principles state that all price movements of all negotiable
equities consist of the sum of more or less the same component price waves.

@ In spite of this, fundamental factors do affect price movement. And the
effects can be important in both long and short transactions.

© The effect of fundamental events is to alter the amplitude of one or more
price waves.

@ Wave periods and phases are not altered by fundamental events, hence
transaction timing decisions remain relatively uninfluenced.

@ Three kinds of fundamental interaction are known:

1. Sudden, dramatic, usually unpleasant, changes of lasting impact in
a publicly owned company that produce a sudden, one-sided increase
in volume interest in the company’s equities. As a result, an orderly
market in the equities cannot be maintained and there is a sudden
drastic price change. In these cases, trading in the stock is often halt-
ed on organized exchanges. If and when trading is resumed, it may
take place at a drastically altered price.

Wave action continues after the resumption of trading but cannot be
used to account for the sudden large change in price.

2. An unexpected news release or development, often favorable, but of
a more minor nature than that described for type 1. The news or de-
velopment may apply to a single company or may be national or inter-
national in scope. For an affected company’s equity price, the result
is a short, sharp increase in the amplitude of one or several adjacent,
period price waves, usually with periods of the order of 5, 10, or 20
days. This amplitude increase reverts back to normal as quickly as the
increase came about.

3. A fundamental event, rumor, or psychological force, usually favor-
able, which causes a more gradual increase in volume interest in an
equity. This results in a longer term, steady build-up in amplitude of
‘most or all of the waves in a cyclic model. Price movement, which is
the sum of these waves with proportionally increasing amplitudes,
shows development of a steep, usually upward, pseudo trend. This is a

 

Page mmm

 


LESSON

 

HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES continued

trend-like action in price movement caused only by increasing com-
ponent wave amplitudes; wave periods, and phases do not change.

{As volume interest subsides, wave amplitudes also decrease, leading
toa steep pseudo trend in the opposite direction, usually downward.

© The skilled cyclic analyst can make good use of pseudo trend, but the aver-
age practitioner is well advised to avoid such situations.

@ Figure 5-4, a weekly chart for Illustrated World Encyclopedia as of 29 June
1973, shows a type one fundamental interaction with stock prices. On Fri-
day, 21 April 1972 (the last day of the week that is located on grid line 117),
the stock price dropped sharply (by 10%) to close at 12-1/2, the low for the
week. On the following Monday, 24 April, the American Stock Exchange
did not allow trading in the issue pending “clarification of news” concern-
ing the company. On Tuesday, 25 April, an article appeared in The Wall
Street Journal stating that criminal charges had been brought against three
officials of the company.

  

© When the stock began trading again some seventeen weeks later (after a
complex legal battle and the resignation of the president of the company),
prices were in the 4-1/2 to 7 range and moved downward thereafter.

@ Figure 5-5, a daily chart for Litton Industries as of 31 March 1972, illus-
trates the type two fundamental interaction. The price drop between grid
lines 144 and 147 was in response to the news that the government had di
allowed $450 million in contract changes claimed by Litton’s ship building
division.

  

© The fundamental interaction shown in figure 5-5 affected only a single com-
pany and the effect was in the downward direction. Figure 5-6 illustrates
another type two fundamental interaction, but this time of national import-
ance and with an effect in the upward direction.

© The chart in figure 5-6 is a daily plot of the Dow Jones Industrial Average
as of 31 December 1971. The price jump between grid lines 39 and 42 is in
response to the president's August 1971 announcement of the Phase 1 eco-
nomic controls.

@ A type three fundamental interaction is shown in figure 5-7, a weekly chart
