WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE

© In this and workshops to follow, progressively less detail on individual anal-
ysis steps will be provided. Correct results will be shown in all instances,
and you should spend considerable time at first reconciling any differences
between your own analyses and the ones presented.

@ Assume each situation is under study for the first time and that the results
of other analyses for the equity under consideration are not available. This
is good procedure at all times because a fresh analytical viewpoint is as-
sured and the long-term effects of the Principle of Variation are avoided.

© Figures 5-8, 5-9, and 5-10 are, respectively, an analysis form, a weekly chart
of LTR as of 29 December 1972, and a daily chart of LTR as of 29 Decem-
ber 1972. Remove these from the lesson and conduct both a VRS and an RS
analysis (for the sake of experience) on this stock. Summarize your conclus-
ions before comparing them with the results described below. Remember,
the question you are attempting to answer is: “Should a CCA be conducted
in this issue at this time?” If so, you should also know whether it is a long
or a short transaction that makes additonal effort worthwhile, and you
should know the approximate goals of the transaction.

© Rapid analysis should have isolated the following facts:

1, 18-month (80 week) lows exist at grid line 122 and between grid lines
43 and 56.

2. The pre-dominant wave is the nominal 9-month (40 week) wave (in
spite of the near absence of Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctua-
tions between grid lines SO and 105).

3. 2Lis sharply up from grid line $0 through grid line 122. This observa-
tion is based on an overshot 80-week upside FLD projection, 80-week
high translation to the right, an undershot 80-week downside FLD
projection, and the very late 80-week FLD break on the downside (all
between grid lines 50 and 122),

4, Between grid lines 122 and 179, ¥L sharply reversed to the down-
side,

Did you catch this important point in your analysis? Let's discuss it
briefly.


rd

 

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE continued

The trend underlying the 9-month wave consists of the sum of 3L
(trend underlying the 18-month wave) and the 18-month wave. As
price rises from the 18-month nest-of-lows at grid line 122 to the price
high associated with the 9-month wave at grid line 142, the 18-month
wave is sharply up. This coupled with a 3L that is assumed to be up
should provide a very strong uptrend underlying the 9-month wave.
Thus, the 9-month FLD projection upside should be overshot—but
instead it was considerably undershot. Since the direction of the 18-
month wave is known beyond doubt, this could only have been caused
by 3L reversing to the downside in this time region.

5. If the conclusion of item 4 is valid, what should then be expected for
the following 9-month FLD downside projection (between grid lines
142 and 169)?

During this time period the 18-month wave is still up. This coupled
with a EL that is down should result in flat trend underlying the 9-
month wave. Thus, an FLD projection based on this wave should be
met. This is confirmed by actual price action quite precisely.

‘There are now two bits of evidence, each strong, confirming ¥L direc-
tion as down as of 29 December 1972.

(Note: You may have noticed that the trend conditions here are the
same as they were for the previous 9-month FLD upside projection;
and if the underlying trend was flat there, the upside objective should
have been met rather than undershot. One possible explanation for
the fact that the objective was undershot would be that the very sharp
price drop at grid lines 120-122 was due to some fundamental inter-
action and that the undisturbed cyclic price drop would not have been
so great and therefore would not have produced such a high upside
price objective. But it is very important to realize that while this ex-
planation might satisfy us for this anomaly in this instance, there will
be other instances in which an anomaly exists for which we cannnt
find a satisfactory explanation. We must recognize that because of the
probabilistic nature of the cyclic phenomenon and of our tools and
techniques, every situation will not be clean and clear cut. Valuable

). The conclusion to be drawn is that this stock must not be bought at

Present price is about 47, so a 17 point drop is anticipated. This would
be a 36% move. There is no longer any doubt that LTR should be sub-
jected toa CCA in expectation of an excellent short sale in the very
near future.

© The weekly FPA, shown in figure 5-11, was straightforward with the only
difficulties experienced at the 18-month low between grid lines 40 and 60,
and the 9-month low between 165 and 175.

© The 18-month possibles resolved normally (to grid line 43) as shorter period
waves were phased. But the 9-month low would have been placed at grid
line 169 during phasing of the weekly chart. It was only after the daily chart
was phased that this placement was changed to the unusual position at grid-
line 166.

© The daily chart, shown in figure 5-12, offered no difficulties except between
grid lines 80 and 142, Again, phasing of shorter period waves leads to
choice of the 48.9-day wave troughs at grid lines 86 and 129 (as opposed to
the possibles at 106 and 142). This choice was also confirmed by the fluc-
tuations of the Dominancy Envelope centerline, leading to the suspicion
that the sharp price declines between grid lines 99 and 106 and grid lines
135 and 142 were due to fundamental interaction in the form of unusually
heavy selling.

© This situation in the daily chart leads to revision of the weekly phasing to

match (the move of the three-diamond nest-of-lows from an original loca-
tion at grid line 169 to the location shown at 166).

© The daily portion of the current cyclic model includes a situation that is
fairly common and warrants somecomment. The 72.1-day wave is shown as

“uy ‘ssoug Ss9pedy E161 @ 1YBKdo)

section eight

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO continued

93 days along with a bottoming status. The question arises of how long such
a wave should be carried with an overdue phase designation if no proof of
the expected trough is forthcoming. A good rule of thumb for these situ-
ations is that if proof of a trough has not been obtained by the time the
trough of the next shorter wave is due, then it should be assumed that a
trough of the wave did occur at a time given by adding the average period
of the wave to the time of the last known trough. The phase reference is
then shifted to this new trough location, and the wave status is adjusted
accordingly

In the example of figure 5-12 this rule of thumb would be implemented as
follows. Suppose that proof of the 72.1-day wave trough continued to elude
us until the phase was shown as 108 days along (72 days plus one-half of 72
days). At that time we would arbitrarily locate a phasing diamond for the
72.1-day wave at grid line 158 (72 days from the last known trough) and rec-
cord the phase as 36 days along. The wave status would then be shown as
topping

@ Now direct your attention back to figure 5-11. Notice the two broad arrows
marked with a dollar sign and with a wave period. These are the standard
symbols for FLD price projections. The width of the arrow is a reminder
that these projections are indicative, but not precise. Such symbols are nor-
mally placed directly below or above the break involved. The wave period
associated with the projection is noted below the arrow for downside projec-
tions and above the arrow for upside projections. The small arrow shown
with the symbol indicates the best available estimate as to whether the pro-
jection is likely to be undershot, met, or overshot, based upon trend.

 
 

@ The FLD projections shown in figure 5-1] are derived using a refinement

that needs introduction,

The exact point at which an FLD break occurs, and therefore the value of
the FLD price projection itself, can be considerably infuenced by steep
Price moves due to short period waves. To avoid this effect, note the fluctu-
ations of both the price motion and the FLD near the time of a break and
visually smooth these out. Base the FLD price projection on the intersection.
of the smoothed price and FLD movements. Realize, however, that the
price and FLD fluctuations smoothed must be due to waves that are consid-
erably shorter than the wave on which the FLD is based.

true price movements and FLD movements must be used.

© We expect to sell short during the price rally from the 10-week nest-of-lows

SUMMARY


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
All Rights Reserved

mT fr

 

 

© Cyclic concepts permit a wholly technical approach to trading and invest-
ing in all kinds of negotiable equities. These concepts are equally adapt-
able to long, intermediate, and short-term transactions.

© The methods and techniques derived from cyclic concepts permit probabil-
istic evaluation of potential transactions and of transactions in progress.

© The process of cyclic analysis is based upon eight basic Principles. These
are the Principle of Commonality, Cyclicality, Summation, Harmonicity,
Synchronicity, Proportionality, Nominality, and Variation.

© These Principles describe the interrelationship of price waves of which all
price movement is composed. Such price waves are characterized by three
parameters; Amplitude (or size), Period (or length), and Phase (or the place-
ment of the wave in time).

© The basic cyclic principles state that all negotiable equity price movements
consist of a sum of price waves; that such waves tend to have harmonically
related periods; that wave troughs tend to occur simultaneously; that a
specific set of harmonic waves is peculiar to price movements; and that
individual equity wave characteristics vary with time about a long-term
norm,

© A cyclic model is a tabulation of some of the characteristics of price waves.
A useful simplified nominal model consists of waves of periods 18 years, 9
years, 54 months, 18 months, 9 months, 20 weeks, 80 days, 40 days, 20 days,
10 days, and 5 days.

© Cyclic concepts provide a particularly useful definition of trend. A different
trend exists underlying each wave of a cyclic model, and each trend consists
of the sum of all waves longer than the wave that the trend underlies. Both
up and down trends can be in existence at the same time.

© The cyclic definition of trend results in the ability to classify trend lines.
Such trend lines are known as valid trend lines, or VTL’s, VTL’s are con-
structed according to specific rules. They always connect consecutive price
lows or highs associated with the troughs or crests of a specific wave. They
are drawn so as not to cut through (or intersect) price bars. And a valid
downtrend line is not drawn from price highs between which there is a
trough of a wave longer than the wave on which the VTLis based.

ge


 

rt

 

LESSON T

 

summary

continued

© VTL’s may be used to identify price highs or lows associated with crests or
troughs of specific waves. When prices break up through a valid downtrend
line the probabilities favor that the lowest price low within a span of past
time equal to one period of the wave next longer than the VTL wave is as-
sociated with a trough of this next longer wave. Similarly, when prices
break down through a valid uptrend line, the probabilities favor that the
highest price high within a span of past time equal to one period of the wave
next longer than the VTL wave is associated with a crest of this next longer
wave.

© There is a future line of demarcation, or FLD, associated with each wave
of a cyclic model. FLD'’s are constructed by shifting price values forward
by one-half the period of a wave plus one. This shift value is called the FLD
number.

© FLD’s may also be used to help locate price highs or lows associated with
wave crests or troughs. When an FLD based on a given wave is broken by
prices, the probabilities favor that the highest price high or lowest price low
within a span of past time equal to the FLD number is associated with a
ctest or a trough (respectively) of the wave on which the FLD is based.

© FLD’s are the principal means by which the extent of future price
can be estimated. When an FLD is broken by prices, prices have moved ap-
proximately one-half as far as that wave is likely to carry them. The likeli-
hood of such an FLD price objective being undershot, met, or overshot is
influenced by whether the trend underlying the wave on which the FLD is
based is opposed to, flat, or in the direction of (respectively) the FLD
projection.

@ FLD’s may be drawn and used in both past and future time on the chart.
When drawn in future time, both single FLD shape and the patterns form-
ed by multiple FLD's have utility in analysis processes.

@ When an FLD is moving down in future time, prices tend to be pushed up
at that time by the wave on which the FLD is based. When an FLD is mov-
ing up in future time, prices tend to be pushed down at that time by the
wave on which the FLD is based. When an FLD forms a trough in future
time, prices tend to form a top at that time. When an FLD forms a crest
in future time, prices tend to form a low at that time.

© When prices are moving down, some FLD’s will be above prices. The more
FLD’s that are above prices, the more vulnerable prices are to an upward
reversal. When prices are moving up, some FLD’s will be below prices. The
more FLD’s that are below prices, the more vulnerable prices are to a down-
ward reversal. This is known as the magnet effect.

@ An FLD cascade is likely to occur when a price break of a single FLD im-
plies that another FLD will be broken, etc. FLD cascade formations occur

when FLD's are sloping steeply, are more-or-less parallel to each other,
and rather evenly spaced.

@ A congestion of FLD’s in a horizontal region is called a pause zone, or

PZ. P2's represent price regions where prices are likely to pause or turn
around.

© Cyclic concepts can be used to set up logical price objectives and stop-loss
levels. Once this is done, both profit potential (potential) and loss risk (risk)
can be calculated. The ratio of potential to risk (or potential divided by

tisk) is called the PR ratio and is an important factor in transaction eval-
uation.

© Potential percentage is potential expressed as a percentage of the transac-

tion entry price. It is found by multiplying potential by 100 and dividing by
the transaction entry price.

@ The concepts of PR ratio and potential percentage permit transaction entry
price values to be investigated as to desirability and acceptability. The var-
ious quantities involved are related by simple relationships, as follows:

© A current cyclic model is a tabulation of the characteristics of the price
waves that sum to form price movement. It is constructed as follows: First,
an initial cyclic model is formed. This is used as a guide in preparing a
phasing analysis. The information required for a current cyclic model is,
then extracted from the phasing analysis.

© An initial cyclic model is prepared using wave period estimates from the
nominal cyclic model and from a Periodogram. A Periodogram is a set of
vertical lines, each line representing a single wave. For each line, the hori-
zontal position of the line represents the wave period and the height of the
line represents the wave amplitude. A Periodogram is interpreted using the
Principles of Nominality and Harmonicity. Individual lines in a price-
movement Petiodogram are not of importance. It is the shape formed by
groups of such lines that denote the presence or absence of a given wave in
price movement.

© A phasing analysis (or formal phasing analysis, or FPA) is the means by
which an intial cyclic model is converted into the more accurate wave peri-
od and phase estimates needed in a current cyclic model. An FPA is a

“uy ‘ssaug SUOPEAL E161 @ WBUAdO)


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

 

All Rights Reserved

LESSON

summary 5

best attempt to estimate the time positions of all of the troughs of all of the
waves of which the price movement under analysis is composed.

© A Dominancy Envelope contains all (or most) of price movement. Its cen-

terline tends to fluctuate in sympathy with the wave just longer than the
longest wave contained by the envelope. The former is called the pre-
dominant wave, while the latter is known as the dominant wave. The Dom-
inancy Envelope and its centerline are said to act as a period-divider, since
price movement is effectively separated into two (simpler) composite waves.
In the FPA process, the Dominancy Envelope and its centerline function as
allink, or bridge, between the initial cyclic model and price movement.

 

© Inthe FPA process, FLD’s and VTL’s are used in past time to help locate
price highs and lows associated with given wave crests and troughs.

@ FPA takes place in three steps: entry, extension, and completion.

© FPA entry can be effected by observing the Dominancy Envelope centerline
fluctuations; by direct comparison of initial cyclic model period estimates
with Dominancy Envelope centerline fluctuations or price movement; and
by locating troughs of long waves using FLD’s and VTL's in past time.

© FPA extension is an ordered procedure. Shorter wave troughs are placed
(by Synchronicity) in the same time positions located for longer waves. This
is called a first-step extension. Second-step extension consists of using an
augmented measuring strip to locate troughs of the next shorter wave be-
tween adjacent troughs of the wave just phased. In third-step extension, the
augmented measuring strip is used to search for troughs of the next shorter
wave that are to the right or left of single troughs of the wave just phased.
Fourth step extension is accomplished by direct comparison of the wave
period on an augmented measuring strip with raw price data.

(An augmented measuring strip is one which starts with initial eyclie mod-
el indications of period on it, and picks up additional markings as new pe-
riod samples are located.)

© FPA completion proceeds from a fully-phased short-period wave to suc-
cessively longer waves. The process consists of filling phasing gaps by
application of the Principle of Harmonicity.

 

rect

© FPA information extraction begins with averaging the wave-period samples

summary

for each wave. The easiest way to do this is to note the difference between
the grid-line numbers for the two extreme phasing diamonds of a given
wave. This difference is then divided by the number of period samples con-
tained between the extremes. Average wave periods and period-sample var-
iation from the average are laid out in future time to gain an estimate of
when future nests-of-lows are likely to form. The phase of each wave is
noted as the time elapsed since the last known trough of the wave, and the
wave direction is inferred from the phase.

@ FPA always begins with a long wave and extends toward consecutively
shorter waves. FPA completion always begins with a fully-phased short
wave, and proceeds toward consecutively longer waves.

© A completed FPA provides the information needed to construct a current
cyclic model. The information tabulated in a current cyclic model consists
of the nominal-model wave periods, the corresponding average periods
from the FPA, the phase of each wave at present time, the estimated direc-
tion of action of each wave at present time, and the wave FLD numbers.

© Time translation, which is caused by trend, is a shift in the time positions of
price highs or lows relative to the associated wave crests or troughs. The
combined effects of the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity cause
high translation to be much more pronounced than low translation, This
is the reason why price lows are used in FPA instead of price highs. When
trend is down, low translation is to the right and high translation is to the

left. When trend is up, low translation is to the left and high translation is
to the right.

 

 

isk potential is an estimate of the capital that will be lost if a transaction
is stopped out. Risk potential can be estimated quite accurately. Trans-
action risk is the likelihood of being stopped out of a transaction instead

of terminating the transaction at a profit. Transaction risk cannot be eval-
uated directly, but a properly conducted cyclic analysis provides insight and
feel for this quantity. This kind of risk is high for edge-band transactions,
especially for long transactions entered near nests-of-lows that contain long
waves and for short transactions entered near expected price tops. Trans-
action risk is minimized when trend supports the expected move, when

analysis evidence is non-ambiguous, and when transaction entry is effected
at mid-band.

fr

 

© The concepts of mid band and edge band are closely related to the idea of
a trading cycle. The trading cycle is the longest wave of the composite wave
determined to be responsible for price move expectations. Transactions
entered at or near the price lows or highs of the trading cycle are known as
edge-band transactions. Transactions entered at or near the center of the
expected price move associated with the trading cycle are known as mid-
band transactions.

@ A trading cycle is determined prior to each transaction. This may be done
by measuring the percentage price moves associated with various compos-
ite waves. The longest wave in a composite wave that produces acceptable
percentage price moves is then the trading cycle. Alternatively, if price pro-
jections are available, the trading cycle is the longest wave of the composite
wave responsible for the projected price movements.

© A current cyclic model permits estimation of future time trend underlying
each wave of the model. This is particularly important with regard to the
trading cycle. The process consists of adding a fixed amount of time to the
phase of each wave involved in a particular trend and determining the
new wave-action directions. The number of up directions and down direc-
tions are noted, and the net difference is used as the desired indication of
trend direction at the new time.

© Action signals are price events that confirm anticipated wave action. The
most commonly used action signals are price breaks of VTL’s, FLD's, or
horizontal price lines. There are many different kinds of action signals
including stop-loss levels, buy signals, sell signals, sell-short signals, cover-
short signals, profit-preservation signals and situation warnings.

© A complete cyclic analysis is called a CCA and typically requires 40 min-
utes to 1-1/2 hours. Two rapid-analysis modes are also available called
VRS (very rapid scan) and RS (rapid scan).

© ACCA consists of a Periodogram interpretation leading to an initial cyc-
lic model; use of the initial cyclic model in conjunction with the Dominancy
Envelope and its centerline to initiate an FPA; completion of the FPA; ex-
traction of information from the FPA; construction of a current cyclic
model; and complete VTL-FLD analysis, including FLD pattern interpre-
tation.

 


 

ry

summary

continued ©

© VRS and RS methods are used to scan a stable of issues rapidly to isolate
those equities in which transaction opportunities may be near. These
methods make extensive use of two-finger measurements on a chart, and of
past-time FLD implications (obtained without actually drawing in the
FLD’s). VRS requires about 5 minutes while RS requires about 20 minutes.

 

© The process of transaction analysis consists of a number of organized steps.
VRS or RS analysis is used to determine whether a CCA is warranted. If
s0, a CCA is conducted, including FLD and VTL layout and analysis from
which current and future wave status estimates are formed and transaction
risk is estimated.

Price objectives and stop-loss levels are then determined, after which PR
ratios and potential percentages versus transaction entry prices are studied.
Action signals are selected; the trading cycle is determined; future trends
are noted; and a maximum buy price (or minimum sell-short price) is es-
tablished.

Not until this entire effort is complete is the analyst in a position to make
a decision to enter a transaction or to wait for another opportunity.

© FLD’s can be used in past and near-current time to aid in detecting various
trend changes. In this application, single FLD price projections are com-
pared to actual price performance. Then trend implications are derived by
noting whether FLD objectives are over-shot, met, or undershot. Trend is
presumed to be in the direction of the objective when the objective is over-
shot, flat when the objective is just met, and against the direction of the ob-
jective when the objective is undershot.

© tis most important to note the presence of outstanding (non-retired) FLD
price objectives from the past. An FLD price objective is retired when
satisfactory evidence is in hand that the wave crest or trough that was to
produce the price low or high associated with the objective has been passed.

© The process of cyclic analysis is never mechanical. Throughout, the tech-
nique of “building evidence” and the use of cyclic concept logic is required.

© Difficult analytical problems should be approached first by searching basic
knowledge for a possible solution; second, by determining whether the
problem really requires solution before a transaction decision can be made;
and third, by continuing the analysis, to see if a solution to the problem

CF

 

“au ‘smug S19pUAL £161 @ WHKIOD


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.
‘All Rights Reserved

CYCLITEC

COURSES |

MTT TT) [ea
a
i

does not come out of further developments. Analytical problems are often
resolved routinely as the analysis progresses.

© When analytical problems are encountered, an appraisal should be made of
the impact on the transaction decision of a possible error in the problem
solution. A large part of the skill of cyclic analysis lies in the ability to de-
termine which analytical results should cause concern and which should
not.

‘© Minimum potential percentage is a function of trading or investment goals,
but should not be less than about 20%. Transactions with PR ratios of one
are no better than gambling if transaction risk is $0% or higher. Since
transaction risk cannot be accurately evaluated, PR ratios less than two
should not be employed.

© Price tops are intrinsically different from price bottoms. The combined
effects of the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity are responsi-
ble for this, causing sharp lows and rounded tops. In addition, low transla-
tion effects tend to be small while high translation effects tend to be large.

© Because of these factors, short transactions should be handled in a differ
ent way than long transactions. In general, short sales should take place
near trading cycle mid band and should then only be made when sufficient
price movement remains to satisfy PR ratio and potential percentage re-
quirements.

© Short transactions have certain special characteristics: A given price move
produces less potential percentage than a long transaction. For stocks, a
1/8th up-tick is required to short; the short-seller is required to pay any
dividends that may come due during the transaction; it is possible to lose
more than the initially invested capital even if margin is not used; and stock
may not be available for borrowing at the desired time. In general, it is
easier to short commodities than to short stocks.

© The price movements of all equities consist of the sum of price waves with
more or Jess common characteristics. Nevertheless, fundamental factors
do influence price. They do so by interacting with cyclic phenomena, gen-
erally causing changes in wave amplitudes. Wave periods and phases are
unaffected, thus transaction timing is not deranged by fundamental con-
siderations.

© There is more to managing funds using cyclic concepts than setting up a
transaction. Intra-transaction analysis is necessary as well as fund-place-
ment strategy and rigid emotional control. Such topics will be covered in
following lessons.

© This completes your study summary of the elemental cyclic concepts pre-
sented so far. It is most important that you grasp the meaning and implica-
tions of all of this material before proceeding with your training. If there are
concepts that are not yet clear in your mind, you should study the appropri-
ate portion of the material in lessons one through five until understanding is
achieved. Do not be satisfied with merely taking and scoring the examina-
tion for this lesson. Use it as a means of determining that the meaning and
implications of the point in question are clear in your mind. Time and effort
spent on this summary and exam will be well repaid during the lessons
to follow.

GLOSSARY

Page S3—


‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

LESSON

 

ACTION SIGNAL A price break of a VTL, FLD, or horizontal price line
indicating a strong likelihood that an anticipated price move is under way.

AMPLITUDE The size or amount of the price movement involved as a price
wave goes from trough to crest.

AUGMENTED MEASURING STRIP A measuring strip that has had pe-

riod samples added to the original nominal-model and Periodogram wave
period estimates.

AVERAGE PERIOD A wave period estimate formed by averaging two or
more trough-to-trough measurements.

BAR-CHART A common way of plotting, or graphing, equity prices. The
high and low prices for a day are joined by a vertical straight line to show the
range over which prices traded for that day. The consecutive set of such lines
is called a daily bar-chart of that equity. Similarly, the high and low prices for
‘a week can be compiled to form a weekly bar-chart for the equity.

BOTTOMING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a
trough.

BREAK Prices break a VTL or an FLD when they pass from one side of the
VTL or FLD to the other (from above to below, or vice versa).

CASCADE A rippling, or one-thing-followed-by-another, effect. In cyclic
theory, the tendency under certain circumstances for an FLD break to predict,
prices which will break another FLD, thus predicting still greater price moves,
ete.

CCA See complete cyclic analysis.

COMMONALITY A term indicating that price movements of negotiable

equities have similar qualities in spite of the diversity of visual appearances
of price charts.

COMPLETE CYCLIC ANALYSIS orCCA The most extensive cyclic analysis

possible. Used prior to each transaction to assure that no wave information of

importance has been overlooked. The primary means by which transaction
risk is evaluated.

COMPLETION As applied to phasing analysis, the process of filling phasing
gaps after entry and extension have been accomplished.

 

Page 54——


rr

VICES
INNING
| COURSEO

 

COMPONENT A single wave in price movement or in the composite wave
formed from several single waves.

COMPOSITE WAVE The single new form resulting from the combination
by summation of two or more price waves.

CONSECUTIVE TROUGHS Wave troughs from consecutive cycles of a
given wave.

poasasay sunt ny
“UY 'SS24q S19PELL CLGT @ WIBUKdOD

CREST or WAVE CREST The highest point on a component price wave.

CURRENT CYCLIC MODEL A tabulation of wave periods, phases, and
directions of action. The best available estimate of the values for these charac-
teristics of the waves active in the price movement of a given equity at a given
time. Usually includes FLD numbers for easy reference.

CYCLE One complete movement of a price wave from one trough to the
next consecutive trough. QO

CYCLICALITY Derived from the word cycle. A generic term referring to the
wave phenomena in the price action of equities.

CYCLIC ANALYSIS. The means by which cyclic concepts are used to pro-
duce transaction-decision information.

CYCLIC MODEL See current cyclic model.

DIRECT COMPARISON A technique whereby available period estimates
are laid out on a measuring strip which is then visually compared with price
action.

DOMINANT WAVE or DOMINANT COMPONENT The longest wave con-
tained by the Dominancy Envelope.

DOMINANCY ENVELOPE A computer generated aid present on Cyclitee
charts. A constant vertical height envelope containing most of price move-
ment. Contains the dominant wave and all shorter waves. Serves as a wave-
period divider.

DOMINANCY ENVELOPE CENTERLINE A smooth curve central to the
Dominancy Envelope. The sum of all waves longer than the dominant wave.

DOWNSIDE Ina downward direction.


is Ine.
Copyright © 1973 Traders Press,
‘All Rights Reserved

LESSON

 

DOWNTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price highs.
‘The later price high is lower than the earlier price high so that the line slants
downward to the right.

EDGE-BAND TRANSACTION A transaction that is entered at a major
nest-of-lows near the edge of the trading cycle channel.

EL Read sigma et. Stands for sum long. Consists of the sum of all waves
longer than the longest wave identified and phased.

ENTRY The means of starting a phasing analysis. The placement of the
first phasing diamonds of a phasing analysis.

EQUITY or NEGOTIABLE EQUITY A generic label for securities, com-
modity futures contracts, etc., traded on an auction market.

EXTENSION As applied to a phasing analysis, the means of phasing suc-
cessively shorter waves after an entry has been effected.

FLD An acronym for Future Line of Demarcation. An FLD is a forward-
shifted replica of past price averages and has useful properties.

FLD NUMBER A number used in constructing an FLD. It is found by divid-
ing the period of the wave on which the FLD is based by two, then adding one.

FLD PRICE PROJECTION The process by which an analyst can, at the time
of an FLD price break, form an estimate of the extent of the existing price
move.

FLD PRICE OBJECTIVE The price to which an analyst expects prices to go
as a result of an FLD price projection,

FORMAL PHASING ANALYSIS A complete phasing analysis with a full set
of phasing diamonds, as distinguished from a more cursory phasing analysis
that might result from VRS or RS.

FPA See formal phasing analysis.

FUTURE TIME POSITION A place on an equity price bar-chart corres-
ponding to some time in the future; that is, some time to the right of the last
known price bar on the chart.

GAP Ina row of phasing diamonds, a space too long to represent a period

 

PARE Sb


LESSON

—— Page 57.

glossary

continued

 

sample, On a Cyclitec chart, a space where no price bar is shown because
the market was closed that day.

GRID LINE NUMBER Refers to the time scale reading on a Cyclitec chart
corresponding with a given vertical grid line on the chart. A convenient ref-
erence for various activities displayed on a Cyclitec chart.

HARMONICITY Derived from the word harmonious or harmonic. Means
that wave periods are related by small, whole numbers.

HIGH TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price highs
versus wave crests.

INITIAL CYCLIC MODEL A tabulated set of wave periods derived from
Periodogram interpretation and the Principle of Nominality. Used as a guide
in setting up a current cyclic model.

LONG To go long in an equity is to purchase it in the expectation of selling
it later at a higher price.

LOW TRANSLATION The time translation effect as related to price lows
versus wave troughs.

MARGIN The actual amount of capital provided by an investor when part
of the capital necessary for a transaction is borrowed against the stock, bond,
or other equity involved.

MEASURING STRIP A strip of paper with estimated periods laid out on
it. Used in phasing analysis.
Also, a strip of paper with an FLD number laid out on it. Used for FLD
measurements.

MID-BAND TRANSACTION A transaction that is entered at a minor nest-
of-lows near the middle of the trading cycle channel.

MID-CHANNEL PAUSE A significant slowdown or double reversal in price
movement that occurs approximately midway between the trading cycle high
and the trading cycle low.

MOST SIGNIFICANT PRICE LOW Means most visually evident. Applied
to the coarsest price movement between two consecutive wave troughs. A price
low that is made up of a greater number of coincident wave troughs than are
adjacent price lows.

LESSON

 

NEIGHBORING As applied to waves, refers to two waves in a set of waves
whose periods are closest to being the same.

NEST-OF-LOWS A time when two or more component price waves form
simultaneous troughs.

NOMINAL MODEL or NOMINAL CYCLIC MODEL list of the periods
of the price waves to be expected in price movements.

NOW TIME Synonymous with current, or present, time. The time at which
an analysis is being made. The latest time for which price data is available.

PAST-TIME FLD An FLD plotted in the chart area representing time prior
to current time. Usually used to aid identification of wave trough location.
Uses a simplified FLD number consisting of merely one-half of the wave
period estimate.

» PAST-TIME POSITION A location on an equity price bar-chart correspond-
ing to some time in the past; that is, some time to the left of the last known
price bar on the chart.

 

PERIOD The length of time that elapses between a trough of a price wave

and the next consecutive trough of the same wave.

PERIODOGRAM A convenient means of presenting information about
many price waves simultaneously. A plot of wave amplitude versus wave pe-
riod. Consists of a series of vertical lines, each representing a potential wave.
Line height indicates amplitude, while horizontal line position indicates
period.

PERIOD SAMPLE A measurement of elapsed time between estimated posi-
tions of consecutive wave troughs.

PHASE The time relationship between two price waves.

Al Rights Reserved

PHASING ANALYSIS A procedure for isolating the wave components pre-
sent in a complex composite wave. A series of methods whereby wave trough
positions are estimated. The means of refining an initial cyclic model into a
current cyclic model.

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

PHASING DIAMOND A diamond-shaped mark placed on a chart to denote
the best estimate of the time location of a wave trough.

glossary taAINinG
i COURSEO

POTENTIAL Short for potential profit. The difference between the entry
and exit prices of a transaction expressed in price units.

POTENTIAL PERCENTAGE Anticipated profit (potential) expressed as a
percentage of the transaction entry commitment.

PR An acronym for Potential-Risk ratio. The ratio of potential to risk;
that is, potential divided by risk.

PREDICTION An estimate of something expected to happen in the future.
Never exact, always an expected high likelihood.

PRE-DOMINANT WAVE or PRE-DOMINANT COMPONENT The wave
just longer than the dominant wave. The shortest wave that is part of the sum
of waves comprising the Dominancy Envelope centerline.

PRICE ACTION or PRICE MOVEMENT Refers to the fact that equity
prices change constantly as time passes. The motion of prices as time goes on. )

PRICE-MOVEMENT PERIODOGRAM A Periodogram constructed by
analysis of price data. Interpreted by noting the period of the tallest line of
each set that forms a peak in the Periodogram shape.

poasasoy RIV
-ouy ‘ssa4g S49PBAL £L61 @ WSO

PROBABILISTIC Having to do with probabilities. Dealing with likelihood
stronger than possibility, but falling short of certainty.

PROPORTIONALITY orPROPORTIONAL TO A relationship between
‘two quantities such that as one increases, the other also increases, and by the
same percentage. In cyclic concepts, relates to the amplitude and period of
the various waves in a cyclic model.

PSEUDO TREND A trend-like price movement that is caused by price wave
amplitude changes stemming from fundamental events.

PZ Anacronym for Pause Zone. A price region in which one or more FLD's
tend to congregate in a horizontal band. A price region where prices tend to
slow-down or reverse a cascade movement.”

RALLY An upward price movement.

RANGE OF PERIOD ESTIMATES A time span corresponding to the dif-
ference between maximum and minimum estimates of the period of a given
wave.

RAINING il
COURSEO LL)

 

RAPID SCAN or RS A simplified method of cyclic analysis requiring about
twenty minutes.

RISK Short for Joss risk. The amount that will be lost in case the transaction
is terminated because prices penetrate the stop-loss level.

SHORT or SELL SHORT To sell borrowed stock in the expectation that the
price will subsequently go down. If the price does go down, a profit is made by
replacing the borrowed stock with stock purchased on the open market at the
reduced price.

STABLE A group of equity charts that are updated, followed, and analyzed
regularly.

STOP-LOSS LEVEL A special kind of action signal. A price level at which a

transaction is terminated because the probabilities no longer favor a profit-
able outcome. This action usually results in a loss on the transaction,

SUMMATION The act of summing, or adding.

SYNCHRONICITY Derived from the word synchronous or synchronize.
Means to occur simultaneously in time. In cyclic theory, the term refers to the
tendency of the troughs of one component wave to occur at the same time as
the troughs of other component waves in the same composite wave.

TIME-ALONG A term used as a convenient measurement of wave phase.
Means time elapsed since last known wave trough.

TIME SCALE Ona Cyclitec chart, a series of numbers laid out horizontally
and corresponding to a count of the vertical grid lines. A convenient means of

referring to specific grid lines.
Also a convenient means of measuring elapsed time between marks on a

measuring strip.

TIME TRANSLATION The shift that underlying trend causes in the time
position of price lows and highs so that they no longer coincide with associated
wave troughs and crests.

TOPPING In reference to a wave, means that the wave is forming a crest.

TRADING CYCLE The longest wave of the composite wave relied upon to
take prices from the transaction entry price to the objective.

Page 6mm

 


 

LESSON

Page 61.

 

TRANSACTION RISK The probability of a transaction terminating at a loss
as opposed to terminating with a profit.

TREND Generally, the strength and direction of price moveinent if short,
term fluctuations in price are ignored. In cyclic theory, trend is the sum of all
price waves longer in period than the specific wave for which trend is being
defined.

TREND LINE A line drawn across'specially selected price highs (or price
lows).

TROUGH or WAVE TROUGH The lowest point on a component price
wave.

UPSIDE In an upward direction.

UP-TICK A small price move in the upward direction.

UPTREND LINE A trend line connecting specially selected price lows. The
later price low is higher than the earlier price low so that the line slants up-
ward to the right.

VALID TREND LINE A trend line drawn according to specific rules so that
the result has meaning in terms of cyclic concepts. Abbreviated VTL.

VARIATION In cyclic concepts, refers to the fact that actual local wave
conditions differ from the nominal, or long-term average, expectation.

VERY RAPID SCAN or VRS A simplified method of cyclic analysis requir-
ing about five minutes.

VOLATILITY INDEX A number appearing in the heading line of Cyclitee
charts. Gives a measure of the relative volatility of the issue; that is, the per-
centage rate, or speed, with which prices for the issue tend to change.

VRS See very rapid scan.

WAVE As applied to price movement, a repetitive fluctuation in price with
well defined, measurable characteristics.

WAVE DIRECTION The best estimate of the direction in which a given
price wave is tending to move prices at present time. Spoken of as being up,
down, flat, bottoming, or topping.

WAVE-PERIOD DIVIDER A means of separating the sum of all waves
forming a given price movement into two simpler sums of waves. Each of the
two sums consists of contiguous waves of the cyclic model.

The Dominancy Envelope and its centerline function as a wave-period divider,

WAVE PHASE An indication of the location in time of the wave shape. Con-

veniently measured as time elapsed from the most recent wave trough to

current, or present, time,

It is suggested that you go all the way through the examination a first time
without referring to the cassette or text material, In doing so, mark answers
only to the questions you are sure of: don’t guess. Leaving an answer un-
marked will flag that question for further attention on the second pass
through the exam.

After you have finished the first pass, check the answers you have marked
against the answer key, circling any that are wrong. Then go back through the
examination and refer to the text material to refresh your memory on those
questions that you answered wrong or left unanswered.

An FLD and a VTL (both based upon the same wave) are broken by
prices simultaneously. Under these circumstances:

a. The VTL break cancels the meaning of the FLD break

SECTION ONE REVIEW
In lessons one through three you learned of the basic eyclic concepts and of,
the important methods and processes for analyzing an equity. Then, in lesson
four, you put these ideas and methods to work for the first time in setting up
a long transaction.

In this lesson, we wish to do the same for a short sale.

There are fundamental cyclic differences between price tops and price bot-
toms which require that short transactions be dealt with in a somewhat dif-
ferent manner than long transactions. Yet, as we shall see, the basic proced-
ures of setting up a short transaction are not too different from those already
learned.

The specific steps taken to analyze any transaction are so important that an
organized review is warranted before proceeding to a specific discussion of
selling short.

Cyclic analysis of any transaction involves fourteen basic procedures. In
chronological order, these are as follows:

First is the use of VRS or RS analysis to determine whether immediate inter-
est is in order.

Ifa decision is then made to continue the study of a specific transaction,
an FPA will be required. This results in a phased set of charts from which a
current cyclic model is constructed.

From the phased charts and the current cyclic model, information regarding
transaction risk and nearness of possible action is obtained.

The current cyclic model makes it possible to construct VTLs, lay out current
and future time FLD’s, interpret the FLD patterns, and set the price ob-
jective.

Following this, the trading cycle is determined, the action signal is selected,
and a projection is made of the trend underlying the trading cycle.

The selection of the trading cycle clears the way for determining the stop-
loss level, which then permits a study of PR ratios and gross percentage po-
tentials versus transaction entry prices.

Page 78 ——

 


 

7 fEC )
cassette script

LESSON

SECTION ONE REVIEW continued

The final two steps in analyzing a transaction consists of setting the maxi-
mum buy price (or minimum short-sell price), and of a final reevaluation of

transaction risk.

 

poaesay susni tv

‘Uy "saad S4APEAL C161 @ IYBUKdOLy

‘These fourteen steps are involved in one form or anothet in all transactions
based on cyclic principles. Because of their importance, they can form the
basis for a transaction evaluation checklist, a summary form of which will be
found in the accompanying text.

Before we leave the subject, let's go through a quick review of those proced-
ures which were presented for the first time in the last lesson.

A VRS analysis requires about five minutes, consists of several two-finger

FLD passes followed by interpretation of the results, and is primarily used to

isolate the equities of most immediate interest from all others in a stable. »
¢

RS analysis takes about twenty minutes, is based on a partial phasing anal-
ysis, and on roughly sketched in VTL’s and FLD’s.

A complete cyclic analysis, or CCA, consists of several well-defined steps.

First, a weekly initial cyclic model is prepared. From this, a weekly FPA fol-
lows which then results in a weekly current cyclic model.

‘The same three processes are repeated on a daily basis, after which a com-
posite current cyclic model is formed.

The final steps of a CCA consist of locating the estimated time positions of
future nests-of-lows, drawing in current and future time FLD’s, and inter-
preting the FLD patterns.

The concept of transaction risk (as opposed to risk potential) was also intro-
duced in lesson four. Unlike risk potential, transaction risk cannot be fully
quantified. Where risk potential is an estimate of the amount of capital that
may be lost in a transaction, transaction risk is a measure of the probabil-
ity, or likelihood, of taking that loss.

 

‘A method of extracting useful trend information from a study of past-time
FLD price projections was discussed. This technique is based on the fact that
single FLD price projections tend to be undershot, met, or overshot by prices

 

Page 79


AU Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

cassette script —

 

©

SECTION ONE REVIEW continued

depending on whether the trend underlying the wave for which the projection
is made respectively opposes, is neutral to, or supports the projection.

The concept of an action signal was considerably broadened in the last lesson.
An action signal, which can be used for a wide variety of purposes, consists of
any future price action that answers a wave behavior question, The most
commonly used action signals involve price interaction with VTL’s, FLD’s,
and horizontal price lines.

The trading cycle for a given transaction is the longest wave of the composite
wave depended upon to carry prices from the transaction entry to the ob-
jective. In some instances, analysis circumstances permit use of a shorter wave
for the trading cycle so long as the implications remain the same. The trading
eycle determines the stop-loss level, and the wave chosen for the trading cycle
should not be changed during a transaction.

‘The importance of understanding and making full use of the complete set of
methods for analyzing a transaction cannot be over-emphasized. The steps

involved are summarized for ready study and reference in the accompanying
text. Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section one.

SECTION TWO WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS

In the last section we reviewed the elements that are common to setting up a
transaction, whether long or short. Now we are concerned with the differences
that exist between long and short situations.

In lesson one a short transaction was defined as the selling of borrowed stock
in the expectation that prices will subsequently go down. If prices do go down,
a profit is then made by replacing the borrowed stock by purchase on the open
market at the reduced (later) price.

The mechanics of how this is accomplished are well explained in other liter-
ature and we do not need to be concerned with them in this course. What is
of concern, however, is the manner in which cyclic concepts can be used to
provide very much the same assistance in short-selling transactions that they
do in long transactions.

Ina long transaction the desire is to buy at or near a nest-of-lows and to sell at.
or near a price objective that is higher. In selling short the intent is to sell at

 

fc

Page 50—


 

LESSON

Page 81

cassette script

 

SECTION TWO WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS continued

or near a price high and then buy, or cover the short sale, at or near a price
objective that is lower.

Thus far in the course you have learned enough about cyclicality to know that
price tops have different characteristics than price bottoms, but this point
has not yet been emphasized. This fundamental cyclic difference between
price tops and price bottoms has a dramatic impact on cyclic analysis and
transaction decision making in the case of selling short.

First of all, let’s gather together the bits and pieces of cyclic knowledge
already in hand relating to price tops.

For example, you may recall from lesson one a figure that demonstrated why
nests-of-lows tend to form and why similar nests-of-highs are precluded from
forming. This is due to the combined effect of the Principles of Harmonicity
and Synchronicity.

‘The practical result is a characteristicly rounded shape for price tops as op-
posed to a sharp, or peaked, shape at price bottoms.

Visualize what happens as a price bottom is approached. Prices are droppings
toward a nest-of-lows, which is defined as a simultaneously occurring set of
wave troughs. As the nest-of-lows is approached, a point is reached where
each wave associated with the nest-of-lows is acting downward, forcing prices
todrop steeply. As the nest-of-lows is reached, each wave reverses at the same
time, and all of the waves that were formerly causing prices to drop are now
acting together to cause prices to rise. The net effect is a sharp and sudden
price reversal.

Ata price top, no such combined action of waves comes about. At price tops,
each rally and retreat of prices is caused by just a single wave except as sup-
ported or opposed by the trend underlying that wave. The result is a strong
tendency for prices to form a generally rounded top interrupted by oscillations
caused by individual waves.

‘These effects by themselves are enough to cause large differences between
price tops and bottoms. However, the situation is complicated still more
by the time-translation effect.

As price bottoms are traversed the trends underlying most of the waves in the

 

Peasasou smuBR IY

“Duy ‘ssag S19PE4L EL6T @ INBLIKdO


{All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc.

LESSON

 

SECTION TWO WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS continued

associated nest-of-lows also reverse direction. Thus, low translation is seldom
significant except in the case of very strong trend underlying the longest wave
involved in the price reversal.

Just the contrary is true at price tops. Individual waves are cresting one after
the other all throughout a price top so that various trends are constantly in
the process of altering direction. As a result, the price high associated with the
crest of one wave may be time-translated to the left while that of another may
be time-translated to the right. The particular wave whose crest will ultimate-
ly be associated with the highest price high in a price top is therefore depend-
ent on the strength of the various trends involved. As a consequence, price
tops can take on a wide variety of overall shapes while still retaining the gen-
erally rounded characteristic due to Harmonicity and Synchronicity,

Trend underlying any given wave could be perfectly forecast if price move-
ments consisted of the sum of ideal waves. Since this is not the case, our ability
to estimate future trend is quite rough. Such estimates still have very great
utility, but the present state-of-the-art of cyclic analysis does not permit trend
estimation with sufficient accuracy to handle price top situations with the
same degree of accuracy as price bottoms. This does not mean that selling
short necessitates greater transaction risk, but it does mean that shorts must
be dealt with differently in order to assure equivalent risk. The methods of
doing this will be described in a section to follow.

Now that the basic wave implications of price top formation are in hand,
our understanding of topping action can be further developed.

Since bottoms take place quickly while tops take place slowly, prices are in
the process of forming some kind of a top almost constantly. Suppose, for ex-
ample, that the trading cycle for a given transaction is the 10-week wave.
Then, between two consecutive troughs of this wave a single broad top is to be
expected. However, this top is not smooth and in fact consists of a succession
of tops caused by a composite wave the longest wave of which is shorter than
the trading cycle. Each of these tops, in turn, consists of a succession of still
smaller and shorter tops, etcetera.

Itis apparent that the complexity of a price top grows less as the longest wave
associated with it grows shorter. This is true because of the reduced number
of waves available to produce oscillations and translations throughout top

 


 

Page 83.

 

Hggette script 3 :

  

soyoperes

 

 

SECTION TWO WHAT AFFECTS MARKET TOPS continued

formation. This is an important outgrowth of our understanding of how tops
form, and will be used to good advantage in the strategy for setting up short
transactions.

Also, it should be clearly understood that the general conclusions drawn
about price tops versus price bottoms are, as for all cyclic considerations,
based on strong tendencies rather than on certainties. Cases can be found
where price bottoms are slow and rounded, or where price tops are sharp and
peaked. These are exceptions, however, and the cause of them will be discus-
sed later.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section two.

SECTION THREE EDGE-BAND AND MID-BAND SHORT SALES

We have seen how price bottoms and tops are basically different, and how
this difference increases the transaction risk of any attempt to sell short at or
near a top. This corresponds to an edge-band short transaction as defined in
lesson four. The remedy is to sell short at mid-band only, but this is easier said
than done as we shall see.

Let us assume that prices have formed a top and are moving down toward a
nest-of-lows. Further, a CCA has been performed and the FLD pattern anal-
ysis has provided a downward price objective with an acceptable gross poten-
tial percentage relative to the present price.

In this example let us further assume that the trading eycle has been identi-
fied. The transaction would seem to be ready for determination of a stop-
loss level and an action signal.

In the case of long transactions, synchronicity of wave troughs and the fact
that low translation is relatively small made it easy for us to set up an action
signal based on a short wave that would give us satisfactory evidence that the
trading cycle’s price low had indeed occurred. However, for short transac-
tions things are not quite so simple. We do not have synchronicity of wave
crests, and high translation can be considerable because the price high assoc-
iated with the crest of any given wave can jump from one crest to the other

of the next shorter wave as a result of only moderate underlying trend. And
unfortunately we do not have the ability to estimate future trend accurately.

 

poasasoy Siu ITV
“buy ‘ssang S4ap¥A E161 © HBLKKO


All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc,

ry

 

SECTION THREE EDGE-BAND AND MID-BAND SHORT SALES
continued

This means that we are unable to trace the trading cycle’s price high down to
the crest of a short wave.

Therefore, for our short transaction, we must go to considerably longer waves
in order to obtain solid evidence that the price high associated with the trad-
ing cycle crest has indeed passed. Either a valid uptrend line based on the
wave just shorter than the trading cycle or the FLD based on the trading cycle
wave must be broken in the downward direction.

While one or both of these events are necessary to establish a high likelihood
of prices being at or near the mid-band position, neither is suitable as an ac-
tion signal. The reason for this is that, just about the time such a break oc-
curs, prices are ready to rally from a minor nest-of-lows. They must then pro-
ceed to form a minor top — and the ideal time to short is just when this top is
completed. But, it was to avoid the transaction risk incurred at tops that the
decision was made to short only at mid-band!

Nevertheless, the situation has been improved because now we are dealing
with a minor top. As pointed out in the last section, the shorter the longest
wave in the composite wave responsible for forming a top, the less complex
that top becomes. And in the next section it will be shown that the longest
wave in the minor top at mid-channel is at least two waves removed from the
trading cycle.

Finally, the same VTL-FLD technique can be reapplied to the minor top at
mid-channel to further reduce transaction risk if so desired.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section three.
SECTION FOUR THE MID-CHANNEL PAUSE

In section three it was stated that the wave twice removed from a trading cycle
is likely to produce price rallies at mid band. These price rallies are such a
common occurrence that they are given a special name, the mid-channel
pause.

The mid-channel pause is not restricted to downward price movements but,
tends to take place on upward movements as well. Let’s see how and why it
comes about.

Assume a nominal, ideal cyclic model. The Principles of Harmonicity and

Page $4—

 


 

TTITITITT CYELITES

 

       

o

"baosoren ced

 

SECTION FOUR THE MID-CHANNEL PAUSE continued

‘Synchronicity insure that a given wave, the twenty-week wave for example, will
have troughs simultaneously with the ten-week, five-week and all shorter
waves.

paaasay SIS ITV

-auy S504 S19PE] L61 O MBIUKdOD,

Now focus your attention on the sequence of events that follows such a nest-
of-lows. When the twenty-week wave is forming a crest, the ten-week wave is
at its trough. Thus, midway between twenty-week troughs is a composite-wave
dip caused by a ten-week wave trough. Therefore, as the composite wave
climbs from a twenty-week trough, it is headed toward a high associated with
the ten-week wave crest.

 

At the same time, the five-week wave has gone through an entire cycle and is

back toa trough. This five-week trough occurs just one-fourth of the way

along between twenty-week troughs and midway between composite wave

Iows and highs. In this example, when prices have moved up from the twenty-

week trough to the composite wave high associated with the first five-week ( )
crest, it is the decline into the first five-week trough that forms the mid-chan-

nel pause.

 

Exactly the same line of reasoning can be used to establish the existence of a
mid-channel pause on the downside. Further, there are similar pauses for

all trading cycle selections where the period ratio of successive shorter waves is
one-half, And we have just seen that, as previously stated, the mid-channel
pause is caused by the shorter wave just twice removed from the trading cycle.

The situation just described was for ideal and perfectly Harmonious and Syn-
chronous waves, but as we know, the waves in price movement vary consider-
ably from ideal. Nevertheless, the mid-channel pause is a very familiar
occurrence in price movement and is the basis for the rule never to short on the
action signal that indicates a trading cycle is past its crest.

‘You should turn your tape unit off now and return to the text at section four.
SECTION FIVESPECIAL IDIOSYNCRACIES OF SHORT SALES

Short-selling is an ingenious method for making money while prices are fal-
ling, But a price is paid for this convenience in the form of several drawbacks
that do not apply to long transactions.

In general, a short transaction is more convenient in the case of commodities

Page BS


 

1973 Traders Press, Ine.
‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright

 
 
 
 

CYCLITEC
SERVICES |
TRAINING | || 7
COURSE® L| |||

SECTION FIVE SPECIAL IDIOSYNCRACIES OF SHORT SALES

‘continued
than in the case of stocks. This is true because of several peculiarities of short
sales in the stock markets.

A stock cannot be sold-short until a 1/8th-dollar up-tick in price has taken
place. The practical result of this requirement is that transaction entry can
sometimes be obstructed. For example, prices may generate an action signal
by downside movement, then continue down well past the minimum price

at which you are willing to sell before an up-tick occurs. In circumstances
such as this a perfectly valid transaction opportunity may be lost completely.

You should also know that while you hold a short position in a stock you will
be required to pay (to the stockholder from whom you have borrowed the
stock) any dividends that the issuing company may declare.

Further, a transaction analysis may be prepared and an action signal noted,
but all to no purpose when it is found that no stock is available to be borrowed
and therefore the transaction cannot be completed.

These particular peculiarities do not affect a commodity short transaction,
but there are others that affect both stock and commodity shorts.

For example, to achieve a given percentage price change, prices must move
down more price units for a short transaction than they must move up for a
Jong transaction.

Also, it is theoretically possible in a short transaction to lose more capital than
is invested, but this is not possible in a non-margined long transaction.

These special short-transaction considerations must be kept in mind and ac-
counted for in planning but do not seriously affect the desirability of

short transactions. There are market conditions in which the investor would
be relegated to total inactivity if the short-sale process were not available.

Please return to the text now at section five for a study summary of these
special short-sale considerations.

SECTION SIXHOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES
For some time we have been holding off on any discussion of fundamental
factors in relation to price movement. But your background in cyclic theory

has by now been developed to the point where such a discussion can be under-
taken.

 

Page 86


—— Page 87

t

it

TTT

i
i

 

babserte script

 

SECTION SIX HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES
continue

The fact that the subject of fundamentals is brought up in a lesson that is
otherwise devoted to short selling is purely coincidental, for the impact of
fundamentals on price movement is equally important to both long and short
transactions, This should be kept in mind during the following discussion.

In lesson number one it was learned that all price action of all equities consists
of the sum of a number of non-ideal price waves.

This is truly an incredible concept when you stop to think about it. It certainly
runs completely counter to widely held academic opinion that the price move-
ment of negotiable equities is almost totally random in nature — meaning
that there is no discernible order to price variation. Even at this early stage in
your studies of cyclic concepts, you have had enough experience to know that,
‘a very great deal of order exists in price movement.

Further, the Principles of Commonality, Cyclicality, and Summation appear,
at first glance, to make no allowance for the possible effects of such funda-
mental considerations as crop failures, dividend records, earning potentials,
etcetera.

Itis indeed true that the basic cyclic concepts do not admit the existence of
more than a negligible amount of random, or non-ordered price movement.
Itis further true that, to the extent of the knowledge currently available about
cyclic processes, price movement does consist virtually entirely of the sum of
the kinds of waves studied in previous lessons. However, it does not follow
from cyclic theory that fundamental factors do not affect price movement,

for they do.

You may well wonder how prices can consist entirely of the sum of waves
and still be affected by fundamental factors. The Principle of Variation pro-
vides the answer to this question.

As you will recall from lesson number one, one of the several manifestations
of the Principle of Variation takes the form of a smooth change in the ampli-
tude of a price wave as time passes. There is considerable evidence to support
the theory that a good part, if not all, of this amplitude change with time is
due to fundamental considerations.

However, because the amplitude change takes place so slowly, this type of
fundamental interaction with price waves has little to do with practical trading

 

rystssdory

possay WRT

ou ‘ssa4d S1OPBAI, €L61 O


‘All Rights Reserved

Copyright © 1973 Traders Press Ine

CYCLITEC [JJ] TT

SERVICES | | doses uu tu
TRAINING ||| 48 ott
coursed |1)1/)////)///I/

 

 

SECTION SIX HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES

continued

situations beyond increasing the complexity of cyclic analysis. Of far
greater importance are certain other fundamental reactions with cyclicality

which are readily provable and directly influence transaction decisions based
on cyclic concepts.

There are three categories of such interactions that the cyclic analyst must
be aware of:

One: Something very drastic may happen to the company that has issued
astock. The disclosures related to Equity Funding in the early
months of 1973 are an excellent example of this type of event.

In cases like this, trading on the exchanges is often halted in the
issue. If trading is resumed at a later date, the issue may be found of-
fered at a drastically changed price that seems to have little or no
connection with cyclic concepts. However, after trading is resumed,
it will be found that cyclic price movements are continued just as if

the large price transient directly caused by a fundamental considera-
tion did not exist.

This kind of occurrence can be unfortunate as far as the individual
or organization caught by it is concerned. Some measure of protec-
tion against this kind of happening, which fortunately is the excep-
tion rather than the rule, is provided by strict observance of stop-
loss levels. However, in a sufficiently traumatic case that results in
panic selling, even stop-loss levels will not protect against large loss-
es. And of course if trading is stopped, stop-loss orders cannot be

executed until trading is resumed at what may be a drastically lower
price.

There are only two investment strategies that really protect against
such events. One of these is to select issues of companies that are un-
likely to be involved in drastic and scandalous events. The other is
more certain and invokes the protection of diversification, the plac-
ing of investment ot trading funds in several equities instead of a
single one. These subjects will be discussed in more detail in later
lessons.

Two: The second type of fundamental interaction with price waves is

— —— Page 8


 

Page. 89.

 

CYCLITEC
SEAVICES
TRAINING
COURSEO

SECTION SIX HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES
Continue
exemplified by the behavior of the Dow Jones Industrial Average just

following President Nixon's announcement of Phase One economic
controls in 1972.

‘The immediate result was an unprecedented 17-point gap to the up-
side in this widely followed index.

What happened from a price wave viewpoint was that several rather
short waves, of the order of 10 to 20 days in period, increased rapidly
and temporarily in amplitude, then subsided equally as quickly.

 

Interestingly, if you were to conduct a cyclic analysis on this average
for that time, you would find that this dramatic fundamental effect
occurred exactly when a significant nest-of-lows was to be expected.

Noone knows, as yet, why this should be, but time after time you will
find that fundamental events appear to occur just at the right time
to seem to initiate a price move that is already to be expected from
cyclic considerations.

In any case, this is a typical example of fundamental interaction
with price waves. For a short time interval, several adjacent price
waves develop greatly increased amplitude, after which the effect
damps out and normal wave amplitudes are restored.

Three: The third way in which fundamental events interact with price waves
is more long lived.

This one has to do with an increase or decrease in investor interest
in an equity.

Ifa fundamental event causes increased interest so that volume ex-
pands, it will be found that all price waves in the cyclic model start
to increase in amplitude — proportionally. That is, by the time the
10-day wave has doubled in amplitude, so has the 20-day, 40-day
and so on. The result of this, when the component waves are sum-
med, is very much like trend. In fact the term pseudo trend is com-
monly used to describe the effect on prices. Cyclic behaviour persists,
throughout; nests-of-lows are formed, time-translation effects oc-
cur, and the basic cyclic principles continue to apply — but price

 

 

ver eg, PMP SREY
‘Uy "594d S39PEL] E161 @ IYBIsKdoD,


Copyright © 1973 Traders Press, Inc,

All| Rights Reserved

LESSON

 

SECTION SIX HOW FUNDAMENTALS AFFECT PRICE WAVES
continued
movement proceeds as if a very steep trend had developed. Most
commonly, this pseudo trend is in the upward direction.

As volume interest subsides, the amplitudes of all price waves grad-
ually diminish to former values, resulting this time in a pseudo
trend in the opposite direction.

Several conclusions can be drawn from these observations.

First of all, itis now apparent why it can be said on the one hand that price
movement consists entirely of the sum of waves — and on the other hand that
fundamental events can and do affect price movement. Both statements are
true because the entire effect of fundamental events appears as a change in
amplitude of wave components!

Secondly, it is clear that transaction timing implications based on cyclic con-
cepts are nor altered by fundamental events. For timing to be changed, either
wave period or phase or both would have to change in response to fundamen-
tals, The facts are that neither wave periods nor phases are so altered; wave
amplitude is the only cyclic variable influenced.

The practical effect is to free the cyclic analyst from concern as to the impact
of a wide following of traders and investors using cyclic methods. Such a wide
following, even if concentrated in only a few equities would only constitute a
change in volume interest for those equities. The result of this would be alter-
ed wave amplitudes resulting in pseudo trends — but no change in the times
when price movement undergoes reversal. Furthermore, any pseudo trend
developed in this manner is in the correct direction for a cyclic trade, and
could not help but benefit the traders involved.

  

These considerations account for the apparent lack of relationship between
price histories and national or world historical events. What is seen in price
histories is simply a record of wave activity, occasionally amplified by funda-
mental interactions.

With a little practice you will learn to identify the visual effects in price move-
ment of fundamental events through the pseudo trend effect. Any time strong
trend exists without apparent cyclic reason, wave amplitudes are undergoing
rapid change in response to fundamental considerations. Pseudo trend can be

SECTION SEVEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE
In the lesson number four workshop each individual step of the analysis was
described in great detail since it was the first complete analysis performed.

In this and following workshops, progressively less detail will be presented on
individual analysis steps so that the number of analysis situations can be in-
creased. However, in each case, correct results will be presented — and you
should first complete your own analysis, then check the results against the
correct ones shown until you understand how they were derived.

‘This brings to mind an important point. For any given situation, you should
first assume that no prior knowledge is available on the equity under consid-
eration. Thus, the analysis proceeds wholly on the basis of information con-
tained in the Cyclitec weekly and daily charts that are most up to date.

This admonition is given because there is a natural tendency on the part of a
cyclic analysis newcomer to check results against a large amount of past data.
Unfortunately, the Principle of Variation can cause an increase in confusion
over long spans of time. For this reason the Cyclitec charts present a care~
fully chosen amount of data which is sufficient for analysis purposes, yet not
so large as to cause confusion.

Remember that the only purpose of cyclic analysis is to align in your favor
the probabilities of being right on a transaction decision. A conscientious cyc-
lic analysis will do this as well as it can be done on just the amount of data
presented on the Cyclitee charts.

However, operating in this manner can result in successive analyses of a given
‘equity that are slightly different from each other. This is normal, and should
not be cause for alarm. Simply complete each analysis in a workmanlike man-
ner and make transaction decisions based on the results of each as if no pre-
ceding analysis exists.

SECTION SEVEN WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE continued

The first unit of this workshop consists of a scan of Loew's Corporation stock
as of 29 December 1972. Use VRS and RS analysis methods to answer the

SECTION EIGHT WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO

In the discussion of workshop unit number one you were introduced to the
concept of using trend analysis during rapid-scan processes. Further, a short-
ened version of such trend analysis was presented that does not require the
existence of current cyclic model.

This is a very powerful concept and you will be applying it regularly from now
on. However, do keep in mind that during rapid-scan processes the only intent
is to determine whether or not a situation warrants further analysis effort. Do
not make the error of forming actual transaction decisions based on such
primitive estimates as those arrived at during VRS or RS.

  

You should also note from unit one how a nearly complete cyclic status esti-
mate was derived with an absolute minimum of wave trough location effort.
Itis the concept of comparing past FLD price projections with actual price

moves that makes this possible — and the potency of this technique should
never be overlooked.

You have now made the decision that LTR, as of 29 December 1972, is deserv-
ing of a CCA. Please turn your tape unit off now, and return to the text at
section eight for workshop unit number two.

SECTION NINE WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER THREE

In workshop unit two, a CCA confirmed that LTR is a short-sale candidate.
An expected downward move to the objective price of 30 will be driven by
sigma el, the 18-month wave, and the second cycle of the 9-month wave.

Although the 9-month wave becomes the actual trading cycle for the potential
transaction, it is important to note that the downside move is predicated on
the assumption that the 18-month price high has already occurred. In fact the
price rally of the last several weeks must be considered to be the mid-channel
Pause on the downside for the 18-month wave,

‘The principal requirement for a short transaction has, therefore, been met in
