SECTION TEN CONCLUSION

You have now been exposed to the process of setting up a transaction using
cyclic concepts — both on the long and the short side of price movements.
Furthermore, most of the cyclic concepts and tools that you need have been
touched upon at least once.

However, there is much more to trading and investing than just pre-transaction
analysis, and you have still to acquire the needed experience in handling a
wide variety of situations. For these reasons, it is well to repeat at this time the
warning that you should not yet attempt actual transactions in the market.

 

Nevertheless, you have reached a rather erucial point in your self-training
program. In order to handle the material in the following lessons with a mini
mum of frustration, itis essential that the basic concepts of the first five les-
sons be at your fingertips for ready use.

For this reason, no additional workshop units are provided in this lesson. In-
stead, an expanded summary and self-examination are included. You should
expend considerable time on these, reviewing the material in past lessons as
required to assure mastery and understanding.

Please turn your tape unit off now and return to the text summary for study
and review.

REVIEW
© This is the half-way point in the Course. It is
the material and concepts in proper perspective.

important at this time to have

  

@ So far you have:

1. Verified the existence and characteristics of price waves for yourself
2. Learned how to search for transaction opportunities

3. Been exposed to the methods and techniques used to evaluate trans-
action nearness and risk

4. Learned how to set up and evaluate both long and short transactions,
in advance of price moves.

© You should also have a good feel by now for what cyclicality can and cannot
do for you. It does not provide a positive assurance of profit on each trans-
action, but it does provide a strong indication as to the most likely future
course of price movements.

© The remainder of the course will provide needed additional experience
in cyclic analysis; will show you how to behave during a transaction; will
describe the best ways to apply cyclic concepts in portfolio management;
and will cover numerous techniques and bits of knowledge that are useful,
but do not fall easily into any broad category.

 

© In lesson five, price tops and short transactions were studied. You learned
that price tops are basically different from price bottoms, and you learned
how the combined effects of the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchro-
nicity account for this.

© The phenomena of the mid-channel pause was discussed. This is a price
pause or double reversal near the center of the price move based on a given
trading cycle. The mid-channel pause is caused by the shorter wave twice
removed ftom the wave on which the trading cycle is based. The mid-chan-
nel pause does not occur in the rare case in which the trading cycle and the
two next shorter waves have periods that are not related by a ratio of 2 (for
example, the 9-year, 54-month, and 18-month waves).

 

© Price tops are continuously forming. A price top is formed at a mid-channel
pause, for example, which is part of a still longer top, etc. For meaningful

 

Page I.

 


Oo

_ section one

REVIEW continued
discussion and interpretation, the price top must be associated with the

longest wave involved in its formation,

poasasay siuna LY
“Duy ‘ss04q S19PEAL E161 @ WBUKAOD

@ Transaction risk is generally unacceptably high for edge-band short trans-
actions. This is because of the inability of the cyclic analyst to predict time-
translation effects accurately across tops. For this reason, short transactions
are best entered at mid-band for a given trading cycle.

© Short selling is an effective and useful process, but it is characterized by
several idiosyneracies that must be taken into account. These include:
1. A1/8th-dollar price up-tick is required for stocks before a short can
be effected.
2. The short seller of a stock must pay any dividends declared during the _
transaction. 4

3. Stock is not always available to be borrowed at the time a short is.
desired.

4, To generate a given percentage profit, prices must move further for a
downside move than for an upside move.

5. More than the originally invested capital can be lost on a non-mar-
gined short transaction, whereas this is not possible on a non-mar-
gined long transaction.

@ In general, commodities are easier to short than stocks since fewer restric-
tions apply.

@ Fundamental events and situations can and do affect equity prices even
though all price movements consist of the sum of the same number and
kinds of price waves. This is possible because the total impact of a funda-
mental event is absorbed in the form of price wave amplitude changes. Peri-
ods and phase are unaffected and consequently transaction timing criteria
remain unaltered.

© The impact of fundamental events on price movement can be classified in
three categories:

1. A dramatic, usually (but not necessarily) unpleasant, change in

uc


LESSON

section one |
©

REVIEW continued

fundamentals can sometimes resul

 

cessation of trading.

2. An unexpected news release can cause a temporary, but sometimes
large, increase in the amplitude of several rather short waves.

3. Achange in volume interest can cause long term increases in the am-
plitudes of longer waves. The effect generally takes the form of a
pseudo trend. Pseudo trend has many of the characteristics of normal
trend, but cannot readily be predicted.

section two,

On

MAKING THE MOST OF COMMONALITY

© All of cyclic analysis is based upon the eight basic cyclic principles
defined in lesson one. So far, the Principles of Nominality, Harmonicity,
and Synchronicity have been applied directly in the processes of cyclic a-
nalysis, but the most fundamental Principle of all, that of Commonality,
has been largely ignored.

© The Principle of Commonality assures us that identical specific and fore-
castable wave processes occur in all negotiable equities of all types on all
markets of the world. So all-pervasive is this Principle that it is only the
Principle of Variation that prevents the shape of price histories of all equi-
ties from being nearly identical. And, as we have seen, itis the interaction
of fundamental events and situations with cyclicality, causing wave ampli-
tude change, that is responsible for the Principle of Variation.

© The Principle of Commonality can be used directly in cyclic analysis in spite
of the effects of the Principle of Variation. This is done by preparing a
commonality phasing model.

© A commonality phasing model is, in effect, a large measuring strip used to
preserve wave phase and period information from the analysis of two or
more equities. Only the most certain of the wave trough locations are used
from any given analysis. As results are added from analysis of more and
more equities, gaps are filled in and a commonality distribution range is
established for each wave trough position in time.

© A commonality phasing model can be maintained continuously, thus re-
cording the most definitive evidence of wave phase and period from all
analyses conducted. Results can be used from VRS, RS, or CCA analyses,
Dates are recorded on this inter-equity measuring strip so that direct com-
parison with price movement is possible as each new analysis is started,

  

@ Let us assume that it is the weekend following Friday, 29 December, 1972.
The Cyclitec chart mailing (for the stock service) would consist of updated
charts for 9 issues (1/3d of the 27 issues in the Cyclitec stock stable) and the
DJIA (Dow Jones Industrial Average).

© On the date assumed, these nine issues were: CDA, CQ, FCI, FLE, FT,
GLM, HEC, ITK and LTR. For the sake of example, let us suppose that
you have analyzed the weekly charts of seven of the issues, all except FCI

@ Let us see what a commonality phasing model based on seven of the issues
can do for us with regard to the remaining two issues (chosen for analysis,
last because of apparent ambiguities needing resolution).

@ As an exercise, use the techniques you have learned so far to locate the most
certain wave trough positions of the 80-week, 40-week, and 20-week waves
for CDA, CQ, FT, GLM, HEC, ITK and LTR. Then transfer the phasing
diamond locations to the appropriate positions on the blank grid of figure
6-8 to form a commonality phasing model.

 

© You may find, after the first pass through the seven issues, that your com- ¢
monality phasing model has some gaps where there should be troughs but
where no marks were transferred from any of the charts. If this is the case,
relax your requirements for “the most certain” trough locations a little
and go back through the seven charts, extending the phasing analysis for
each until you have at least two or three marks on the commonality phasing
model for each trough location.

© The commonality phasing model is, in effect, an error averaging device.
‘And while you would like for all the points on it to come from trough lo-
cations that you are fairly confident of, you do need some points at every
trough location in order to have samples to average. So don’t be 100 critical
of your phasing in those areas where you need samples for the commonality
phasing model.

@ Asa guide to the format for your own model, refer to the completed com-
monality phasing model shown in figure 6-9; but do not use this figure as.
an aid in completing the exercise for yourself. When you have completed
the exercise, go through the following discussion and compare your re-
sults to those shown.

© You should have located relatively unambiguous wave troughs at some-
thing like the grid line locations shown in the table on figure 6-9. The re-
sulting commonality phasing model is also shown in this figure as the

clusters of marks along the horizontal grid lines labeled at the right with the

© Aswe have said before, different analysts will produce different analyses
of any given chart, but the differences should not be so great as to result in
different conclusions. Similarly in the present instance, while your individ-
ual chart analyses will probably not match the table exactly, your com-

© When you are making commonality phasing models for your own use in the
future, you may want to turn the blank chart paper sideways so that the
Jong dimension is horizontal. Place the phasing marks along the then upper
portion of the paper, starting at the left. This will leave quite a bit of space
at the right to extend the model as time passes.

Also you can cut off the upper margin of the chart paper to make it easier to
align the grid lines of the model with the grid lines of the chart under ex-
amination.

© Figure 6-10 shows the commonality phasing model results superimposed
on the weekly chart of FCI as of 29 December 1972. If you were approach-
ing this issue for the first time, it is clear that a thorough analysis is scarce-
ly needed to inform you of the current wave status. The commonality phas-
ing model has removed the need for much of the detailed analysis on a new-
ly-approached situation!

 

© Also, ambiguous situations are often easily resolved. For example, on this
FCI chart, note the problem of selecting the price low associated with the
18-month nest-of-lows in the vicinity of grid lines 50 to 70. The common-
ality phasing model strongly suggests that the price lows at grid lines 66 and
70 are not associated with this nest.

© This kind of occurrence is not rare at major nests-of-lows. It is the primary

reason why edge-band transactions result in increased transaction risk; and
the longer the period of the longest wave in the nest-of-lows, the greater the

tisk.
@ By using a commonality phasing model, it is often possible to arrive at a
satisfactory current cyclic model without going through a complete formal

phasing analysis. For an example, turn to figure 6-11, which is a weekly
chart of FLE with the commonality phasing model superimposed upon it.

© We can quickly see that the left hand three-quarters of the chart matches
the commonality phasing model very nicely. We put in 20-week phasing
diamonds at grid lines 1 and 145, and we see from the commonality phasing
‘model that there are seven samples of the 20-week period between these two
diamonds. This gives us an average period of 20.6 weeks, which we enter
in the current cyclic model table (shown in figure 6-11).

© We multiply the 20-week average period by two to get 41.2 weeks for the
average period of the 40-week wave, and we multiply this by two again to
get 82.4 weeks for the average period of the 80-week wave. These average
periods are also entered in the current cyclic model table.

© Now for the current phase. Here things are not quite so.easy because of the
poor definition of the latest 40-week wave trough in the commonality phas-
ing model. There are only four points for this trough in the model, and they
are widely dispersed between grid lines 156 and 170.

© On the FLE chart there is a pronounced price low at grid line 173. But this
is outside the present range of the commonality phasing model and would
give rather long period samples, 51 weeks for the 40-week wave and 28
weeks for the 20-week wave.

© If we examine the overall price action shown on the chart, it seems quite
possible that FLE is now in a strong downward pseudo trend similar to the
strong upward pseudo trend that existed between grid lines 84 and 142.
Sudden reversals of this kind are quite common in pseudo trends, which is,
one reason why they must be handled with caution.

© The most we can say until we see additional price movement is that there is
a possible 40-week trough at grid line 173. Accordingly, we place X’s on the
chart at this grid line for 40- and 20-week troughs. But we are not confident
enough of this location to use it as a phase reference.

© The price low at grid line 164 also looks like a reasonable possible for the
40-week trough. It falls at about the center of the range in the commonality
phasing model and would give quite reasonable period samples of 42 weeks

“sup ‘ss0aq 519pB4L EL61 @ IHBLKAOLD

©

MAKING THE MOST OF COMMONALITY continued

for the 40-week wave and 19-weeks for the 20-week wave. We mark it with
X’s, but again we are reluctant to use it as a phase reference point.

© Our wisest course seems to be to go back to the 80-week nest-of-lows at
grid line 122 as a phase reference for the 80- and 40-week waves and to use
the 20-week trough at grid line 145 as the phase reference for the 20-week
wave.

© Accordingly, we place phasing diamonds at grid line 122 for the 80- and 40-
‘week waves. And we record the current phase of 57 weeks along and the
current status of down in the current cyclic model table.

© For the 40- and 20 week waves, we run up against an overdue situation,
with 57 and 34 weeks along, respectively.

© In section eight of lesson five, an overdue condition existed for the 80-day
wave on the LTR daily chart in figure 5-12. That situation was different
from our present one, however, in that no possibles existed for the over-
due trough location. A rule of thumb governing phase estimation in an
overdue situation was given in lesson five, and that rule is repeated here re-
worded to specify the condition that no possible exists for the overdue
trough location:

When a trough is overdue for a given wave and there is no price low in
the vicinity that is strong enough to be considered as a possible location
for the trough, then the wave phase is listed in the current cyclic model as
elapsed time from the last known trough (this will be longer than the
average period) and the wave status is listed as bottoming. This phase
reference and status are continued until the point is reached when the
elapsed time is equal to one and one-half times the average wave period.
At this point a phasing diamond (with an X in it) is arbitrarily placed one
average wave period from the last known trough and is now used as the
new phase reference. The wave status is changed to topping, in keeping
with the new wave phase.

(As more time goes by, the phasing of shorter waves will probably provide
a more suitable location for the arbitrarily placed phasing diamond by
means of the Principles of Harmonicity and Synchronicity. But by this time


 

MAKING THE MOST OF COMMONALITY continued

the location of this trough will probably be of little consequence because it
will no longer have any effect on transaction decisions.)

@ On the FLE chart in figure 6-11 the overdue situation is somewhat differ-
ent. Here we do have two possible locations for the 40-week nest-of-lows.
We ate therefore justified in assuming that the nest has occurred. Our best
information to date (the average wave period and the location of the last
known low) is used to place X-filled phasing diamonds on grid line 163 for
the 40-week trough and (by Synchronicity) the 20-week trough. The X’s are
left at the possible locations on grid lines 164 and 173 as a reminder that
these diamonds at 163 were arbitrarily placed.

© A wave phase of 16 weeks is then entered in the current cyclic model table
for both the 40-week and 20-week waves. The 40-week wave status is listed
as up and the 20-week wave status as down.

© This leaves the status of 3 Las the only item needed to complete the weekly
portion of the current cyclic model. As an exercise, determine the status of
LL for yourself before reading the next paragraph. Bear in mind that any
pseudo trend present is considered a part of EL.

© A two-finger check of the 40-week FLD quickly establishes that the 40-week
downward price objective has been way overshot by any possible 40-week
trough location later than grid line 157. We feel that the trough has occur-
red somewhere between grid lines 163 and 173, which indicates that ¥L is
strongly down.

© Further, we see that although we are only 57 days along in the current 80-
week cycle, already prices ate lower than the last 80-week low.

@ We therefore conclude that 3L is strongly down and enter the status as
down in the current cyclic model table.

© Thus, by judicious use of the commonality phasing model, we have arrived
at a quite satisfactory weekly current cyclic mode! without resorting toa
formal phasing analysis.

phasing model and the DJIA for the same date. This comparison is shown

in figure 6-12. Note that commonality is just as effective for an average of
many issues as itis for an individual issue,

© You should derive several benefits from this exercise on the commonality
phasing model:

1. A feeling for the really incredible persistence and extent of the re-
markable Principle of Commonality

2. A better realization of the fact that cyclic analysis, properly applied,

can place the odds strongly in your favor on a given transaction but
can never guarantee 100% successful results

An understanding that when working with a stable of equities as op-

posed to a single equity, the Principle of Commonality makes possible
¢ a drastic reduction in analysis effort

An understanding that although the most visually evident price lows

are most often associated with nests-of-lows involving longer waves,
this is not always the case.

© The commonality phasing model is a very powerful analysis aid, and its
use will do much to increase your understanding of basic cyclic concepts.

You should study the figures in this section thoroughly to be sure you com-
pletely grasp the concept and applications; then,

GOLDEN FACT AND FICTION

© It is commonly assumed that the price of gold shares goes up as the price
of most other stocks goes down, and vice versa. (By gold shares we mean the
common stock of gold mining companies such as Campbell Red Lake Mine
and Homestake Mining.) This is not true in general, but the behavior of
gold shares is slightly different than other stocks from a price wave view-
point — and this slight difference is sufficient to create the impression of
contrary gold price movement.

© Gold shares exhibit all of the same wave characteristics expected in other
issues. The same nominal model applies. Harmonicity and Synchronicity
behave in the same way. And all cyclic analysis techniques may be used.
The difference lies in the fact that gold shares show more variation in
period for long waves (such as the 18- and S4-month waves) than do other
equities. This sometimes causes shorter waves to be temporarily one-half
period out of phase with the same waves in other equities.

© The increased period variation in gold shares can be used to advantage. If
gold share prices complete a bottom or a top significantly in advance of other
equities, funds can sometimes be put to work when they would otherwise be
idle.

© Homestake Mining is a major gold stock, and you analyzed this issue as of
22 December 1972 in lesson four. As an exercise, compare the commonality
phasing model for 29 December 1972 (figure 6-9) with the phased weekly
chart of HM (figure 4-12). Now directly compare the weekly DJIA chart for
29 December 1972 (figure 6-12) with HM (figure 4-12), (Be sure to make the
proper allowance for the fact that figure 6-9 and 6-12 end on 29 December
1972 while figure 4-12 ends on 22 December 1972.) Note as many similar:
ties and differences as you can for yourself before reading on. What con-
clusion do you reach as far as wave action in gold stock prices is concerned?
Can you see why it is commonly believed that gold stock prices move con-
trary to those of other stocks — and why this belief could get you into trou-
ble if you behaved as if it were so?

© Comparing figures 4-12 and 6-9, we see the following:

1, The 18-month nest-of-lows shown in figure 4-12 at grid line 127 com-
pares reasonably well with the commonality phasing model.

Page 3—_—

 


 

section three

GOLDEN FACT AND FICTION continued
2. The 20-week nest-of-lows shown in figure 4-12 at grid line 148 also
compares well with expectation from the commonality phasing model.

paarosry swe HW
“buy ‘sang S9pHAL EL6T O MBKdOD

3. Also, the far-left 20-week nest-of-lows at grid line 2 checks out very
well.

4. But notice the 40-week wave, The 40-week trough at grid line 32. on
the HM chart falls in between the 40-week troughs at grid lines 2-10
and 43-55 on the commonality phasing model. And the 40-week
trough at 75 on the HM chart falls in between the 40-week troughs at
43-55 and 83-91 on the commonality phasing model.

5. This is traceable to the failure of HM to exhibit an 18-month nest-of-
lows at or near what would be grid lines 43 to 56 for the seven other
issues used in the commonality phasing model. Instead, HM formed
its 18-month nest-of-lows at 32, a full 12 weeks before the earliest of @
the other issues studied.

6. This behavior caused the following 18-month wave period to be
long, and the periods of all shorter waves were longer in proportion.
‘Asa result, there were times during this 18-month cycle when HM
prices were going up while others went down and vice versa. Yet, the
overall wave action and characteristics remained the same. Variation
only was responsible for the differences.

© Comparing figures 4-12 and 6-13:

1. This comparison is far more remarkable for its similarities than for
its differences. After making this comparison, how can anyone state
with conviction that gold stocks move contrary to the market?

2. At the left of each chart, prices are descending into an 18-month nest-
of-lows.

3. Prices on each chart then reverse and rapidly ascend to a price high
associated with the first 18-month wave crest in a 54-month wave
cycle.

4, Next, prices on each chart plunge for about 30 weeks into the next 18-
month nest-of-lows, oscillating meantime with the expected shorter
period waves.

5. The price rise that follows is clearly to a 9-month wave crest in both
cases.

© Despite these similarities, the differences are present too — and to a some-
what larger extent than would be expected if a stock other than gold were
under consideration.

1. On the HM chart, prices were falling dramatically (into a 9-month
nest-of-lows) from grid lines 70 to 75. The DJIA, on the other hand,
rose from 750 to 830 during this same period.

2. The decline of HM prices into a 9-month nest-of-lows at grid line 175
was much more pronounced than the similar action in the DJIA.

© From this exercise it is seen that gold prices actually behave very much like

) other equities from a wave viewpoint, but that increased variation causes
local price change that is, at times, in opposition to commonality expecta-
tions. It must be remembered that non-gold equities exhibit the same be-
havior, but not to the same degree.

 

© Once the true nature of gold-price variation is familiar to him, the eyclie
analyst can turn the apparent vagaries of gold to trading advantage.

THE ROLE OF THE DJIA IN CYCLIC ANALYSIS

© Because of the Principle of Commonality, periods and phases for waves $4
months and longer that are obtained by analysis of the Dow Jones Indus-
trial Average (DJIA) can be used as an aid in individual stock analysis.

© The Cyclitec Stock Service provides a new chart each week for DJIA daily
and weekly data. Once each month, an updated chart based on monthly
data is also supplied.

© The monthly DJIA chart contains 180 months of data, or 15 years. Thus,
this chart is capable of showing up to 3 complete 54-month cycles, and at
least 1 cycle of the 9-year wave.

© Figure 6-13 is a monthly chart of the DJIA as of 31 December 1972. Com-
plete an FPA on this chart just as you normally would for a weekly or a
daily chart. Write down your conclusions as to the overall cyclic state of the
DJIA as of 31 December 1972 before proceeding to the discussion that
follows.

@ The desired FPA is shown in figure 6-14, and results in period estimates
as follows:

1. 9-year wave: 8.38 years
2. S4-month wave: 49.8 months

3. 18-month wave: 16.6 months
4, 9-month wave: 8.3 months
5. 20-week wave: 18.0 weeks.

© Questions: Did you include the estimate for the 9-year wave in your anal-
ysis? How was the number 8.38 years shown above derived? Is there any
other way of estimating the period of this wave from the chart?

© The 8.38 year estimate was derived above by doubling the 49.8-month
estimate (to get 99.6 months), then dividing by 12 (to get 8.38 years), This
wave period could also be estimated by noting that the pre-dominant wave
on this chart (indicated by oscillations of the Dominancy Envelope center-
line) is the nominal 9-year wave. This locates troughs for this wave at grid
lines 53 and 148, The length of this single sample is then 95 months, or 7.9

 

 

LESSON

Page 25


 

section four —

THE ROLE OF THE DJIA IN CYCLIC ANALYSIS continued

years. The estimate of 8.38 years is considered to be more accurate since it,
is based on a little over three cycles of the 49.8-month wave instead of a
single sample of the 9-year wave.

© In fact, all of the wave periods in the preceding tabulation except that of
the 9-month wave were obtained by this same procedure of applying the
Principle of Harmonicity to a neighboring wave. It is time that you began to
use this method yourself, and it is outlined in the following paragraph for
your convenience.

© The best available estimates of average wave periods are obtained as,
follows:

1, From your FPA select the wave that has the greatest time span be-
tween the endmost phasing diamonds and that is also reasonably un-
ambiguous in phasing. (By unambiguous phasing we mean that there
are few, if any, X's or ?’sin the string of diamonds. The ? is a new
symbol introduced in this section to indicate a trough that is known to
be present because of the Principle of Harmonicity but for which no
corresponding price low can be identified.)

 

  

2. Measure the time span between the endmost diamonds and divide
this time span by the number of period samples that it contains. (Be
sure that the sample count includes any samples that may be delimit-
ed by X's or 7's, Also, round off the division to one decimal place.)

3. Use this average period as a base to obtain the average period of all
other waves shown in the FPA.

Multiply successively by two to obtain average periods of longer
waves, and divide successively by two to obtain average periods of
shorter waves. The only time three would be used to multiply or
divide would be on a monthly chart between the 18-month and 54-
month waves (as in the monthly DJIA chart just analyzed).

© Acomment is in order here regarding the rule that two should always be
used as the factor between neighboring waves except for the 18- and S4-
month waves. Itis true that there are times when the price action on a chart
will visually favor a factor of three rather than two. But you will find that

©

THE ROLE OF THE DJIA IN CYCLIC ANALYSIS continued
you always use two, even to the point of placing an X or a ? in the phas-
ing string, you will avoid confusion at current time when projecting antici-
pated nests-of-lows and you will obtain more consistently reliable results,

 

© Asa general note it is seen that over the past 15 years the longer waves aver-
age out to be somewhat shorter than nominal.

@ The wave state of the DJIA as of 31 December 1972 is as follows:

1. Lis up. (Most recent 9-year low is higher than preceding 9-year
low.)

2. The 9-year wave is 30 months (2.5 years) along, hence is strongly up.
3. The $4-month wave is also 30 months along, and is therefore down.
4. The 18-month wave is 12 months along, and is therefore down.

© itis quite apparent that prices are moving at this time into a high associ-
ated with the second 9-month cycle in an 18-month cycle. This 18-month
cycle is the second in a S4-month cycle. And this 54-month cycle is the first
in the 9-year wave that had its last trough in May of 1970. The expectation
would be for prices to form this 9-month high soon, then retreat for some
months to form an 18-month nest-of-lows.

© In actual fact, the DJIA did rise for two more months (to about 1065),
then retreated steadily for the next 6 months (to the vicinity of 865, a drop
200 points).

© As this behavior of the DJIA came to pass, prices of individual equities
simultaneously dropped, many as much as 30 to 70 per cent. Many exciting
shorting opportunities developed, predicted in general by the above analysis,
of the DJIA monthly chart.

section five ,

ON TRACK OR IN TROUBLE AND WHAT TO DO ABOUT IT

© A transaction that has been set up is not normally left unattended to pro-
duce the preset profit or loss. This can, of course, be done — but the degree
of optimization that can be obtained by use of intra-transaction analysis
makes the added effort well worthwhile.

© The frequency with which such intra-transaction analysis is conducted is
largely dependent on the expected transaction turnaround time. For long
trading cycles (of the order of 9 to 18 months or more) analysis on a weekly
or even a monthly basis may be all that is required. The shorter the trading
cycle the more frequently the transaction progress should be assessed.

@ Intra-transaction cyclic analysis employs the same techniques you have
learned for the purpose of setting up a transaction. However, the prime pur-
pose in intra-transaction analysis is to provide a basis for correction of cye-
lic assumptions. This is accomplished by a feedback process. That is, price
move predictions are generated, then compared with actual, subsequent
price action. If the two are similar, then no change in wave status assump-
tions is made. If prices do not behave according to expectations, then wave
status assumptions may be modified accordingly.

@ Intra-transaction analysis can result in several kinds of transaction action:

 

Relocation of the stop-loss level
2. Generation of trailing profit-preservation signals
3. The initiation of special transaction termination procedures.

The first two types of action will be covered in this lesson while the latter
will be the subject of lesson seven.

© When stop-loss levels are changed during the course of a transaction, they
must always be changed in such a way as to further limit possible capital
loss. These adjusted stop-loss signals need not necessarily be a fixed price
level. VTL's and FLD's often serve as the most useful intra-transaction
stop-loss action signals,

@ When the stop-loss action signal is changed during a transaction so as to

tp


LESSON

section five

 

ON TRACK OR IN TROUBLE AND WHAT TO DO ABOUT IT

continued

successively protect more and more paper profits, the resulting action sig-
nals are alternatively called profit-preservation action signals.

© Intra-transaction analysis procedures will be demonstrated in the work-
shops to follow, so no exercise is included in this section.

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE

© The workshops in this lesson are intended to provide you with additional
experience in cyclic analysis; to broaden the variety of situations you are
capable of handling; to provide experience in applying the new concepts of
the lesson: and to introduce the subject of intra-transaction cyclic analysis,

@ You should study the completed examples and associated discussions with
considerable care, striving to identify those areas in which you are weakest.
As such areas are identified, look up and study the appropriate material
in past lessons,

© Do not expect your analyses to duplicate the descriptions in the workshops
identically. However, you should be concerned if your analyses result in
significantly different transaction decisions than those shown. If this oc-
curs, the need for additional study is indicated.

© For this workshop unit, turn to figure 6-15 (a weekly chart of FLE as of 29
December 1972). Use the commonality model for this date (figure 6-9) to
obtain the equivalent of an RS analysis. Do not phase this weekly chart.
Instead, again use the commonality model to arrive at a current weekly
cyclic model and a preliminary transaction evaluation.

Accomplish these exercises before consulting the following discussion.

© This accurate weekly current cyclic model was all developed without FPA,
riding on the results of previous analyses. The power and utility of acom-
‘monality phasing model should be quite evident to you by now! Already, in
a matter of minutes, much of the information normally obtainable only by
a CCA is at hand. Let's see what sort of transaction evaluation information
can now be obtained.

@ A two-finger FLD based on the 41.2-week wave is broken to the downside
at a price of about 41 or 42 (between grid lines 155 and 160).

‘The resulting projection to a price of about 33 to 35 was heavily overshot —
in spite of the fact that the 18-month wave was acting upward at this time. a
This confirms the very strong 3L downtrend.

© A two-finger FLD based on the 82.4-week wave was broken to the down-
side between grid lines 160 and 170 (at a price of about 31). This provides
an 18-month downside projection to the vicinity of 13, once the strength is
gone from the current 9-month upward move.

© A big question: How far up are prices likely to go before the 9-month top is,
seen?

@ To answer this, we note from the current cyclic model that the longest
wave for which current trend is likely to be flat is the 10.3-week wave. (In
the very recent past, when the 20.6-week wave was up, there were two up
arrows and two down arrows indicating the direction of the trend underly-
ing the 10.3-week wave.) Thus, FLD projections based on this wave should
be met, while projections from all longer wave FLD's are likely to be under-
shot, reaching only to the 10.3-week projection. Using a measuring strip, an
FLD based on the 10.3-week wave produces an upward objective of about
33, and prices have already reached this objective. The implication is that
the price high associated with the 5.2-week, 10.3-week, 20.6-week, and
41.2-week wave crests has already been seen (grid line 176). If so, dramatic
high translation to the left is implied—again providing information on the
overriding downward strength of 3L in this issue at this time.

i

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER ONE continued

@ The conclusion: This stock should not be bought. An opportunity to short
is probably close at hand. The probable stop-loss level is 34, the short-sale
price is of the order of 22 to 25, and the objective is 13 (or lower, since the
82.4-week objective is likely to be overshot on the downside). The potential
gain is 40.9% to 48.0%. The potential risk ratio is only .75 at a sell-short
price of 22, and is 1.33 for a sell-short price of 25. Since these PR’s are low,
this issue should be shorted only at higher prices that may occur as a result
of the rally from the 41.2-day nest-of-lows that appears to be at hand.

Selling-short at 26 to 28 would appear reasonable — and the associated
PR's would be 1.5 and 2.3.

© The principal object of this workshop unit has been to demonstrate the
value of a commonality phasing model. Results will not always be as dra-
matic as in this instance, but you will always find this concept to provide
significant savings in time and effort.

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER TWO
@ In this workshop unit, your experience in the use of a commonality phasing
model will be extended to applications using daily charts.

@ Figures 6-16 through 6-22 are the daily charts for the same seven issues
and for the same date used in the weekly commonality phasing model. Use
the blank chart paper of figure 6-23 to form a daily commonality phasing
model from these charts. Use rapid scan and FPA methods to derive the
needed trough locations. Complete the effort for yourself before referring to
figure 6-24 or to the discussion that follows.

@ Figure 6-24 shows the completed daily commonality phasing model. The
table that is included in the figure gives the trough locations for the individ-
ual issues so that you can check your own pre-commonality-model phas-
ings. Do not expect your results to be identical, but you should have arrived
at results that have the same general features.

© Ifyour phasing of FT resulted in 80-day troughs at grid lines 15, 79, and
149 instead of the 15, 95, 168 shown in the table, do not change your phas-
ing yet, but wait for a further discussion of this chart a little later on in this
section.

@ On several of the charts it was not at all clear which 40-day nest-of-lows
also included an 80-day low. In these cases, the best procedure was to trans-
fer the information on the 80-day lows from the weekly charts.

@ The groups of points on the daily commonality phasing model are not near-
ly as tight as those on the weekly commonality phasing model. This does
not mean that the Principle of Commonality does not apply to the shorter
waves as well as the longer ones. It merely means that over this particular
span of time there was a marked amount of variation present in some of the
issues being considered.

@ Refer back to the weekly commonality phasing model of figure 6-9. The
daily charts under discussion cover the last 26 weeks shown on the weekly
charts, from grid line 153 to 179 on figure 6-9. If we notice the wide spread
between points for the last 40-week nest-of-lows on the weekly commonality
Phasing model and see that this spread covers a goodly portion of the time
span presented in the daily charts, we might very well expect these daily
charts to exhibit considerable variation, If we had selected for the daily

charts the time span between grid lines 100 and 126 on the weekly common-

© Now try something very interesting. See if you can separate the seven daily
charts into two sets, each of which produces a separate commonality phas-
ing model having fairly tight groups of points. Have a try at this yourself
before you read the following paragraphs. (Omit FT if you had the 80-day
troughs at grid lines 15, 79, and 149.) The selection process is greatly aided
by identifying the points on your composite commonality phasing model
according to the chart they were taken from. This identification can be
done by means of color coding or with small numbers (with a color or a
number assigned to each chart).

© The set selection that seems to provide the best two commonality phasing
models is shown in figure 6-25. It is readily apparent that these two models
are more useful than the single composite model. This technique is often
very helpful during time periods when many issues are showing consider-
able variation and the composite commonality model is therefore somewhat
confused.

  

© Ifyou originally phased FT with 80-day troughs at grid lines 15, 79, and
149, now compare the FT chart with your new split commonality phasing
model. The 15-79-149 phasing does not fit into either commonality model.
However, if the FT chart is phased as shown in the table on figure 6-24, it
fits quite nicely into the upper commonality model. And this new phasing is
quite acceptable visually if one rationalizing assumption is made; and that
i that the price structure between grid lines 72 and 84 is anomalous, pos-
sibly due to some short term fundamental factor.

© This treatment of FT illustrates a technique that can be very useful in dif-
ficult phasing situations. That is: From several alternative but not entirely
satisfactory phasing solutions, select the one that can be made satisfactory
with the least number and least drastic of rationalizing assumptions. In the
foregoing case of FT, the assumption that the price structure cited was an-
‘omalous was less drastic than the assumption that very wide variation from
commonality was present.

section eight,

WORKSHOP UNIT NUMBER THREE

© In this workshop unit you will set up a transaction for use in the following
workshop unit where you will be introduced to the concept of intra-transac-
tion cyclic analysis. This is analysis performed during a transaetion in order
to minimize transaction risk and maximize transaction profit.

@ Use the transaction analysis form of figure 6-26 and the weekly and daily

charts of FCI for 6 July 1973, figures 6-27 and 6-28, for the following activ-
ities

 

1. Conduct a VRS analysis on both charts and summarize your
conclusions.

2. Conduct an RS analysis on both charts and summarize your
conclusions.

3. Combine the results of the VRS and RS analyses, and summarize the
overall conclusions, giving reasons for each conclusion.

Perform this exercise before reading the following discussion or looking at
figures 6-29 and 6-30.

© Rapid scan methods applied to the weekly and daily charts of FCI for 6 July
1973 result in conclusions as follows:

1, From the weekly chart it is seen that FCI is 84 weeks along on an 18-
month cycle, since past-time FLD’s establish an 18-month nest-of-
lows at grid line 95.

2. 2 Lis strongly up. (There was high translation to the right on the 18-
month wave, and the price high at grid line 160 overshot the 18-month
FLD objective. Also, present prices are at the 9-month FLD objective
ata time when the 9-month low is expected. For the 9-month objective
to be met rather than overshot with the 18-month wave down would

require XL to be up in order to produce a flat trend underlying the 9-
month wave.)

3. FCIis at or near an 18-month nest-of-lows and should not be sold
short. A buying opportunity may be near.

 

 

4. On the rally out of the 18-month low, the 18-month FLD objective
should be met or overshot (>L up). This objective should take prices
